## 3 Mathematical framework for functional $d a t a$

In previous chapters, we have seen examples of functional data and learned
how to perform certain exploratory analyses. In order to perform inference,
e.g. hypothesis tests and estimation with associated measures of uncertainty,
we must treat the observed functions as elements of some space. In elementary
statistics, observations are numbers (scalars), and are elements of the real line.
In multivariate statistics, observations are vectors, and so are elements of the
Euclidean space $\mathbb{R} ^ { d } ,$ where $d$ is the dimension of the observed vectors. Even
though functions are observed at discrete points $t _ { j } ,$ in FDA they are treated
as infinitely dimensional objects. We have seen that the first step in analysis
of functional data is to convert functions observed at discrete time points to
functional objects using basis expansions. For example, if the basis consists
of trigonometric functions, each functional object is a function defined on the
whole interval because each sine and cosine function is defined on the whole
interval. So the question we must address is what kind of space these functional
objects, which we simply call functions, live in. Based on the examples we have
seen, we might require that the functions we work with are continuous and
so are elements of the space of continuous function on the interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ If
we are concerned with the estimation of derivatives, we may require that the
functions are not only continuous, but also have sufficiently many derivatives,
which are also continuous functions themselves. It turns out, that the simplest
setting arises if the functions are treated as elements of a much larger space,
the space of square integrable functions. Our objective in this chapter is to
outline basic ideas related to data living in such a space. Our exposition focuses
on introducing suitable notation and explaining the fundamental concepts so
that the remaining chapters can be understood without searching for further
references. The concepts we discuss in this chapter are revisited in Chapters
10 and 11, which contain a more rigorous exposition and a large number
of problems which help to absorb these ideas more fully. An excellent and
very extensive account of mathematical foundations of FDA is given in the
monograph of Hsing and Eubank (2015).

<!-- PageNumber="37" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="38" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 3.1 Square integrable functions

To simplify the notation, we assume that all functions are defined on the
unit interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ (If the argument $t$ of a function $f$ is in the interval $\left[ a , b \right] ,$
then we simply consider the function $f ^ { \star }$ defined by $f ^ { \star } \left( u \right) = f \left( t \right)$ , where
$\left. u = \left( t \quad - \quad a \right) / \left( b \quad - a \right) . \right)$ A function $f$ is said to be square integrable if

$$\int f ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \left\{ f \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t < \infty .$$

In the following, if the limits of integration are not specified, $i t$ is understood
that the integration is over the interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ We denote the set of all square
integrable functions by $L ^ { 2 } .$

Square integrable functions form a vector space. This means that if $f ,$ $g \in$
$L ^ { 2 } ,$ then $a f + b g \in L ^ { 2 }$ for any scalars $a$ and $b .$ Multiplication by scalars and
addition are defined pointwise, i.e.

$$\left( a f + b g \right) \left( t \right) = a f \left( t \right) + b g \left( t \right) , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$$

Readers aware of measure theoretic foundations of integration, will recall that
the elements of $L ^ { 2 }$ and operations on them do not need to be defined for every
point t, they are defined for almost $a l l$ points t. In this chapter, we will ignore
measure theoretic considerations.

What makes the space $L ^ { 2 }$ so convenient is that we can define the inner
product of two functions by

$$\langle f , g \rangle = \int f \left( t \right) g \left( t \right) d t .$$

This inner product is analogous to the inner (or scalar) product of two vec-
tors. Recall that if $\mathrm { x } = \left[ x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots x _ { d } \right] ^ { T } ,$ $\mathrm { y } = \left[ y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } , \ldots y _ { d } \right] ^ { T } ,$ then $\langle \mathrm { x } , \mathrm { y } \rangle =$
$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } x _ { j } y _ { j } .$ The inner product allows us to entertain the notion of orthogonal-
ity, which makes the geometric structure of the space $L ^ { 2 }$ very similar to that
of the usual finite dimensional Euclidean space, at least at an intuitive level.
We say that functions $f$ and $g$ are orthogonal (or perpendicular) if $\langle f , g \rangle = 0 .$
The inner product also allows us to introduce the notion of distance $b e t w e e n$
functions. This is done by defining the norm

$$\| f \| = \sqrt { \langle f , f \rangle } = \left\{ \int f ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$

The reader will notice that the norm is analogous to the length of a finite
dimensional vector. The distance between two functions in $L ^ { 2 } \text { is then the }$
norm of their difference, i.e. $d \left( f , g \right) = \| f - g \| .$ There is a very useful relation

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="39" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Mathematical framework for functional data" -->

between the inner product of two functions and their norms:

$$| \int f \left( t \right) g \left( t \right) d t | = | \langle f , g \rangle | \leq \| f \| \| g \| = \left\{ \int f ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } \left\{ \int g ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$
(3.1)

Relation (3.1) is known as the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality . Other properties
of the norm and the inner product are fully analogous to the properties of
the length of a vector and of the inner product in a finite dimensional space.
For example, $\| f + g \| \leq \| f \| + \| f \|$ (triangle inequality) and $\langle a f + b g , h \rangle =$
$a \langle f , h \rangle + b \langle g , h \rangle ,$ which is actually a defining property of any inner product. We
will use such relations in the following chapters; they are listed in a systematic
way in Chapter 10.

As we have already seen, basis expansions play an important role in
methodology for functional data. We say that a set of functions $\left\{ e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , e _ { 3 } , \ldots \right\}$
is a basis in $L ^ { 2 }$ if every function $f \in L ^ { 2 }$ admits a unique expansion

$$f \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } a _ { j } e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

We say that $\left\{ e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , e _ { 3 } , \ldots \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis, if, in addition, $\langle e _ { j } , e _ { j ^ { \prime } } \rangle = 0$
whenever $j \neq j ^ { \prime }$ and $\| e _ { j } \| = 1 .$ Trigonometric functions form an orthonormal
basis; B-splines form a basis which is not orthonormal (spline functions are
not orthogonal). For an orthonormal basis, $a _ { j } = \langle f , e _ { j } \rangle ,$ and we have the
following Parseval's equality:

$$\int f ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t = \| f \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle f , e _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left\{ \int f \left( t \right) e _ { j } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 2 } .$$
(3.2)


### 3.2 Random functions

The starting point of a statistical analysis is a sample. For example,
$x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { N }$ can be heights of females age 20 from a certain population. To
perform inference, these numbers are treated as realizations of random vari-
ables $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ A setting which is often considered is that the $X _ { i }$ are
independent and share a common distribution, for example each $X _ { i }$ is normal
with mean $\mu$ and variance $\sigma ^ { 2 } .$ Statistical inference is then concerned with the
estimation, construction of confidence intervals and testing hypotheses related
to these parameters. The reader will be aware of many other inferential pro-
cedures which are not related to the normal parameters. The central point is
that they all involve measures of statistical uncertainty like significance levels
and P-values. To compute such measures, the observations $x _ { i }$ must be treated
as realizations of random variables $X _ { i }$ which have a certain distribution, e.g.
a density or a probability mass function.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="40" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

In FDA, the observations are functions. For example, $x _ { i }$ can be the growth
curve of female $i ,$ cf. Section 2.3. The 54 females considered in Section 2.3 form
a sample of size $N = 5 4$ from a large population. Since these females were
randomly selected, we treat the growth curve $x _ { i }$ as a realizations of a random
function $X _ { i } .$ We can think of $X _ { i }$ as the growth curve of the ith female before
she has been selected. The shape and other attributes of $X _ { i }$ are thus random.
In this section, we describe how they can be quantified. We will focus on
attributes similar to the mean and variance of scalar observations, and we
will explain what it means that a random function is Gaussian. These topics
are revisited in much greater detail in Chapter 11.

In the remainder of this section $X$ denotes a random function. Just as
a random variable, a random function is defined on a probability space, say
$\Omega ,$ so for each $\omega \in \Omega ,$ $X \left( \omega \right)$ is a deterministic function. We assume that all
realizations $X \left( \omega \right) ,$ $\omega \in \Omega ,$ are elements of the space $L ^ { 2 }$ of square integrable
functions. This means that of each $\omega \in \Omega ,$

$$\| X \left( \omega \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \int \left\{ X \left( \omega \right) \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t < \infty .$$

The function $\omega \mapsto \| X \left( \omega \right) \|$ is thus a random variable (it takes nonnegative
values), and we can ask if it has a finite second moment or not. If it does,
i.e. if $E \| X \| ^ { 2 } < \infty ,$ we say that the random function $X$ is square integrable.
It is important to see the distinction between a square integrable determinis-
tic function (integration refers to the interval $\left. \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right)$ and a square integrable
random function (integration refers to the probability space (2). It might be
more pedagogical to say that a random function has a finite second moment,
rather that to say that it is square integrable, but we will stick to this more
usual terminology. In the following, we assume that the random function $X$ is
square integrable; this ensures that all objects we study exist. In particular,
the analog of the variance, the covariance function, exists in a suitable space.

In Chapter 1, we considered the sample mean and covariance functions.
Recall that if we observe curves $x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { N } ,$ then the sample mean function
is defined by $\widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } x _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$ and the sample covariance function by

$$\widehat { c } \left( t , s \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left( x _ { i } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) \right) \left( x _ { i } \left( s \right) - \widehat { \mu } \left( s \right) \right) .$$

In inferential procedures, we often treat the curves $x _ { i }$ as realizations of a
random function $X ,$ for which we define the mean and covariance functions
by

$$\mu \left( t \right) = E X \left( t \right) , \quad c \left( t , s \right) = E \left[ \left( X \left( t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) \left( X \left( s \right) - \mu \left( s \right) \right) \right] .$$
(3.3)

The sample functions $\widehat { \mu }$ and $\widehat { c }$ are viewed as estimators of the population
parameters $\mu$ and c.

The chief importance of the covariance function in FDA is that it leads

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="41" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Mathematical framework for functional data" -->

to Functional Principal Component Analysis. We explain here the idea at an
intuitive level. Every square integrable function $X$ can be represented as

$$X \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$
(3.4)

The $v _ { j }$ depend on the covariance function $c$ of $X .$ Specifically, they are defined
as the eigenfunctions of c, i.e. they are solutions to the equation

$$\int c \left( t , s \right) v \left( s \right) d s = \lambda v \left( t \right) .$$

There are countably many solutions, $\left( \lambda _ { 1 } , v _ { 1 } \right) ,$ $\left( \lambda _ { 2 } , v _ { 2 } \right) , \ldots ,$ which we call the
eigenelements of $X$ (or of the covariance function c). The $\lambda _ { j }$ are called the
eigenvalues of $X$ (or c). The eigenvalues, and so the corresponding eigenfunc-
tions $v _ { j }$ are arranged in nonincreasing order: $\lambda _ { 1 } \geq \lambda _ { 2 } \geq \ldots .$ The random
variables $\xi _ { i } ,$ called the scores, are given by

$$\xi _ { j } = \langle X - \mu , v _ { j } \rangle = \int \left( X \left( t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) v _ { j } \left( t \right) d t .$$

It can be shown that

$$E \xi _ { j } = 0 , E \xi _ { j } ^ { 2 } = \lambda _ { j } , \mathrm { C o v } \left( \xi _ { j } , \xi _ { k } \right) = 0 , \mathrm { i f } j \neq k .$$

and

$$E \int \left( X \left( t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) ^ { 2 } d t = E \| X - \mu \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } .$$
(3.5)

The interpretation of the above formula is that $\lambda _ { j }$ is the variance of the random
function $X$ in the principal direction $v _ { j } .$ The sum of these variances is the total
variance of $X .$ Relation (3.5) is thus a decomposition, or analysis, of variance.

Decomposition (3.4) is optimal in a sense that $X$ can be well approximated
using only very few initial $v _ { j } ,$ in many applications 2-3 of them. We will
refer to (3.4) as the Karhunen-Loéve expansion of $X .$ The functions $v _ { j }$ form
an orthonormal basis and are called the Functional Principal Components
(FPC's) of $X .$

EXAMPLE 3.2.1 [Brownian motion] In Chapter 1 we used 50 realizations (a
sample of size $\left. N = 5 0 \right)$ of the random walk to illustrate the computation
of the sample mean and covariance functions, as well as of the estimated
FPC's. We generated the random walk on $\mathrm { a }$ grid of ten thousand points. As
the grid becomes finer and finer, a realization of random walk approaches a
realization of a random function known as the Brownian motion or the Wiener
process. This random function can be defined on the whole positive half-line,
but to stick to the setting of this section, we consider its restriction to the

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="42" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ The Wiener process we consider is thus the random function
$W = \left\{ W \left( t \right) , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right\} .$ It can be shown that

$$\mu \left( t \right) = E W \left( t \right) = 0 , \quad c \left( t , s \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( W \left( t \right) , W \left( s \right) \right) = \min \left( t , s \right) ,$$
$$\lambda _ { j } = \frac { 1 } { \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) ^ { 2 } \pi ^ { 2 } } , \quad v _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi t \right) .$$

The Karunen-Loéve expansion (3.4) becomes

$$W \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { Z _ { j } } { \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi } \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi t \right) , .$$
(3.6)

where the $Z _ { j }$ are $i n d e p e n d e n t$ standard normal. The scores $\xi _ { j }$ are thus given
by $\xi _ { j } = \left( \left( j - 1 / 2 \right) \pi \right) ^ { - 1 } Z _ { j } .$ The decomposition of variance (3.5) becomes

$$E \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } W ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } t d t = \frac { 1 } { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) ^ { 2 } \pi ^ { 2 } } .$$

Note that expansion (3.6) offers a way of generating smooth approximations
to the Brownian motion; the sum must be truncated at some finite $j .$ This
is an alternative to a step-wise approximation by a random walk. The latter
approach is often preferred on "visual grounds" because it produces jugged
realizations which reflect the fact that realizations of $W$ are not differentiable
functions. However, if this aspect is not important, a truncated version of (3.6)
can be used as well, and leads to a computationally faster way of generating
approximate realizations of the Wiener process.
☐

We conclude this section with the definition of a Gaussian random func-
tion. Expansion (3.6) shows that the Wiener process is a linear combination of
orthonormal functions $v _ { j }$ with scores which are normal random variables. This
observation leads to a general definition. A random function $X$ is Gaussian $i f$
the scores $\xi _ { j }$ in (3.4) are Gaussian random variables. An equivalent definition
is that $X$ is Gaussian if for any function $u \in L ^ { 2 } ,$ the random variable $\langle X , u \rangle$
is Gaussian.


### 3.3 Linear transformations

Linear transformation play a very important role in statistics. In the straight
line regression $y _ { i } - \mu _ { Y } = \beta \left( x _ { i } - \mu _ { X } \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } ,$ the centered explanatory observations
are multiplied by the slope $\beta$ to yield centered responses observed with an
error. In FDA, many types of regressions are considered; they involve linear
transformations of functions to functions, functions to scalars and vectors to

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="43" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Mathematical framework for functional data" -->

functions. They will be discussed in subsequent chapters. In this section, we
introduce some basic concepts related to general linear transformations to
help the reader follow the chapters on functional regressions. A more detailed
treatment is given in Chapter 10.

Linear transformations, often called operators, are defined on vector
spaces. If $\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } ,$ $\mathcal{V} _ { 2 }$ are two vector spaces, a function $L : \mathcal{V} _ { 1 } \rightarrow \mathcal{V} _ { 2 }$ is called
linear if $L \left( a x + b y \right) = a L \left( x \right) + b L \left( y \right) ,$ for any x, $y \in \mathcal{V} _ { 1 }$ and any scalars a, $b .$

EXAMPLE 3.3.1 Suppose $\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } = \mathbb{R} ^ { 2 } ,$ $\mathcal{V} _ { 2 } = \mathbb{R} ,$ and consider the following $t w o$
transformations:

$$L \left( \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \right) \right) = 2 x _ { 1 } + 3 x _ { 2 } , \quad G \left( \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \right) \right) = x _ { 1 } x _ { 2 } .$$

It is easy to check that the $L$ is linear and $G$ is not.
☐

Two situations commonly encountered in FDA are those with $\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } =$
$L ^ { 2 } ,$ $\mathcal{V} _ { 2 } = \mathbb{R}$ and $\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } = L ^ { 2 } , \mathcal{V} _ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } .$ In both cases, we use integral operators
defined, respectively, as follows

$$L _ { 1 } \left( x \right) = \int \psi \left( t \right) x \left( t \right) d t , \quad L _ { 2 } \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s .$$

The operator $L _ { 1 }$ transforms a function $x$ to a real number $L _ { 1 } \left( x \right)$ $L _ { 2 }$ transforms
a function $x$ to another function, $L _ { 2 } \left( x \right) .$ The reader is encouraged to check
that both transformations are indeed linear. To ensure that the number $L _ { 1 } \left( x \right)$
is finite, we must assume that $\int \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t < \infty$ to ensure that the function
$L _ { 2 } \left( x \right)$ is square integrable whenever $x$ is so, we must assume that

$$\int \int \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < \infty .$$
(3.7)

These assumptions are stronger than what is actually needed, but they are
common and simplify many arguments. We emphasize that no similar as-
sumptions are needed in the theory of scalar or vector regressions because
such regressions involve matrices consisting of a finite number of entries, and
all sums are consequently finite. The functions $\psi$ are called kernels of the
respective operators. An integral operator with the kernel satisfying (3.7) is
called a Hilbert-Schmidt operator.

An operator that is used very often in FDA is the covariance operator. The
covariance function $c$ defined in (3.3) is its kernel, so its action on a function
$x \in L ^ { 2 }$ is defined by $C \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \int c \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s .$ The $\mathrm { F P C } ^ { \prime } \mathrm { s } v _ { \imath }$ introduced in
Section 3.2 are thus the eigenfunctions of $C : C \left( v _ { j } \right) = \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } .$ The operator $C$
has a very convenient representation

$$C \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle v _ { j } ,$$
(3.8)

which is known as its spectral decomposition. Representation (3.8) is analo-
gous to the diagonal representation of the covariance matrix by means of the
Cholesky decomposition.

<!-- PageBreak -->


<figure>

Taylor & Francis
Taylor & Francis Group
http://taylorandfrancis.com

</figure>


<!-- PageBreak -->


## 4 Scalar-on-function regression

Linear regression is one of the most fundamental tools in statistics. In the
usual linear model

$$y _ { i } = x _ { i 1 } \beta _ { 1 } + x _ { i 2 } \beta _ { 2 } + \ldots x _ { i p } \beta _ { p } + \varepsilon _ { i } , \quad i = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N ,$$
(4.1)

all variables and parameters are scalars, and the regressors $x _ { i k }$ are typically
assumed to be known scalars. In a functional linear model, some of these quan-
tities are curves, and analogs of the coefficients $\beta _ { k }$ must be then appropriately
defined.

Functional regression models are subdivided into three broad categories,
depending on whether the responses or the regressors, or both, are curves. We
assume for simplicity that the responses and the regressors have mean zero.

Scalar-on-function regression:

$$Y _ { i } = \int \beta \left( s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } .$$
(4.2)

The regressors are curves, but the responses are scalars. The parameter is
the regression function $\beta .$

Function-on-scalar regression:

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } x _ { i k } \beta _ { k } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$
(4.3)

The responses are curves, but the regressors are scalars. The parameters
are the coefficient functions $\beta _ { k }$ .

The function-on-function regression:

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \int \beta \left( t , s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$
(4.4)

The responses $Y _ { i }$ are curves, and so are the regressors $X _ { i } .$ The parameter
is the kernel (surface) $\beta .$

The above formulations are just prototypes intended to illustrate the gen-
eral idea. The main point is that the functional parameters are infinite di-
mensional objects which must be estimated from a finite sample. Without any

<!-- PageNumber="45" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="46" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

restrictions on the functional parameters, a perfect fit is possible (all residuals
are zero), and the resulting estimates are erratic, noisy functions which do
not provide useful insights. Estimation therefore often involves additional re-
strictions, either by imposing smoothness conditions or restricting the action
of the corresponding operators to appropriate subspaces. Several chapters of
Ramsay and Silverman (2005) are dedicated to functional linear models and
contain the discussion of some issues which are not addressed in this book. In
this chapter, we focus on the scalar response model which bears the closest
resemblance to the standard model (4.1); the discrete index $k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , p$
is replaced by the continuous index s. This model also illustrates many is-
sues common to all models with parameters which are functions. Chapter 5 is
dedicated to regressions with functional responses.

This chapter proceeds as follows. In Section 4.1, we present several ex-
amples of data that can be modeled using scalar-on-function regression. We
follow with Section 4.2 which reviews the standard regression theory, and
then with Section 4.3 which explains chief differences between the cases of
functional and vector regressors. The next three sections explain several ap-
proaches to the estimation the regression function. Implementation of these
approaches in the R package refund is illustrated in Section 4.7. We con-
clude the chapter with Section 4.8 which discusses nonlinear approaches to
scalar-on-function regression.


### 4.1 Examples

In this section, we present several data sets that have been analyzed using
scalar-on-function regression. The first data sets consists of data for $N = 6 0$
gasoline samples. It is available as gasoline in the refund package, which
is discussed in greater detail in Section 4.7. The responses $Y _ { i } ,$ $1 \leq i \leq N ,$
shown in Figure 4.1, are octane ratings obtained through a careful chemical
analysis of each gasoline sample. The explanatory curves are near infrared
spectral curves. The curve for the first gasoline sample is shown in the left
panel of Figure 4.2. The differences between the 60 curves are small relative
to their range, two representative differences are shown in the right panel of
Figure 4.2. The purpose of regression analysis is to determine the form of
dependence between the octane rating $Y$ and the spectral curve $X .$ If we can
find a functional $g : L ^ { 2 } \rightarrow R$ such that $Y \approx g \left( X \right) ,$ then it is easy to determine
an approximate octane rating of a gasoline sample from its spectral curve. The
spectral curves are easy to obtain, while chemical analysis is time consuming
and expensive.

The second data set consists of data for $N = 2 1 5$ meat samples. It is avail-
able as tecator in the R package fda . usc. Similarl to the gasoline example,
the explanatory curves are spectral curves (defined at shorter wavelengths).

These curves are shown in the left panel of Figure 4.3. The right panel shows

in the left panel. There are three scalar responses for each meat sample: fat,
water and protein content in percent, obtained by chemical analysis. As for
the gasoline samples, the objective is to determine the form of dependence
between the spectral curves and the response variables to quickly determine
the response without performing a chemical analysis which may be impratical
in an industrial setting.

Scalar-on-function regression has been applied to Diffusion Tensor Imag-
ing data discussed in Section 1.5. The regressors are curves like those shown
in Figure 1.12, or similar curves obtained from a brain imaging. The responses
are various scores of patients, for example The PASAT (Paced Auditory Se-
rial Addition Test) scores. These scores measure cognitive function and assess
auditory information processing speed and flexibility, as well as calculation
ability.

### 4.2 Review of standard regression theory

There are dozens of excellent textbooks on linear regression. For example, Far-
away (2009) provides an exposition which carefully addresses practical aspects
while Seber and Lee (2003) focus on the theory. In this section, we review only
the most fundamental properties to provide some reference points.

In the standard linear model (4.1), the response for the $i$ ith subject, $y _ { i } ,$ is
equal to a linear combination of the observed values, $x _ { i 1 } , x _ { i 2 } , \ldots , x _ { i p }$ of the
vector $\mathrm { x } = \left[ x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { p } \right]$ of explanatory variables (regressors) plus a mean
zero random error $\varepsilon _ { i } .$ It is often assumed that

$$y _ { i } , \mathrm { x } _ { i } = \left[ x _ { i 1 } , x _ { i 2 } , \ldots , x _ { i p } \right] ^ { T } , \varepsilon _ { i }$$

are id realizations of the random variables y, $\mathrm { x } = \left[ x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { p } \right] ^ { T } ,$ E. The
population model for (4.1) thus is $y = x ^ { T } \beta + \varepsilon .$ If the regressors $\mathrm { X } _ { i }$ are treated
as deterministic, then it is enough to assume that the errors $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are iid. If, in
addition, the errors are assumed to be normal, then the least square estimates
of the parameters $\beta _ { 1 } , \beta _ { 2 } , \ldots , \beta _ { p }$ are jointly normal with standard errors which
can be readily estimated.

The least squares estimation of the parameter vector $\beta = \left[ \beta _ { 1 } , \beta _ { 2 } , \ldots , \beta _ { p } \right] ^ { T }$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="49" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 4.3: Left: Absorbance curves of 100 meat samples; Right: Derivatives of these curves. Curves of samples with fat content of less than 20% are in red.</figcaption>

Absorbances

Derivatives

2.0 2.5 3.0 3.5 4.0 4.5 5.0 5.5

0.04

0.02

0.00

-0.02

850

900

950

1000

1050

850

900

950

1000

1050

Wavelength

Wavelength

</figure>


proceeds as follows. Introduce the following vectors and matrices:

$$\mathrm { Y } = \left[ \begin{array}{} y _ { 1 } \\ y _ { 2 } \\ \vdots \\ y _ { N } \end{array} \right] , \quad \mathrm { X } = \left[ \begin{array}{} x _ { 1 1 } & x _ { 1 2 } & \ldots & x _ { 1 p } \\ x _ { 2 1 } & x _ { 2 2 } & \ldots & x _ { 2 p } \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ x _ { N 1 } & x _ { N 2 } & \ldots & x _ { N p } \end{array} \right] , \quad \beta = \left[ \begin{array}{} \beta _ { 1 } \\ \beta _ { 2 } \\ \vdots \\ \beta _ { p } \end{array} \right] , \quad \varepsilon = \left[ \begin{array}{} \varepsilon _ { 1 } \\ \varepsilon _ { 2 } \\ \vdots \\ \varepsilon _ { N } \end{array} \right] .$$
(4.5)

In this notation, relations (4.1) can be succinctly written as

$$\mathrm { Y } = \mathrm { X } \beta + \varepsilon .$$
(4.6)

The least squares estimator of $\beta$ to minimize the Euclidean norm
of the difference $\mathrm { Y } - \mathrm { X } \beta ,$ i.e. the distance between $\mathrm { Y }$ and $\mathrm { X } \beta .$ Denote by $L _ { X }$
the subspace of $\mathbb{R} ^ { N }$ spanned by the columns of $\mathrm { X }$ and denote by $\theta = \mathrm { X } \beta$ a
generic element of $L _ { X } .$ By the projection theorem, cf. Theorems 10.2.1 and
10.2.2, $\mathrm { X } \widehat { \beta }$ must be the projection $\widehat { \theta }$ of $\mathrm { Y }$ onto $L _ { X } .$ Thus $\widehat { \theta }$ is the unique vector
minimizing the length of $\mathrm { Y } - \theta$ over $\theta \in L _ { X } .$ The least square estimator $\widehat { \beta }$
must thus satisfy $\widehat { \theta } = \mathrm { X } \widehat { \beta } .$ While $\widehat { \theta }$ is always unique (as a projection), $\widehat { \beta }$ can
be uniquely determined only if the columns of $\mathrm { X }$ are linearly independent, i.e.
if $\mathrm { X }$ has rank $p .$ Assuming this is the case, we now explain how $\widehat { \beta }$ is computed.
The vector $\mathrm { Y } - \widehat { \theta }$ is orthogonal to $L _ { X } ,$ so $\mathrm { X } ^ { T } \left( \mathrm { Y } - \widehat { \theta } \right) = 0 ,$ i.e. $\mathrm { X } ^ { T } \widehat { \theta } = \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } .$
Therefore $\widehat { \beta } \mathrm { s a t i s f i e s }$ the normal equations

$$\mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \widehat { \beta } = \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } .$$

It can be shown, Problem 4.1, that if $\mathrm { X }$ is of $\mathrm { r a n k } p ,$ then $\mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X }$ is nonsingular,
and so the least squares estimator of $\beta$ is given by

$$\widehat { \beta } = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } .$$
(4.7)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="50" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Combining relations (4.6) and (4.7), it can be shown that the estimator $\widehat { \beta }$ is
unbiased. Under the assumption that the errors $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are iid normal, the standard
errors of the components of $\widehat { \beta }$ can be estimated, see e.g. Chapter 3 of Seber
and Lee (2003).

In fact, the least squares estimator works well under a wide variety of
assumptions. Notice that we can write

$$\widehat { \beta } = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \left( \mathrm { X } \beta + \varepsilon \right) = \beta + \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \varepsilon .$$ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒

To ensure that the estimator is consistent, i.e. $\widehat { \beta } \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } \beta ,$ as $N \rightarrow \infty ,$ it is enough
if

$$N ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \rightarrow \Sigma _ { X } \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad N ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \varepsilon \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } 0 .$$

If the matrix $\mathrm { X }$ is deterministic, the existence of $\Sigma _ { X }$ is assumed. The second
condition follows from the law of large numbers, $N ^ { - 1 } \varepsilon \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } 0 ,$ which holds un-
der very general assumptions on the errors. If the regressors are random, the
above conditions can be established under a wide range of assumptions includ-
ing dependence, heterogeneous errors, and increasing dimensions. Though the
least squares estimator may not be optimal in these situations, it is often still
consistent and asymptotically normal.

A problem that often arises in regression analysis is that of testing the
significance of some coefficients $\beta _ { \ell }$ in (4.1). For example, if $\beta _ { p }$
icantly different form zero, then a simpler model with only the first $p \quad - \quad 1$
regressors should be used. To streamline the exposition, suppose that the ex-
planatory variables are ordered in such a way that the simpler model has the
form $y _ { i } = x _ { i 1 } \beta _ { 1 } + \ldots + x _ { i m } \beta _ { m } + \delta _ { i } ,$ for some $m < p .$ We want to test the null
hypothesis

$$H _ { 0 } : \beta _ { m + 1 } = \ldots = \beta _ { p } = 0 .$$

Assuming that the errors in both models are iid normal, the standard proce-
dure is to use the F-test. To define the test statistic, we introduce the following
quantities

$$R _ { p } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left( y _ { i } - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } x _ { i j } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( p \right) } \right) ^ { 2 } , \quad R _ { m } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left( y _ { i } - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { m } x _ { i j } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( m \right) } \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

The estimators $\widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( p \right) }$ and $\widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( m \right) }$ are the least squares estimators computed, re-
spectively, using the model with $p$ and $m$ regressors. Each of the quantities, $R _ { p }$
and $R _ { m } ,$ is called the residual error sum of squares. Since $m < p ,$ $R _ { m } \geq R _ { p } .$ If
$\beta _ { m + 1 } = \ldots = \beta _ { p } = 0 .$ the difference $R _ { m } - R _ { p }$ will be small, otherwise it will
be large. The F-statistic is basically a suitably normalized difference $R _ { m } - R _ { p }$
defined by

$$F = \frac { \left( R _ { m } - R _ { p } \right) / \left( p - m \right) } { R _ { p } / \left( N - p \right) } .$$
(4.8)

If $H _ { 0 }$ is true, then $F$ has the $F _ { p - m , N - p }$ distribution (assuming iid normal
errors). The null hypothesis is rejected if the observed $F$ statistic exceeds a
suitable upper quantile of this distribution.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="51" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->


### 4.3 Difficulties specific to functional regression

This section contains a discussion of those aspects of functional regression
which are peculiar to functional setting, or much more pronounced in this
setting than in the usual multiple regression (4.1). An important distinction
is that in functional regression, we are interested not only in computing an
estimate of the function $\beta$ in (4.2), but we must also ensure that this estimate
has a useful interpretation, and can be used for effective prediction of responses
from explanatory functions. Intervals of the $s$ values where $| \beta \left( s \right) |$ is large are
influential for the responses, and the sign of $\beta \left( s \right)$ on those intervals implies
either negative or positive association. If an estimate $\widehat { \beta }$ jumps chaotically, no
such interpretation is possible; predictions tend to center around the mean of
the responses and have large variance.

To see differences between the usual regression model $\left( 4 . 1 \right)$ and the scalar-
on-function regression, it is illustrative to begin with an alternative derivation
of the estimator (4.7). Consider the population version of (4.1), i.e. assume
that

$$Y = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \beta _ { i } X _ { i } + \varepsilon$$

in which $\left[ X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { p } , \varepsilon \right]$ is a random vector. We will first find $\beta$ which
minimizes

$$R \left( \beta \right) = E \left( Y - \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \beta _ { i } X _ { i } \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

This is done by equating the partial derivatives $\partial R \left( \beta \right) / \partial \beta _ { \ell }$ to zero for $\ell =$
$1 , 2 , \ldots , p .$ In Problem 4.3, the reader is asked to verify that this leads to the
solution

$$\beta = \mathrm { C } _ { X } ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { C } _ { X Y } ,$$
(4.9)

where

$$\mathrm { C } _ { X } = \left[ E \left[ X _ { i } X _ { j } \right] , 1 \leq i , j \leq p \right] , \quad \mathrm { C } _ { X Y } = \left[ E \left[ X _ { i } Y \right] , 1 \leq i \leq p \right] ^ { T } .$$

We emphasize that to obtain (4.9), we must assume that the $p \times p \text { matrix } \mathrm { C } _ { X }$
is invertible. In Problem 4.4, the reader is asked to verify that the invertibility
of $\mathrm { C } _ { X }$ is equivalent to the linear independence of the variables $X _ { i } ,$ i.e. to the
implication

$$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \alpha _ { i } X _ { i } = 0 \quad \Longrightarrow \quad \alpha _ { 1 } = \alpha _ { 2 } = \ldots = \alpha _ { p } = 0 .$$
(4.10)

Linear independence of explanatory variables is a common assumption because
otherwise some of them could be expressed as linear combinations of others,
and would not be needed. To obtain an estimator based on expression $\left( 4 . 9 \right) ,$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="52" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

we replace the matrix $\mathrm { C } _ { X }$ and the vector $\mathrm { C } _ { X Y }$ by their method of moments
estimators whose entries are defined by

$$\widehat { C } _ { X } \left( i , j \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n i } X _ { n j } , \quad \widehat { C } _ { X Y } \left( i \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n i } Y _ { n } .$$

Using the notation introduced in Section 4.2, these estimators can be repre-
sented as

$$\widehat { \mathrm { C } } _ { X } = N ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } , \quad \widehat { \mathrm { C } } _ { X Y } = N ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } ,$$

leading to the usual least squares estimator (4.7).

We now turn to the functional regression (4.2) for which the population
model is

$$Y = \int \beta \left( t \right) X \left( t \right) d t + \varepsilon .$$

Our objective is to retrace the above steps and obtain an estimator of
the function $\beta \left( \cdot \right) .$ We will see the difficulties such an extension entails. We
cannot compute the partial derivatives to find the minimum of $R \left( \beta \right) =$
$E \left( Y - \int \beta \left( t \right) X \left( t \right) d t \right) ^ { 2 }$ because there are infinitely many, in practice too many,
values $\beta _ { t } = \beta \left( t \right) .$ We can however attempt to derive an analog of (4.9) is a
different way. Define the analogs of the matrix $\mathrm { C } _ { X }$ and the vector $\mathrm { C } _ { X Y }$ by

$$c _ { X } \left( t , s \right) = E \left[ X \left( t \right) X \left( s \right) \right] , \quad c _ { X Y } \left( t \right) = E \left[ X \left( t \right) Y \right] .$$

Assuming that $X$ and $\varepsilon$ are independent, we obtain, Problem 4.6,

$$c _ { X Y } \left( t \right) = \int c _ { X } \left( t , s \right) \beta \left( s \right) d s .$$
(4.11)

The above relation can be viewed as a system of infinitely many linear equa-
tions, in practice of a large number of equations, which is impossible, or diffi-
cult, to solve. Even if it is possible to solve, experience shows that the resulting
function $\beta \left( \cdot \right)$ evaluated at a large number of points $t _ { j }$ is very noisy and diffi-
cult to interpret. This is because solving equation (4.11) without any further
restrictions does not utilize any intuition that the function $\beta \left( \cdot \right)$ should be
smooth. One must postulate some special form of this function to make the
problem practically solvable with an interpretable solution. We will $d e s c r i b e$
such approaches in Sections 4.4 and 4.5. There is another justification why,
in general, it is not possible to solve equation (4.11). Assuming $E X \left( t \right) = 0 ,$
we recognize the kernel $c _ { X } \left( t , s \right)$ as the covariance functions of the random
function $X ,$ cf. (3.3). In Chapter 3, we also observed, cf. (3.8), that the right-
hand side of (4.11) can be written as $C _ { X } \left( \beta \right) ,$ where $C _ { X }$ is the integral operator
with kernel $c _ { X } \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) v _ { j } \left( s \right) ,$ with nonnegative eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ and
eigenfunctions $v _ { j }$ which form an orthonormal basis in the space $L ^ { 2 } .$ In this
operator notation, equation (4.11) can be written as $C _ { X } \left( \beta \right) = c _ { X Y } .$ It cannot
be always solved because the inverse operator $C _ { X } ^ { - 1 }$ does not exist, cf. Prob-
lem 4.5. Arguments based on such principal components expansion motivate
the approach described in Section 4.6.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="53" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->

Another elucidating perspective on methods presented in this Chapter can
be gained by viewing the scalar response model (4.2) as an approximation to
the regression

$$Y _ { i } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \beta \left( t _ { j } \right) X _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) + \varepsilon _ { i }$$

with a large number of points $t _ { j } .$ If $t _ { j }$ is close to $t _ { j ^ { \prime } } ,$ then, for every $i ,$ the
values $X _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right)$ and $X _ { i } \left( t _ { j ^ { \prime } } \right)$ will be close. If the curves are approximately peri-
odic, the values $X _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right)$ and $X _ { i } \left( t _ { j ^ { \prime } } \right)$ can be close even for $t _ { j }$ not geometrically
close to $t _ { j ^ { \prime } } .$ The point is that there will, in general, be many column vectors
$\mathrm { X } \left( t _ { j } \right) = \left[ X _ { 1 } \left( t _ { j } \right) , X _ { 2 } \left( t _ { j } \right) , \ldots , X _ { N } \left( t _ { j } \right) \right] ^ { T }$ which are strongly correlated, or nearly
linearly dependent in some complex way. If the columns of the matrix $\mathrm { X }$ in
(4.5) are nearly linearly dependent, we say that the regression exhibits $c o l -$
inearity, or multicolinearity. In such cases, the variances of some components
of $\widehat { \beta }$ given by (4.7) become large, so precise estimation is not possible. This
is intuitively understandable because if two regressors are linearly related,
strongly correlated, then their coefficients can be almost arbitrarily chosen as
long as their linear combination has a certain value. To illustrate, consider the
regression

$$Y _ { i } = \beta _ { 1 } x _ { i 1 } + \beta _ { 2 } x _ { i 2 } + \varepsilon _ { i }$$

with just two regressors. One can show that if the correlation coefficient be-
tween the vectors $\left[ x _ { 1 1 } , x _ { 2 1 } , \ldots , x _ { N 1 } \right] ^ { T }$ and $\left[ x _ { 1 2 } , x _ { 2 2 } , \ldots , x _ { N 2 } \right] ^ { T }$ is r, then the
variances of the least squares estimators are

$$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \widehat { \beta } _ { 1 } \right] = \frac { \sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } } { \left( 1 - r ^ { 2 } \right) s _ { 1 } ^ { 2 } } , \quad s _ { 1 } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } x _ { i 1 } ^ { 2 } ;$$
$$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \widehat { \beta } _ { 2 } \right] = \frac { \sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } } { \left( 1 - r ^ { 2 } \right) s _ { 2 } ^ { 2 } } , \quad s _ { 2 } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } x _ { i 2 } ^ { 2 } .$$

The simplest, and often very effective, solution to collinearity is to remove
regressors which can be effectively replaced by other regressors, i.e. to reduce
the number of explanatory variables. This is not reasonable in the context of
functional explanatory variables because it would mean removing some chunks
of curves over certain intervals. More sophisticated solutions to collinearity are
ridge regression and principal component regression. Ridge regression is simi-
lar to the estimation with a roughness penalty, Section 4.5, and the principal
component regression is analogous to the method described in Section 4.6. A
reader interested in these methods in the context of the standard model (4.1)
is referred to Seber and Lee (2003), or any other recent textbook on regression
analysis.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="54" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 4.4 Estimation through a basis expansion

To enhance practical applicability, in this and the following sections, we con-
sider the model (4.2) with an intercept term, i.e.

$$Y _ { i } = \alpha + \int \beta \left( s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } , \quad i = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N .$$
(4.12)

The simplest approach is to expand the function $\beta$ using deterministic basis
functions, and so to reduce the problem to the standard model (4.6). Assume
then that

$$\beta \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) .$$
(4.13)

The selected basis functions $B _ { k }$ influence the shape of the estimate. The num-
ber $K$ of basis functions is generally chosen to $b e$ smaller than the number of
time points $t _ { j }$ at which the functions $X _ { i }$ are observed. It reflects intuition as to
how smooth the resulting estimate should be. There are data driven methods
of selecting $K ,$ which we discuss below, but in some applications they lead
to estimates that are difficult to interpret from the angle of a specific applied
problem. It is always useful to experiment with several selections of $K .$

Using expansion (4.13), we obtain

$$\int \beta \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } \int B _ { k } \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t = : \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } c _ { k } .$$

Model (4.12) thus reduces to (4.6), and the parameter vector $\mathrm { c } =$
$\left[ \alpha , c _ { 1 } , c _ { 2 } , \ldots , c _ { K } \right] ^ { T }$ (corresponding to $\beta$ is estimated by $\widehat { \mathrm { c } } = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } ,$
☒ ☒ ☒
where

$$X = \left[ \begin{array}{} { 1 } & { x _ { 1 1 } } & { x _ { 1 2 } } & { \cdots } & { x _ { 1 K } } \\ { 1 } & { x _ { 2 1 } } & { x _ { 2 2 } } & { \cdots } & { x _ { 2 K } } \\ { : } & { : } & { : } & { : } & { : } \\ { 1 } & { x _ { N 1 } } & { x _ { N 2 } } & { \cdots } & { x _ { N K } } \end{array} \right]$$
(4.14)

A disadvantage of this method is that the resulting estimate

$$\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { c } _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right)$$

depends on the shape of the basis functions $B _ { k }$ and on their number $K ,$
both being subjectively chosen. A reasonable approach is to use the same
basis functions as in expansions (1.3) of the regressor functions. Furthermore,
the theory supporting this estimation procedure, assumes that $\beta$ is exactly
equal to a linear combination of the basis functions. In practice this only

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="55" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->

holds approximately which must be respected to produce proper inferential
procedures.

The reduction to the standard model (4.6) also leads to approximate con-
fidence intervals for the unknown regression function $\beta .$ If the errors, $\varepsilon _ { i } ,$ are
independent $\mathcal{N} \left( 0 , \sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } \right) ,$ then the covariance matrix of $\widehat { \mathrm { c } }$ is $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \widehat { \mathrm { c } } \right] = \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } ,$
☒ ☒
Problem 4.2. The error variance, $\sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } ,$ is estimated by the sample variance, $\widehat { \sigma } _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } ,$
of the residuals

$$\widehat { \varepsilon } _ { i } = y _ { i } - \widehat { \alpha } - \int \widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t = y _ { i } - \widehat { \alpha } - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } \widehat { c } _ { k } .$$

The variance of $\widehat { c } _ { k }$ is thus estimated by the corresponding diagonal entry of
the matrix $\widehat { \sigma } _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } .$ Denoting this estimate by $\widehat { \sigma } _ { k } ^ { 2 } ,$ an approximate 95%
☒ ☒

confidence interval for the regression function $\beta$ is

$$\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { c } _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) \pm 1 . 9 6 \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { \sigma } _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) .$$

The above confidence interval can be used only as an exploratory tool. Pre-
cise confidence statements can be made only about the coefficients $c _ { k }$ in the
expansion (4.13), which itself is only an approximation.

We now provide a discussion of the bias resulting from using expansion
(4.13) which involves only a finite number $K$ of basis function. This discussion
further illustrates the issues arising in the functional case which are absent in
the standard multivariate case. The function $\beta$ can be expanded, without any
approximation error, only using all basis functions, i.e. $\beta \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } c _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) .$
Therefore,

$$\beta \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { k = K + 1 } ^ { \infty } c _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } B _ { k } \left( t \right) + \delta \left( t \right) ,$$

where $\delta \left( t \right)$ is the truncation error. In this case, we have

$$\int \beta \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } c _ { k } + \int \delta \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t : = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } c _ { k } + \delta _ { i } .$$

The model is now not the same as the traditional regression model as $\mathrm { Y } =$
$\mathrm { X c } + \delta + \varepsilon .$ The least squares estimate of the coefficients, $\mathrm { c } ,$ in (4.13), is given
by

$$\widehat { \mathrm { c } } = \mathrm { c } + \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \delta + \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \varepsilon .$$ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒

As opposed to traditional multivariate methods, the LS estimator is now ac-
tually biased. The analysis of this bias explains why to obtain an asymptoti-
cally consistent estimate in the functional case, it is necessary to assume that
$K$ increases to infinity with the sample size $N .$ The discussion that follows

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="56" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

should be compared with the discussion of the consistency of 3 presented
at the end of Section 4.2. We focus on $\mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \delta ,$ but note that inverting $\mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \mathrm { X }$
☒ ☒ ☒ ☒
can also cause practical problems. Recall that $\delta \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = K + 1 } ^ { \infty } c _ { j } B _ { j } \left( t \right)$ and
$\delta _ { i } = \int \delta \left( s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s .$ The kth, $1 \leq k \leq K + 1 ,$ component of $N ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \delta$ is
☒ ☒

$$N ^ { - 1 } \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \delta \right) \left( k \right) = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } x _ { i k } \delta _ { i }$$
$$= N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left( \int B _ { k } \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t \right) \left( \int \delta \left( s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s \right)$$
$$= \iint B _ { k } \left( t \right) \widehat { c } _ { X } \left( t , s \right) \delta \left( s \right) d t d s$$
$$= \sum _ { j = K + 1 } ^ { \infty } c _ { j } \iint B _ { k } \left( t \right) \widehat { c } _ { X } \left( t , s \right) B _ { j } \left( s \right) d t d s ,$$ ☒

where $\widehat { c } _ { X } \left( t , s \right) = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i } \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) .$ Thus, for a fixed $K ,$
☒

$$\lim _ { N \rightarrow \infty } N ^ { - 1 } \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { \top } \delta \right) \left( k \right) = \sum _ { j = K + 1 } ^ { \infty } c _ { j } \iint B _ { k } \left( t \right) c _ { X } \left( t , s \right) B _ { j } \left( s \right) d t d s ,$$ ☒

which, in general, is a nonzero number. To make the above limit vanish, we
must assume that $K = K \left( N \right) \rightarrow \infty ,$ and impose assumptions on the rate of
decay of the $c _ { j } .$


### 4.5 Estimation with a roughness penalty

The parameter $K$ in the previous section should be viewed as a tuning pa-
rameter; adjusting $K$ adjusts the smoothness of the resulting estimator $\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) .$
From this perspective, it is often more desirable to smooth by using a rough-
ness penalty term. As in the previous approach, the starting point is expansion
(4.13), but now $K$ is taken to be some large value so that $\widehat { \beta }$ is no longer sensi-
tive to the value of $K .$ It is often recommended to use $K$ equal to the number
of points $t _ { j }$ at which curves $X _ { i }$ are observed. The roughness penalty approach
☒
shifts the control of smoothness from $K$ to the smoothing parameter $\lambda$ and a
differential operator $L$ :

$$P _ { \lambda } \left( \alpha , \beta \right) = \sum _ { i - 1 } ^ { N } \left\{ Y _ { i } - \alpha - \int \beta \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 2 } + \lambda \int \left[ \left( L \beta \right) \left( t \right) \right] ^ { 2 } d t .$$
(4.15)

In (4.15), $L$ is a differential operator that acts on the function $\beta$ and $\lambda$ is a
smoothing parameter. The idea is to force smoothness by penalizing functions

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="57" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->

which are too rough with a penalty term $\lambda \int \left[ \left( L \beta \right) \left( t \right) \right] ^ { 2 } d t .$ A common choice
of $L$ is the second derivative: $\left( L \beta \right) \left( t \right) = \beta ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) .$ The most critical aspect of this
method is the selection of $\lambda$ if it is too large, the estimate of $\beta$ is too smooth
and important aspects of its shape can be suppressed; if it is too small, the
estimate of $\beta$ will reflect random errors. We first explain how the estimate
of $\beta$ is computed for a fixed $\lambda ,$ then we comment on methods of selecting it.
Using the notation introduced in Section 4.4, we can rewrite (4.15) as

$$P _ { \lambda } \left( \alpha , \beta \right) = P _ { \lambda } \left( \alpha , c _ { 1 } , c _ { 2 } , \ldots , c _ { K } \right)$$
$$= \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left\{ Y _ { i } - \alpha - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } c _ { k } \right\} ^ { 2 } + \lambda \int \left[ \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } \left( L B _ { k } \right) \left( t \right) \right] ^ { 2 } d t$$
$$= \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left\{ Y _ { i } - \alpha - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } x _ { i k } c _ { k } \right\} ^ { 2 } + \lambda \sum _ { k , k ^ { \prime } = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { k } c _ { k ^ { \prime } } R _ { k k ^ { \prime } } ,$$

where

$$R _ { k k ^ { \prime } } = \int \left( L B _ { k } \right) \left( t \right) \left( L B _ { k ^ { \prime } } \right) \left( t \right) d t .$$

In Problem 4.7, the reader is asked to show that the parameter vector $\mathrm { c } =$
$\left[ \alpha , c _ { 1 } , c _ { 2 } , \ldots , c _ { K } \right] ^ { T }$ minimizing this expression is given by

$$\widehat { \mathrm { c } } = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } + \lambda \mathrm { R } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } ,$$ ☒ ☒ ☒
(4.16)

where $\mathrm { X }$ is the matrix $\left( 4 . 1 4 \right)$ and $\mathrm { R }$ is the $\left( K + 1 \right) \times \left( K + 1 \right)$ matrix given by
☒

$$\mathrm { R } = \left[ \begin{array}{} 0 & 0 & 0 & \ldots & 0 \\ 0 & R _ { 1 1 } & R _ { 1 2 } & \ldots & R _ { 1 K } \\ 0 & R _ { 2 1 } & R _ { 2 2 } & \ldots & R _ { 2 K } \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & R _ { K 1 } & R _ { K 2 } & \ldots & R _ { K K } \end{array} \right] .$$

We conclude this section by commenting on how the $\lambda$ in (4.16) can be
selected. Broadly speaking, the choice of $\lambda$ reflects the researchers belief on
how smooth an estimate of $\beta$ should be. In practice, it is reasonable, to obtain
estimates for several values of $\lambda$ and choose one based on a visual inspection.
In statistical theory, the belief of a researcher is quantified by mathematical
assumptions on the unknown function $\beta .$ Mathematical derivations then lead
to an optimal $\lambda$ based on this assumed smoothness of $\beta .$ One can however
never be certain that the postulated smoothness conditions actually hold.
The optimal $\lambda$ will also depend on the measure of distance between the target
$\beta$ and its estimator.

An approach that has gained a fairly broad acceptance is known as cross-
validation (CV). It uses the quality of predictions of the $Y _ { i }$ as a criterion,
rather than a postulated smoothness of $\beta .$ As a result, $\mathrm { C V }$ often produces
a $\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right)$ that appears too noisy, as it attempts to optimize the predictive fit.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="58" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

The method works as follows. $F o r \quad a \quad f i x e d > ,$ compute the estimates $\widehat { \alpha } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) }$ and
$\widehat { \beta } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) }$ using the sample without $\left( Y _ { i } , X _ { i } \right) .$ Then compute the best prediction of
$Y _ { i }$ based on these estimates, i.e.

$$\widehat { Y } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) } = \widehat { \alpha } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) } + \int \widehat { \beta } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) } \left( s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s .$$

The preferred value of $\lambda$ should make the differences $Y _ { i } - \widehat { Y } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) }$ collectively
small. The CV method thus selects $\lambda$ which minimizes

$$S _ { N } \left( \lambda \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left\{ Y _ { i } - \widehat { Y } _ { \lambda } ^ { \left( - i \right) } \right\} ^ { 2 } .$$

In software implementations, the user can generally specify the range of the
values of $\lambda$ and the resolution of the grid. The idea of cross-validation de-
scribed above has been extended in many directions. For example, the func-
tion $S _ { N }$ can be replaced by a different measure of predictive fit, samples with
more than one pair of observations removed can be used.

Other approaches to the selection of smoothing parameters in regres-
sion models include information criteria and restricted maximum likelihood
(REML). These approaches require more background than can be presented
in this textbook; Krivobokova and Kauermann (2007) is a good reference.
In the context of scalar-on-function regression of this section, an approach
based on a connection to a mixed model formulation has been found particu-
larly useful. Advantage can be taken of existing software to estimate random
effect variance using REML, and this variance acts as the tuning parameter
that controls the smoothness of the coefficient function $\beta \left( \cdot \right) .$ The numerical
implementation discussed in Section 4.7 is based on this approach. An inter-
ested reader is referred to Goldsmith $e t$ al. (2011) and Ruppert $e ^ { \dagger }$ al. (2003),
Chapter 5.


### 4.6 Regression on functional principal components

We have seen in Section 3.2 that every random function $X$ in $L ^ { 2 }$ admits the
expansion

$$X \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$

where the $v _ { j }$ are the functional principal components, i.e. the eigenfunctions of
the covariance operator of $X .$ Denote by $\widehat { v } _ { j }$ the estimated functional principal
components (EFPC's) of the regressor functions $X _ { i } .$ In the FPC regression,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="59" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Scalar-on-function regression" -->

reduction to the standard model (4.6) is achieved by using the approximation

$$X _ { i } \left( t \right) \approx \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \xi } _ { i j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad \widehat { \xi } _ { i j } = \int \left[ X _ { i } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) \right] \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) d t .$$

Replacing this approximation by an equality, informally reduces the scalar
response model (4.2) to

$$Y _ { i } = \alpha + \int \beta \left( t \right) \left( \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \xi } _ { i j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) \right) d t + \varepsilon _ { i }$$
$$= \beta _ { 0 } + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \xi } _ { i j } \beta _ { j } + \varepsilon _ { i } ,$$

where

$$\beta _ { 0 } = \alpha + \int \beta \left( t \right) \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) d t \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad \beta _ { j } = \int \beta \left( t \right) \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) d t$$

are treated as unknown parameters. Defining the $N \times \left( p + 1 \right)$ matrix

$$\Xi = \left[ \begin{array}{} 1 & \widehat { \xi } _ { 1 1 } & \widehat { \xi } _ { 1 2 } & \cdots & \widehat { \xi } _ { 1 K } \\ 1 & \widehat { \xi } _ { 2 1 } & \widehat { \xi } _ { 2 2 } & \cdots & \widehat { \xi } _ { 2 K } \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 1 & \widehat { \xi } _ { N 1 } & \widehat { \xi } _ { N 2 } & \cdots & \xi _ { N K } \end{array} \right] , .$$

we estimate the parameter vector $\beta = \left[ \beta _ { 0 } , \beta _ { 1 } , \ldots \beta _ { p } \right] ^ { T }$ by the least squares
estimator in the regression $\mathrm { Y } = \Xi \beta + \varepsilon .$ Denoting the estimates thus obtained
by $\widehat { \beta } _ { 0 } , \widehat { \beta } _ { 1 } , \ldots , \widehat { \beta } _ { p } ,$ the estimates of the parameters in (4.2) are

$$\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad \widehat { \alpha } = \widehat { \beta } _ { 0 } - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } \int \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) d t .$$

An important aspect of this method is the selection of the number of EFPC's,
$p .$ A simple but effective approach uses the smallest number $p$ such the the
first $p$ EFPC's explain 85 or 90 percent of cumulative variance, cf. Section 1.3.

Despite its appealing simplicity, the principal component regression leads
to estimates whose large sample properties are far from obvious. In addition
to the selections of the number of EFPC's, $p ,$ which is analogous to the selec-
tion of $\lambda$ in Section 4.5, it introduces another approximation error by treating
the entries of the design matrix E as deterministic, whereas, in fact, these
are random quantities with a complex dependence structure; the EFPC's $\widehat { v } _ { j }$
are computed using all regression functions $X _ { i } .$ All methods described in this
chapter must be approximate because they aim at estimating an infinite di-
mensional function $\beta$ using only a finite sample. Their consistency can be
established by imposing smoothness conditions on the target function $\beta$ and
allowing the tuning constants like $K ,$ $\lambda ,$ $p$ to depend on the sample size $N .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="60" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 4.7 Implementation in the $\mathrm { r e f u n d }$ package

In this section, we present implementation in the refund package of the
methods discussed in Sections 4.4, 4.5 and 4.6. They are also implemented
in the fda package, see Ramsay et al. (2009). We use several packages than
enable graphics functions. The analysis to follow must thus be preceded by
the call to the required packages:

rm (list=ls ())
library (refund) ; library (ggplot2)
library (dplyr) ; library (reshape2)
set . seed (9000)

When using many packages, it is advisable to clear the work space with the
rm command to make sure that the analysis does not accidentally use unre-
lated variables. For consistency, we also set the seed of the random number
generator.

We use simulated data to be able to compare the estimates to the true
regression function $\beta .$ We consider model (4.12) with $\alpha = 0 .$ We perform
analysis separately for two regression functions:

$$\beta _ { 1 } \left( t \right) = \sin \left( 2 \pi t \right) , \quad \beta _ { 2 } \left( t \right) = - f _ { 1 } \left( t \right) + 3 f _ { 2 } \left( t \right) + f _ { 3 } \left( t \right) , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$$

where $f _ { 1 } ,$ $f _ { 2 } ,$ $f _ { 3 }$ are normal densities with parameters specified in the following
code:

n = 1000; grid = seq(0, 1, length = 101)
beta = sin (grid * 2 * pi)
beta = - dnorm (grid, mean =. 2, sd =. 03) +3*dnorm (grid, mean =. 5,

$$\left. s d = . 0 4 \right) + d n o r m \left( g r i d _ { \prime } m e a n = . 7 5 , s d = . 0 5 \right)$$

The functions $\beta _ { 1 }$ and $\beta _ { 2 }$ are shown as "Truth", respectively, in Figures 4.4
and 4.5.

The regressor curves are generated as

$X \left( t _ { j } \right) = Z t _ { j } + U + \eta \left( t _ { j } \right) + \varepsilon \left( t _ { j } \right) ,$ $Z \sim N \left( 1 , 0 . 2 ^ { 2 } \right) ,$ $U \sim \mathrm { U N I F } \left[ 0 , 5 \right]$ $\eta \left( t _ { j } \right) \sim N \left( 0 , 1 \right) .$

The iid variables $\eta \left( t _ { j } \right)$ introduce a noisy component to the distribution of the
regressor functions. The random curves, $\varepsilon \left( t \right) ,$ are generated as

$$\varepsilon \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { 1 0 } \frac { 1 } { k } \left\{ Z _ { 1 k } \sin \left( 2 \pi t k \right) + Z _ { 2 k } \cos \left( 2 \pi t k \right) \right\} ,$$

where $Z _ { 1 k } ,$ $Z _ { 2 k }$ are independent standard normal. The generation of $N =$
1, 000 independent pairs $\left( X _ { i } , \varepsilon _ { i } \right)$ of curves is accomplished with the following
code:

Goldsmith and Scheipl (2014) demonstrated on several real data that the
performance of the various estimation methods, measured by prediction er-
rors, depends on the data set. We can draw a similar conclusion from Figures
4.4 and 4.5. For the regression function $\beta _ { 1 }$ in Figure 4.4, all three methods
produce similar estimates. For the function $\beta _ { 2 }$ in Figure 4.5, methods based
on the simple basis expansions and on EFPC's produce almost identical and
reasonably good estimates. The penalized approach oversmooths. Further de-
tails on the application of these methods can be found in the help files of the
package $\mathrm { r e f u n d } ,$ which also contains several other estimation methods for
scalar-on-function regression.

### 4.8 Nonlinear scalar-on-function regression

Methods for scalar-on-function regression that do not rely on model (4.12)
are referred to as nonlinear. We discuss two of them.

The first method is based on the functional generalized additive model,
which is also called the continuously additive model. It takes the form

$$Y _ { i } = \alpha + \int f \left( X _ { i } \left( t \right) , t \right) d t + \varepsilon _ { i } ,$$
(4.17)

where the bivariate function $\left( x , t \right) \mapsto f \left( x , t \right)$ is assumed to be smooth. It is
estimated by an approximation f $f \left( x , t \right) \approx \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { I } \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { L } f _ { i \ell } B _ { i } ^ { \star } \left( x \right) B _ { \ell } \left( t \right) ,$ where the
$B _ { i } ^ { \star }$ and $B _ { j }$ are spline functions, which can be of different type, as the range
and properties of the values $X _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right)$ can be different from those of the domain
☒
points $t _ { j } .$ A penalty term is also introduced, following arguments analogous
to those presented in Section 4.5. This method is implemented in the $\mathrm { r e f u n d }$
package with the default call $a f \left( X \right) ,$ see McLean et al. (2014) for further
details.

A method that has gained a great deal of popularity and has been exten-
sively studied is based on a fully nonparametric representation

$$Y _ { i } = m \left( X _ { i } \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } ,$$ ☒
(4.18)

where $m : L ^ { 2 } \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ is a functional that must be estimated. A large part
of the monograph of Ferraty and Vieu (2006) is devoted to the study of this
method, so we merely highlight one possible approach to the estimation of the
functional m. For an arbitrary function $x \in L ^ { 2 } ,$ which however is thought of
as being in some sense close to the observed regressor functions $X _ { i } ,$ we define

$$\widehat { m } \left( x \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { i } \left( x \right) Y _ { i } , \quad \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { i } \left( x \right) = 1 . ,$$

The idea is that a response to a new regressor function $X \left( t \right)$ should be a
weighted average of known responses with coefficients $w _ { i } \left( x \right)$ which give more
weight to values $Y _ { i }$ which correspond to regressors close to x. A common
approach is to use

$$w _ { i } \left( x \right) = \frac { K \left( h ^ { - 1 } d \left( x , X _ { i } \right) \right) } { \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } K \left( h ^ { - 1 } d \left( x , X _ { i } \right) \right) } ,$$

where $d \left( \cdot , \cdot \right)$ is some measure of distance between two functions. The kernel $K$
is defined on the nonnegative half-line and is some decreasing function, with
$K \left( t \right)$ equal, or close, to zero for large t. The most critical way of selecting the
$Y _ { i }$ to be effectively included in the estimation of $m \left( x \right)$ is through the selection
of $h .$ For any given kernel $K ,$ for the $Y _ { i }$ to be effectively included, $h ^ { - 1 } d \left( x , X _ { i } \right)$
must be close to zero. Thus, if $h$ is small, few $Y _ { i }$ will be included. As $h$ increases,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="64" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

more and more $Y _ { i }$ are included. This will lead to estimates with a smaller vari-
ance but a larger bias (oversmoothing). Selecting the optimal $h$ thus requires
a balance between reducing variance and increasing the bias. Several methods
of achieving it have been proposed. The simplest choice of $d \left( \cdot , \cdot \right)$ would be to
consider the $L ^ { 2 }$ distance introduced in Chapter 3, i.e. $d \left( x , y \right) = \| x - y \| ,$ but
this choice is not always optimal. If the regressors $X _ { i }$ are sufficiently smooth
functions, the $L ^ { 2 }$ distance between the second derivatives is often useful, i.e.
one measures distance between functions by $d \left( x , y \right) = \| x ^ { \prime \prime } - y ^ { \prime \prime } \| .$ Notice that
this function is not a metric in the usual sense because it is possible that
$d \left( x , y \right) = 0$ for some $x \neq y .$ It is an example of a semimetric. The performance
of predictors based on model (4.18) may depend quite a bit on a selection of
the distance $d .$

A number of other approaches, both linear and nonlinear, and discussed
in the review paper of Reiss et al. (2016).


### 4.9 Chapter 4 problems

4.1 Consider the design matrix $\mathrm { X }$ in (4.5). Show that if $\mathrm { X }$ has rank $p ,$ then
$\mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X }$ is nonsingular.

4.2 Consider the linear model (4.6) and the least squares estimator (4.7).
Suppose $\mathrm { X }$ is a deterministic matrix of rank $p$ and the errors $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are uncorre-
lated with variance $\sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } .$ Show that $E \left[ \widehat { \beta } \right] = \beta$ and $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \widehat { \beta } \right] = \sigma _ { \varepsilon } ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } .$

4.3 Verify relation (4.9).

4.4 Show that the matrix $\mathrm { C } _ { X }$ in (4.9) is invertible if and only if relation
(4.10) holds.

4.5 Suppose $C$ is a covariance operator defined by (3.8). We will show in
Chapter 11 that we must have $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } < \infty$ . Consider functions with expan-
sions

$$f \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } f _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad g \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } g _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

Show that the equation $C \left( g \right) = f$ implies $\lambda _ { j } g _ { j } = f _ { j } .$ Find an example $\mathrm { o f }$
eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ and coefficients $f _ { j }$ which satisfy $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } f _ { j } ^ { 2 } < \infty ,$ so that $f \in L ^ { 2 } ,$
but there is no $g \in L ^ { 2 }$ such that $C \left( g \right) = f .$

4.6 Verify relation (4.11).

4.7 Prove (4.16) by equating the partial derivatives to zero and solving a
linear system.
