## 5 Functional response models

At the beginning of Chapter 4, we introduced a general classification of func-
tional regression models. We divided them into three broad classes: scalar-
on-function, function-on-scalar and function-on-function. In Chapter 4, we
explained the main features and estimation principles of functional regression
models using the scalar-on-function regression, and studied this type of re-
gression in detail. In this chapter, we turn to the remaining two classes, both
of them have functions as responses. We will focus on the practical aspects
of estimation of these models, assuming that the reader is familiar with the
general principles of penalized estimation introduced in Chapter 4.

We begin by introducing in Section 5.1 the function-on-scalar model and
showing how it can be easily estimated using the least squares principle. Sec-
tion 5.2 focuses on penalized estimation of this model. Penalized estimation
of function-on-function regression is explained in Section 5.3. The implemen-
tation of these penalized estimation approaches in the $r e f u n d$ package is
illustrated in Section 5.4. The next two sections focus on inference for the
function-on-function regression which is based on functional principal com-
ponents. Section 5.5 is concerned with estimation, Section 5.6 with a hypoth-
esis test. Section 5.7 explains how the validity of the assumption that the
data follow a functional linear regression can be evaluated. We explain how
to construct suitable diagnostic plots similar to the plots used in scalar linear
regression models. We conclude with Section 5.8 which provides references to
papers which elaborate on the topics studied in this chapter.


### 5.1 Least squares estimation and application to angular motion

The function-on-scalar regression model is

i = 1, 2,., N. (5.1)
$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = x _ { i 1 } \beta _ { 1 } \left( t \right) + x _ { i 2 } \beta _ { 2 } \left( t \right) + \ldots + x _ { i q } \beta _ { q } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$$

For each unit i, the response $Y _ { i }$ is a function. There are $q$ scalar explanatory
variables. $\mathrm { T h e } q$ functional regression parameters $\beta _ { \ell }$ are often called the effect

<!-- PageNumber="67" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="68" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

functions. Setting

$$\mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) = \left[ \begin{array}{} Y _ { 1 } \left( t \right) \\ Y _ { 2 } \left( t \right) \\ \vdots \\ Y _ { N } \left( t \right) \end{array} \right] , \mathrm { X } = \left[ \begin{array}{} x _ { 1 1 } & x _ { 1 2 } & \ldots & x _ { 1 q } \\ x _ { 2 1 } & x _ { 2 2 } & \ldots & x _ { 2 q } \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ x _ { N 1 } & x _ { N 2 } & \ldots & x _ { N q } \end{array} \right] , \beta \left( t \right) = \left[ \begin{array}{} \beta _ { 1 } \left( t \right) \\ \beta _ { 2 } \left( t \right) \\ \vdots \\ \beta _ { q } \left( t \right) \end{array} \right] , \varepsilon \left( t \right) = \left[ \begin{array}{} \varepsilon _ { 1 } \left( t \right) \\ \varepsilon _ { 2 } \left( t \right) \\ \vdots \\ \varepsilon _ { N } \left( t \right) \end{array} \right] ,$$

equations (5.1) can be written as $\mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) = \mathrm { X } \beta \left( t \right) + \varepsilon \left( t \right) .$ This section presents
the simplest, yet often effective, approach to the estimation of the parameter
functions $\beta _ { \ell }$ in model (5.1), followed by an illustrative application of this
model.

The least squares estimator of the functional parameter $\beta$ is defined as the
value minimizing

$$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \| Y _ { i } - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { q } x _ { i k } \beta _ { k } \| ^ { 2 } = \int \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } e _ { i } ^ { 2 } \left( \beta , t \right) d t ,$$
(5.2)

where

$$e _ { i } ^ { 2 } \left( \beta , t \right) = \left( Y _ { i } \left( t \right) - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { q } x _ { i k } \beta _ { k } \left( t \right) \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

For each fixed $t ,$ $\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } e _ { i } ^ { 2 } \left( \beta , t \right)$ is minimized if

$$\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) ,$$ ☒ ☒ ☒
(5.3)

see Section 4.2. Thus for any t, $\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } e _ { i } ^ { 2 } \left( \widehat { \beta } , t \right) \leq \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } e _ { i } ^ { 2 } \left( \beta , t \right) ,$ so the func-
tions (5.3) minimize (5.2). The fitted (predicted) values and the residuals are
computed using

$$\widehat { \mathrm { Y } } \left( t \right) = \mathrm { X } \widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) , \quad \widehat { \varepsilon } \left( t \right) = \mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mathrm { Y } } \left( t \right) .$$

The least squares estimation procedure above assumes that the functions
$Y _ { i }$ are fully observed. The functional objects can be constructed from raw
data $Y _ { i } \left( t _ { i j } \right)$ in a way most suitable to a problem at hand. For example, basis
expansions or interpolation can be used. For response functions defined on a
grid which does not depend on $i ,$ and without any missing values, one can
work directly with the raw observations $Y _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) .$

We illustrate the above estimation approach using data collected for a
study aimed at designing an ergonomic car dashboard. Figure 5.1 shows 20
sets of 3 curves. Each set corresponds to a certain location that a driver would
reach by taking his hand off the steering wheel. These data are part of a larger
data set, and correspond only to one driver reaching each location 3 times,
with appropriate randomization and rest intervals. There are thus 60 response
curves, but one outlier curve (in the left.rear.shifter box) has been removed
(the driver changed his mind half way through the motion). The sample size

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="69" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

is thus $N = 5 9 .$ The values $Y _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$ $t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ represent the angle formed by
the shoulder, elbow and wrist. The physical time required to reach a target
is different for every motion; the time has been rescaled to the unit interval
to represent the portion of the motion between start and end. Each of the 20
targets is described by its Cartesian coordinates $\left[ x , y , z \right] .$

The initial regression that can be entertained is

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \beta _ { 0 } \left( t \right) + x _ { i } \beta _ { x } \left( t \right) + y _ { i } \beta _ { y } \left( t \right) + z _ { i } \beta _ { z } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

In this regression $q = 4 .$ Using the estimates $\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \left[ \widehat { \beta } _ { 0 } \left( t \right) , \widehat { \beta } _ { x } \left( t \right) , \widehat { \beta } _ { y } \left( t \right) , \widehat { \beta } _ { z } \left( t \right) \right] ^ { T }$
computed according to (5.3), it has been found that the fitted curves $\widehat { \mathrm { Y } } \left( t \right) =$
$\mathrm { X } \widehat { \beta } \left( t \right)$ provide a poor and inconsistent approximation to the responses. A
better fit can be obtained by using the regression

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \beta _ { 0 } \left( t \right) + x _ { i } \beta _ { x } \left( t \right) + y _ { i } \beta _ { y } \left( t \right) + z _ { i } \beta _ { z } \left( t \right)$$
$$+ x _ { i } y _ { i } \beta _ { x y } \left( t \right) + y _ { i } z _ { i } \beta _ { y z } \left( t \right) + z _ { i } x _ { i } \beta _ { z x } \left( t \right)$$
$$+ x _ { i } ^ { 2 } \beta _ { x ^ { 2 } } \left( t \right) + y _ { i } ^ { 2 } \beta _ { y ^ { 2 } } \left( t \right) + z _ { i } ^ { 2 } \beta _ { z ^ { 2 } } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$$

with $q = 1 0 .$ The above regression can be used to predict the angular curve
Yx0,30,20(t), $t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ for any location $\left[ x _ { 0 } , y _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } \right]$ which may be different from
any of the original 20 locations, but which is not too far away from the overall
range of the experimental locations. Such information may aid the car cockpit
design with the goal of reducing the need for excessive and difficult motions.

Figure 5.2 shows the first four EFPC's of the residual curves $\widehat { \varepsilon } _ { i } ,$ $1 \leq i \leq 5 9 ,$
computed using the formula $\widehat { \varepsilon } \left( t \right) = \mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mathrm { Y } } \left( t \right)$ together with the correspond-
ing eigenvalues. Note that $\widehat { \lambda } _ { 4 } = 5 . 3$ is over 30 times smaller than the largest
eigenvalue $\widehat { \lambda } _ { 1 } = 1 8 2 . 6 .$ The shape of the first EFPC $\widehat { v } _ { 1 }$ indicates that there is
more variability in the middle part of the motion than at the start and end.
This can be understood by realizing that the motion must start and end at
a specific location, whereas the spatial path in the middle of the motion is
less constrained. The shapes of the remaining EFPC's are to a large extent
determined by their orthogonality. For example, the shape of $\widehat { v } _ { 2 }$ not only re-
flects the second most important mode of variability, but also the condition
that $\widehat { v } _ { 2 }$ must also be orthogonal to $\widehat { v } _ { 1 } ,$ and this mathematical condition places
a constraint on its shape. For this reason, the subsequent $\widehat { v } _ { j }$ have more and
more zero crossings. The shapes of the EFPC's in Figure 5.2 are thus typical
of those obtained from other "well-behaved" functional samples.


### 5.2 Penalized least squares estimation

In Section 5.1, the regression functions $\beta _ { \ell }$ were estimated pointwise. This is
justified if the response functions are smooth, as is the case for the angular

<!-- PageNumber="71" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="72" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

motion data. If the responses are noisy, the above approach is modified by
introducing a penalty that favors smooth estimates $\widehat { \beta } _ { \ell } .$ This reflects the as-
sumptions that the functions $\beta _ { \ell }$ in (5.1) are smooth, but the error functions
$\varepsilon _ { i }$ are noisy, leading to noisy response functions $Y _ { i } .$

The starting point of estimation with a penalty is to assume that the
functions $\beta _ { \ell }$ admit the expansion

$$\beta _ { \ell } \left( t _ { j } \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } b _ { \ell k } \varphi _ { k } \left( t _ { j } \right) ,$$
(5.4)

where $\varphi _ { 1 } , \varphi _ { 2 } , \ldots , \varphi _ { K }$ are some basis functions, whose number should be large.
These functions do not have to be orthogonal or normalized. Assuming that
the responses are available at points $t _ { j } ,$ $1 \leq j \leq J ,$ model (5.1) can be written
as

$$\mathrm { Y } = \mathrm { X B } \Phi + \varepsilon ,$$

using the following matrices:

$$\mathrm { Y } = \left[ Y _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) , 1 \leq i \leq N , 1 \leq j \leq J \right] , \left( N \times J \right) ,$$
$$\mathrm { X } = \left[ x _ { i \ell } , 1 \leq i \leq N , 1 \leq \ell \leq q \right] , \left( N \times q \right) ,$$
$$\mathrm { B } = \left[ b _ { \ell k } , 1 \leq \ell \leq q , 1 \leq k \leq K \right] , \left( q \times K \right) ,$$
$$\Phi = \left[ \varphi _ { k } \left( t _ { j } \right) , 1 \leq k \leq K , 1 \leq j \leq J \right] , \left( K \times J \right) ,$$
$$\varepsilon = \left[ \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) , 1 \leq i \leq N , 1 \leq j \leq J \right] , \left( N \times J \right) .$$

The penalized sum of squared residuals is then defined as

$$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \left( Y _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) - \left[ \mathrm { X B } \Phi \right] _ { i j } \right) ^ { 2 } + \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { q } \lambda _ { \ell } \int \left\{ \left( L \beta _ { \ell } \right) \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t .$$
(5.5)

The integrand in the penalty term can be expressed $\mathrm { i n }$ terms of the elements
of the matrix $\mathrm { B }$ and derivatives of the basis functions $\varphi _ { k } .$ For example, if the
second derivative is used, then

$$\left( L \beta _ { \ell } \right) \left( t \right) = \beta _ { \ell } ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } b _ { \ell k } \varphi _ { k } ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) .$$

Similarly as in Section 4.5, the matrix $\mathrm { B }$ minimizing (5.5) can be expressed
in terms of the matrices $\mathrm { X } ,$ $\Phi ,$ $\mathrm { Y }$ and the matrices

A = diag(A1, 12, ... , Ag), $\mathrm { a n d } \mathrm { R } = \left[ \int \left( L \varphi _ { \ell } \right) \left( t \right) \left( L \varphi _ { k } \right) \left( t \right) d t , 1 \leq \ell , k \leq K \right] .$

To state the formula, we must introduce the concept of a vectorized matrix and
of the Kronecker product of two matrices. Suppose $\mathrm { M }$ is a $p \times q$ matrix. Then
$\mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { M } \right)$ is a column vector of length $p q$ constructed by stacking the columns of

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="73" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

M, placing the first column on top. To define the Kronecker product, suppose
$\mathrm { A }$ and $\mathrm { B }$ are two matrices with the following dimensions

$$\dim \left( \mathrm { A } \right) = p _ { A } \times q _ { A } , \quad \dim \left( \mathrm { B } \right) = p _ { B } \times q _ { B } .$$

Then, the Kronecker product $\mathrm { A } \otimes \mathrm { B }$ is the $p _ { A } p _ { B } \times q _ { A } q _ { B }$ matrix

$$A \otimes B = \left[ \begin{array}{} a _ { 1 1 } B & a _ { 1 2 } B & \ldots & a _ { 1 q _ { A } } B \\ a _ { 2 1 } B & a _ { 2 2 } B & \ldots & a _ { 2 q _ { A } } B \\ a _ { 2 1 } B & a _ { 2 2 } B & \ldots & a _ { 2 q _ { A } } B \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ a _ { p _ { A } 1 } B & a _ { p _ { A } 2 } B & \ldots & a _ { p _ { A } q _ { A } } B \end{array} \right] .$$

Using this notation, the entries of the matrix $\mathrm { B }$ which minimizes (5.5) are
given by

$$\mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { B } ^ { T } \right) = \left[ \mathrm { U } ^ { T } \mathrm { U } + \Lambda \otimes \mathrm { R } \right] ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { U } ^ { T } \mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { Y } ^ { T } \right) , \quad \mathrm { U } = \mathrm { X } \otimes \Phi ^ { T } .$$
(5.6)

The derivation of (5.6) is complex and is omitted. An alternative expression
for $\mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { B } ^ { T } \right)$ can be obtained by noticing that $\mathrm { U } ^ { T } \mathrm { U } = \left( \mathrm { X } ^ { T } \mathrm { X } \right) \otimes \left( \Phi \Phi ^ { T } \right) .$ This
follows from the following properties of the Kronecker product:

$$\left( \mathrm { A } \otimes \mathrm { B } \right) ^ { T } = \mathrm { A } ^ { T } \otimes \mathrm { B } ^ { T } , \quad \left( \mathrm { A } \otimes \mathrm { B } \right) \left( \mathrm { C } \otimes \mathrm { D } \right) = \left( \mathrm { A C } \right) \otimes \left( \mathrm { B D } \right) .$$

The above properties can be established by direct verification that uses the
definition.

In many applications, it is common to work with the response functions
$Y _ { i }$ expressed in terms of a basis expansion:

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } c _ { i k } \varphi _ { k } \left( t \right) , \quad 1 \leq i \leq N .$$
(5.7)

Expansion (5.7), even with a large $K ,$ is typically an approximation of the
data, but in the following we assume it holds exactly. It is advisable $t o$ use the
same basis in expansions (5.4) and (5.7) because then the predicted functions
have the appearance and the degree of smoothness similar to the responses,
cf. (5.1). Introducing the matrix

$$\mathrm { C } = \left[ c _ { i k } , 1 \leq i \leq N , 1 \leq k \leq K \right] , \left( N \times K \right) ,$$

we can write relation (5.7) as $\mathrm { Y } \left( t \right) = \mathrm { C } \varphi \left( t \right) ,$ with the vector $\mathrm { Y } \left( t \right)$ defined
following (5.1) and $\varphi \left( t \right)$ defined by

$$\varphi \left( t \right) = \left[ \varphi _ { 1 } \left( t \right) , \varphi _ { 2 } \left( t \right) , \ldots , \varphi _ { K } \left( t \right) \right] ^ { T } .$$

Using the relation $\beta \left( t \right) = \mathrm { B } \varphi \left( t \right) ,$ we see that the penalized least squares loss
function takes the form

$$\int \| \mathrm { C } \varphi \left( t \right) - \mathrm { X B } \varphi \left( t \right) \| ^ { 2 } d t + \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { q } \lambda _ { \ell } \int \left\{ \left( L \beta _ { \ell } \right) \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t ,$$
(5.8)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="74" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

where the norm in the first integral is the Euclidean norm in $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } .$ The penalty
term in (5.8) is the same as in (5.5).

To state the formula for the entries of the matrix $B$ which minimizes (5.8),
introduce the matrix

$$\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi } = \left[ \int \varphi _ { k } \left( t \right) \varphi _ { \ell } \left( t \right) d t , 1 \leq k , \ell \leq K \right] , \left( K \times K \right) .$$

Note that if the functions $\varphi _ { k }$ are orthonormal, then $\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi }$ is an identity matrix.
For any $\varphi _ { k } ,$ $\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi }$ is nonnegative definite, Problem 5.1, so its square root $\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi } ^ { 1 / 2 }$
exists. It can be shown that

$$\mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { B } ^ { T } \right) = \left[ \mathrm { U } _ { \varphi } ^ { T } \mathrm { U } _ { \varphi } + \Lambda \otimes \mathrm { R } \right] ^ { - 1 } \mathrm { U } _ { \varphi } ^ { T } \mathrm { v e c } \left( \mathrm { I } _ { \varphi } ^ { 1 / 2 } \mathrm { C } ^ { T } \right) , \quad \mathrm { U } _ { \varphi } = \mathrm { X } \otimes \mathrm { I } _ { \varphi } ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$
(5.9)


### 5.3 Functional regressors

If both responses and regressors are functions, the required model is called
the function-on-function regression or a fully functional linear model. In its
simplest formulation it takes the form

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \alpha \left( t \right) + \int \psi \left( t , s \right) X _ { i } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad i = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N .$$
(5.10)

The regressors $X _ { i }$ are now functions and the regression coefficient is a bivariate
function, or kernel, $\psi .$ We will consider a more general model which can include
more functional regressors as well as scalar regressors. Since we want to focus
on the functional regressors, we will now denote the scalar regressors using
various fonts of the letter $w .$ To lighten the notation, we will consider only
two functional regressors. The methodology explained in this section can be
extended to the case of more than two regressors without any substantive
change. The model we consider thus has the form

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \alpha \left( t \right) + \mathrm { w } _ { i } ^ { T } \gamma + \int _ { \mathcal{S} } \psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s \right) X _ { i 1 } \left( s \right) d s + \int _ { \mathcal{R} } \psi _ { 2 } \left( t , r \right) X _ { i 2 } \left( r \right) d r + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$
(5.11)

For each subject i, $1 \leq i \leq N ,$ we observe

1 < < Li,
$$Y _ { i } \left( t _ { i j } \right) , 1 \leq j \leq J _ { i } , \quad X _ { i 1 } \left( s _ { i k } \right) , 1 \leq k \leq K _ { i } , \quad X _ { i 2 } \left( r _ { i \ell } \right) ,$$

as well as the values of the scalar regressors $\mathrm { w } _ { i } = \left[ w _ { i 1 } , w _ { i 2 } , \ldots , w _ { i q } \right] ^ { T } .$ The
unknown parameters are $\gamma = \left[ \gamma _ { 1 } , \gamma _ { 2 } , \ldots , \gamma _ { q } \right] ^ { T }$ and the bivariate functions $\psi _ { 1 }$
and $\psi _ { 2 } .$ The response functions are defined on a compact interval $\mathcal{T} ,$ while
the regressor functions on possibly different compact intervals $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathcal{R} .$ The
error functions $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are assumed to be independent and identically distributed
Gaussian random functions.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="75" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

To ensure identifiability, i.e. to allow the estimators to distinguish between
the kernels $\psi _ { 1 } ,$ $\psi _ { 2 }$ and the intercept function a, we assume that the regression
functions are centered before the estimation. This means that they must be
replaced by the functions

$$X _ { i 1 } ^ { c } \left( s \right) = X _ { i 1 } \left( s \right) - \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i 1 } \left( s \right) , \quad X _ { i 2 } ^ { c } \left( r \right) = X _ { i 2 } \left( r \right) - \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i 2 } \left( r \right) .$$

In the exposition that follows, we assume that this has been done, and we do
not use the superscript c.

In this chapter, we assume that, for each i, the points $t _ { i j } ,$ $s _ { i k } ,$ $r _ { i \ell }$ are dense
in their respective domains. If these points are sparse, the methodology of
Chapter 7 must be used. We now derive a discrete approximation to model
(5.11) which is used to obtain estimates of the functional parameters $\alpha ,$ $\psi _ { 1 } ,$ $\psi _ { 2 }$
and the scalar parameters $\gamma = \left[ \gamma _ { 1 } , \gamma _ { 2 } , \ldots , \gamma _ { q } \right] ^ { T } .$ We consider the most common
setting in which the functions are observed on the same dense grids for each
subject, i.e. $t _ { i j } = t _ { j } ,$ $s _ { i k } = s _ { k } ,$ $r _ { i \ell } = r _ { \ell } .$ Analogously to (5.7), in the following,
the equalities stated in terms of basis expansions and Riemann sums are only
approximations, but we use the equality sign, =, to be able to manipulate
these expressions without adding additional error terms.

The intercept function is represented as $\alpha \left( t \right) = \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } \alpha _ { m } B _ { m } \left( t \right) .$ The count
$M$ of the basis functions $B _ { 1 } , B _ { 2 } , \ldots , B _ { M } ,$ as well as the count of the other ba-
sis systems used in the following, must be large. Analogously as in Section 5.2,
smoothness of the estimated functions is achieved by applying suitable rough-
ness penalties, as will be explained below. The regression kernels $\psi _ { 1 }$ and $\psi _ { 2 }$
are expanded using bivariate basis systems. Such systems are generally con-
structed as products of univariate basis functions, Problem 5.2. We thus use
the approximations

$$\psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } \psi _ { 1 , g } B _ { 1 , g } \left( t , s \right) , \quad \psi _ { 2 } \left( t , r \right) = \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { H } \psi _ { 2 , h } B _ { 2 , h } \left( t , r \right) .$$

Using these expansions and approximating the integral by a Riemann sum,
we obtain

$$\int _ { \mathcal{S} } \psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s \right) X _ { i 1 } \left( s \right) d s = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \left( s _ { k } - s _ { k - 1 } \right) \psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s _ { k } \right) X _ { i 1 } \left( s _ { k } \right)$$
$$= \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \left( s _ { k } - s _ { k - 1 } \right) \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } \psi _ { 1 , g } B _ { 1 , g } \left( t , s _ { k } \right) X _ { i 1 } \left( s _ { k } \right)$$
$$= \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } B _ { 1 , g , i } ^ { \star } \left( t \right) \psi _ { 1 , g } ,$$

where

$$B _ { 1 , g , i } ^ { \star } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \left( s _ { k } - s _ { k - 1 } \right) B _ { 1 , g } \left( t , s _ { k } \right) X _ { i 1 } \left( s _ { k } \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="76" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Similarly,

$$\int _ { \mathcal{R} } \psi _ { 2 } \left( t , r \right) X _ { i 2 } \left( r \right) d r = \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { H } B _ { 2 , h , i } ^ { \star } \left( t \right) \psi _ { 2 , h } ,$$

where

$$B _ { 2 , h , i } ^ { \star } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { L } \left( r _ { \ell } - r _ { \ell - 1 } \right) B _ { 2 , h } \left( t , r _ { \ell } \right) X _ { i 2 } \left( r _ { \ell } \right) .$$

Observe that once the bivariate basis functions and their count have been
selected, the coefficients $B _ { 1 , g , i } ^ { \star }$ and $B _ { 2 , h , i } ^ { \star }$ are known. The counts $G$ and $H$ are
large, they are typically products of large counts of basis functions for each of
the two components. As a result of the above approximations, model (5.11) is
approximated by the model

$$Y _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) = \mathrm { w } _ { i } ^ { T } \gamma + \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } B _ { m } \left( t _ { j } \right) \alpha _ { m } + \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } B _ { 1 , g , i } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) \psi _ { 1 , g } + \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { H } B _ { 2 , h , i } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) \psi _ { 2 , h } + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) .$$

The unknown parameters are now scalars $\alpha _ { m } ,$ $1 \leq m \leq M ,$ $\psi _ { 1 , g } , 1 \leq g \leq$
$G ,$ $\psi _ { 2 , h } ,$ $1 \leq h \leq H .$ We organize them into high-dimensional vectors

$$\alpha = \left[ \alpha _ { 1 } , \ldots , \alpha _ { M } \right] ^ { T } , \psi _ { 1 } = \left[ \psi _ { 1 , 1 } , \ldots , \psi _ { 1 , G } \right] ^ { T } , \psi _ { 2 } = \left[ \psi _ { 2 , 1 } , \ldots , \psi _ { 2 , H } \right] ^ { T } .$$

.

Using this notation, we now specify a penalized least squares criterion analo-
gous to (5.5). The sum of squared residuals is

$$S \left( \gamma , \alpha , \psi _ { 1 } , \psi _ { 2 } \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \left( Y _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right) - \mu _ { i } \left( t _ { j } ; \gamma , \alpha , \psi _ { 1 } , \psi _ { 2 } \right) \right) ^ { 2 } ,$$
(5.12)

where

$$\mu _ { i } \left( t _ { j } ; \gamma , \alpha , \psi _ { 1 } , \psi _ { 2 } \right) = \mathrm { w } _ { i } ^ { T } \gamma + \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } B _ { m } \left( t _ { j } \right) \alpha _ { m }$$
$$+ \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } B _ { 1 , g , i } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) \psi _ { 1 , g } + \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { H } B _ { 2 , h , i } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) \psi _ { 2 , h } .$$

We proceed with the derivation of the penalty terms. We motivate it by specific
roughness penalties which will lead us to a general form. Suppose we want to
penalize intercept function, a, whose second derivative is cumulatively too
large over the interval $\mathcal{T} .$ A penalty in this case is

$$P _ { 0 } \left( \alpha \right) = \int _ { \mathcal{T} } \left\{ \alpha ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t .$$

Using the basis expansion, we see that

$$P _ { 0 } \left( \alpha \right) = \sum _ { m , m ^ { \prime } = 1 } ^ { M } \alpha _ { m } \alpha _ { m ^ { \prime } } \int _ { \mathcal{T} } B _ { m } ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) B _ { m ^ { \prime } } ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) d t = \alpha ^ { T } \mathrm { D } _ { 0 } \alpha ,$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="77" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

where $\mathrm { D } _ { 0 }$ is a nonnegative definite matrix. The roughness of the bivariate
functions $\psi _ { 1 }$ and $\psi _ { 2 }$ can be quantified in many ways. Suppose we require that
the Laplacian of $\psi _ { 1 } ,$ i.e.

$$\left( L \psi _ { 1 } \right) \left( t , s \right) = \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } } { \partial t ^ { 2 } } \psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s \right) + \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } } { \partial s ^ { 2 } } \psi _ { 1 } \left( t , s \right)$$

be cumulatively small over $\mathcal{T} \times \mathcal{S} .$ Using the basis expansion, we obtain for
any linear operator $L ,$ of which the Laplacian is an example,

$$\left( L \psi _ { 1 } \right) \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { g = 1 } ^ { G } \psi _ { 1 , g } \left( L B _ { 1 , g } \right) \left( t , s \right) .$$

This leads to a penalty of the form

$$P _ { 1 } \left( \psi _ { 1 } \right) = \int _ { \mathcal{T} } \int _ { \mathcal{S} } \left\{ \left( L \psi _ { 1 } \right) \left( t , s \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t d s = \psi _ { 1 } ^ { T } \mathrm { D } _ { 1 } \psi _ { 1 } ,$$

where $\mathrm { D } _ { 1 }$ has entries

$$D _ { 1 } \left( g , g ^ { \prime } \right) = \int _ { \mathcal{T} } \int _ { \mathcal{S} } \left( L B _ { 1 , g } \right) \left( t , s \right) \left( L B _ { 1 , g ^ { \prime } } \right) \left( t , s \right) d t d s .$$

We conclude that a rich class of penalized least squares estimators is obtained
by minimizing

$$S \left( \gamma , \alpha , \psi _ { 1 } , \psi _ { 2 } \right) + \lambda _ { 0 } \alpha ^ { T } \mathrm { D } _ { 0 } \alpha + \lambda _ { 1 } \psi _ { 1 } ^ { T } \mathrm { D } _ { 1 } \psi _ { 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \psi _ { 2 } ^ { T } \mathrm { D } _ { 2 } \psi _ { 2 } ,$$
(5.13)

where the sum of squared residuals, $S \left( \gamma , \alpha , \psi _ { 1 } , \psi _ { 2 } \right) ,$ is given by (5.12), and
$\mathrm { D } _ { 0 } ,$ $\mathrm { D } _ { 1 } ,$ $\mathrm { D } _ { 2 }$ are nonnegative definite matrices. The minimization can be con-
veniently carried out using the function $\mathrm { p f f r }$ in the refund package, as ex-
plained in Section 5.4. As is the case with all penalized methods, the choice of
the smoothing parameters $\lambda _ { 0 } ,$ $\lambda _ { 1 } ,$ $\lambda _ { 2 }$ requires care. The function $\mathrm { p f } \mathrm { f } \mathrm { r }$ provides
default values, but other methods discussed in this book, including generalized
cross-validation, can $a l s o$ be used.


### 5.4 Penalized estimation in the $\mathrm { r e f u n d }$ package

In this section, we provide examples of $\mathrm { R }$ code which implements the methods
of Sections 5.2 and 5.3. We focus on the implementation in the $\mathrm { r e f u n d }$ pack-
age which offers the greatest flexibility at the time of writing of this textbook.
Implementation in the fda package is discussed in Ramsay et al. (2009).

Function-on-scalar regression can be fitted by two different functions in
refund: $\mathrm { f o s } \mathrm { r }$ and $\mathrm { p f f r } .$ While $\mathrm { p f f r }$ is more powerful and flexible overall,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="78" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

the following example illustrates two features currently available only in fosr:
inputting the functional responses as $\mathrm { f } \mathrm { d }$ objects from the fda package, and
fully automated generalized least squares. (A third feature offered only by
$\mathrm { f o s } \underline { \mathrm { r } }$ is permutation testing, via the fosr . perm function, see Problem 5.14.)

To illustrate the interface between the function fosr and the fda package,
we work with the Canadian temperature data available in the fda package.
This data set will be revisited in a different context in Chapter 9. We consider
35 locations in Canada shown in Figure 9.2. Each of them belongs to one
of four climatic regions: Arctic, Atlantic, Continental and Pacific. The tem-
perature curves themselves are shown in Figure 9.1. We denote by $Y _ { i } \left( t \right)$ the
temperature at location $i$ on the tth day of the year. (These are temperatures
averaged over many years.) We postulate that

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \alpha _ { r \left( i \right) } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

The function $\mu$ represents the overall annual pattern of temperature in
Canada, we refer to it as the intercept function. The function $i \mapsto r \left( i \right)$ as-
signs one of the four regions to the location i. The function $\alpha _ { r }$ describes the
effect of the location being in region r, with $r = 2$ for Arctic, $r = 3$ for At-
lantic, $r = 4$ for Continental and $r = 5$ for Pacific. The above model is a
special case of model (5.1), with $\beta _ { 1 } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) ,$ $\beta _ { r } \left( t \right) = \alpha _ { r } \left( t \right) ,$ $r = 2 , 3 , 4 , 5 .$ The
$3 5 \times 5$ matrix $\mathrm { X }$ is defined by $x _ { i 1 } = 1$ and

$$x _ { i r } = \left\{ \begin{array}{} 1 & \text { if } i \text { is in region } r , \\ 0 & \text { otherwise } \end{array} , \quad r = 2 , 3 , 4 , 5 . \right. ,$$

Our objective is to estimate the functions $\mu \left( t \right)$ and $\alpha _ { r } \left( t \right) ,$ $r = 2 , 3 , 4 , 5 .$ To
ensure identifiability, we assume that $\sum _ { r = 2 } ^ { 5 } \alpha _ { r } \left( t \right) = 0 .$

Due to the spacial dependence between the temperature curves at neigh-
boring locations, the error curves $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are not independent, they are correlated.
The ordinary least squares estimator $\left( \mathrm { O L S } \right)$ described in Section 5.2 is opti-
mal if the error curves are uncorrelated. In case of dependence, an estimator
that takes the dependence into account may be better. Such estimators are
generally referred to as generalized least squares (GLS) estimators, and an
estimator of this type is implement in the function $\mathrm { f o s } \underline { \mathrm { r } } .$ We do not discuss
the details, which are presented in Reiss et al. (2010).

The first commands, adapted from the help file for the fRegress func-
tion in the fda package, set up a functional data object representing mean
temperatures (in degrees Celsius) for each day of the year at the 35 sites in
Canada.

require (fda) ; require (refund)
daybasis25 <- create. fourier. basis (rangeval=c (0, 365), nbasis
=25, axes=list ( 'axesIntervals' ) )
Temp . fd <- with (CanadianWeather, smooth . basisPar (day. 5,
dailyAv [, , 'Temperature. C' ], daybasis25) $fd)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="79" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

We wish to estimate an intercept function representing an overall average of
the mean temperature, along with coefficient functions that capture deviations
from this average for each of four regions. We manually set up a model matrix
$\mathrm { X }$ and sum-to-zero constraints for these four coefficient functions.

modmat=cbind (1, model. matrix (~factor (
CanadianWeather$region) -1) )
constraints = matrix (c (0,1,1,1,1),1)

The following command fits the model by penalized OLS, with smoothing
parameter chosen by grid search:

olsmod = fosr (fdobj = Temp.fd, X = modmat, con = constraints,
method="OLS", lambda=100*10:30)

Penalized GLS is implemented as follows:

glsmod = fosr (fdobj = Temp. fd, X = modmat, con = constraints,
method="GLS")

We can then display the two sets of coefficient function estimates:

par (mfrow=c (2,5), mar=c (5,2,4,1))
plot (olsmod, split=1, set.mfrow=FALSE, titles=c ("OLS: Intercept
", levels (factor (CanadianWeather$region) ) ) , ylab="", xlab="
Day")
plot (glsmod, split=1, set.mfrow=FALSE, titles=c ("GLS: Intercept
", levels (factor (CanadianWeather$region) ) ) , ylab="", xlab="
Day")

The estimates are quite similar, but in this case GLS yields somewhat
smoother coefficient functions than OLS.

We now turn to R code that illustrates how the estimation method of
Section 5.3 is implemented. We begin by defining a convenience function which
plots a matrix containing one curve in each row.

library (refund)

fplot <- function (x, y, Ity=1, col=rgb (0, 0, 0, max ( . 1, sqrt (1/
$\left. \left. n r o w \left( y \right) \right) \right)$ ),
$\left. 1 w d = . 5 , \quad \ldots \right) \left\{ \right.$
if (missing (x)) {
if (missing (y) )
stop ("must specify at least one of 'x' and 'y' ")
else x <- 1L : NROW (y)
} else {
if (missing (y) ) {
y <- t (x)
x <- 1L : NROW (y)
} else {
y <- t (y)
}

<!-- PageNumber="80" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

}

The simulated data follow model (5.10) with $N = 1 0 0 .$ Simulation is performed
using the function pffrSim:

set . seed (9312)
data_ff <- pffrSim(scenario=c ("int", "ff"), n=100)

The kernel $\psi ,$ denoted beta in the original source code of $\mathrm { p f f r S i m } ,$ is hard
coded as follows:

psi_st <- function (s, t) {sxcos (pi * abs (s - t) ) - . 19}
s <- seq (0, 1, length = 40) ; t <- seq(0, 1, length = 60)
psi_true <- outer (s, t, psi_st)

This part of the code must be changed by the user to define a different kernel.
The functions $X _ { i }$ are defined on a grid of 40 equidistant points in $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ the
responses $Y _ { i }$ on a grid of 60 such points. The $X _ { i }$ are generated as linear
combinations of cubic B-splines with random normal coefficients. The values
$\varepsilon _ { i } \left( t _ { j } \right)$ are iid normal (noisy error curves). The function $\alpha$ is defined by 1 +
dbeta (t, 2, 7) (a beta density). These hard coded defaults are easy to
modify. The following code plots Figure 5.4 which shows the curves $X _ { i }$ and
the effect of the action of the integral operator with kernel $\psi .$
par (mfrow=c (1,2) )
fplot (s, data_ff$X1, xlab = "s", ylab = "", main="X (s) ")
\# highlight first three regressor functions X_i
matlines(s, t(data_ff$X1[1:4,]), col=1, lwd=2)
fplot (t, attr (data_ff, "truth") $etaTerms$X1, xlab = "t",
main=expression (integral (psi (t, s) *X (s) * ds)))
matlines (t, t (attr (data_ff, "truth") $etaTerms$X1[1:4, ]), col
=1, lwd=2)

Next, we estimate the model on the simulated data using the function
$\mathrm { p f } \mathrm { f } \mathrm { r } ,$ and plot the estimated surface $\widehat { \psi }$ next to the true surface $\psi .$ The plots
are shown in Figure 5.5.

$m _ { - } f f _ { - } < - p f _ { - } f _ { - } \left( Y \right) \sim f _ { - } f _ { - } \left( X _ { 1 } \right) , d _ { a } t _ { a } = d a t _ { - } a _ { - } f f _ { - }$
psi_plot <- plot (m_ff, select = 2, pers=TRUE) [ [ 2 ] ]
layout (t (1:2))
\# true psi surface
par (mar=c (0, 1, opar$mar [3], 1) )
persp (s, t, psi_true, xlab = "s", ylab = "t", main=expression (
psi (t,s) ), phi=40, theta = 30, ticktype="detailed", zlab =
"", border= NA, col="grey", shade = . 7, zlim = range (
beta_true) )

### 5.5 Estimation based on functional principal compo- nents

In this section, and the following two sections, we consider inference for the
fully functional model based on FPC's. This section is concerned with esti-
mation in model (5.10). The error functions $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are assumed to be iid, square
integrable, mean zero and independent of the regressors $X _ { i } ,$ which are iid with
the same distribution as $\mathrm { a }$ square integrable random function $X$ in $L ^ { 2 } .$ The
concept of square integrable random functions is introduced in Chapter 3, and
elaborated on in Chapter 11.

We first show how to reduce the estimation of the kernel $\psi$ to the case of
the zero intercept function a. Taking the expectations of both sides of (5.10),
we obtain $\mu _ { Y } \left( t \right) = \alpha \left( t \right) + \int \psi \left( t , s \right) \mu _ { X } \left( s \right) d s ,$ which leads to

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) - \mu _ { Y } \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) \left( X _ { i } \left( s \right) - \mu _ { X } \left( s \right) \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

We assume that the population mean functions $\mu _ { Y }$ and $\mu _ { X }$ can be estimated
well enough. In the case of fully observed functions, or functions transformed
to functional objects, e.g by spline smoothing of individual trajectories, we
can use the estimators

$$\widehat { \mu } _ { Y } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } Y _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad \widehat { \mu } _ { X } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

The construction of suitable estimators in the case of sparsely observed func-
tions is described in $C h a p t e r \quad 7 .$ In either case, we replace the curves $X _ { i }$ and
$Y _ { i } ,$ respectively, by $X _ { i } ^ { c } \left( t \right) = X _ { i } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mu } _ { X }$ and $Y _ { i } ^ { c } \left( t \right) = Y _ { i } \left( t \right) - \widehat { \mu } _ { Y }$ and obtain an
estimator $\widehat { \psi }$ in the model

$$Y _ { i } ^ { c } \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) X _ { i } ^ { c } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad i = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N .$$
(5.14)

The intercept function is then estimated by $\widehat { \alpha } \left( t \right) = \widehat { \mu } _ { Y } \left( t \right) - \int \widehat { \psi } \left( t , s \right) \widehat { \mu } _ { X } \left( s \right) d s .$

In the remainder of this section, we therefore assume that the data follow
the population model

$$Y \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) X \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon \left( t \right)$$
(5.15)

and that $E X = 0 ,$ which implies $E Y = 0 .$ Our objective is to derive an
estimator of the kernel $\psi$ in (5.15). The starting point is to find a suitable
expression for the kernel $\psi .$ Since we assume zero mean functions, we have
the expansions

$$X \left( s \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i } v _ { i } \left( s \right) , \quad Y \left( t \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \zeta _ { j } u _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$
(5.16)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="85" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

where the $v _ { i }$ are the FPC's of $X$ and the $u _ { j }$ the FPC's of $Y ,$ cf. Chapter 3.
Assuming that $\iint \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < \infty ,$ we will show that

$$\psi \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } \zeta _ { k } \right] } { E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } ^ { 2 } \right] } u _ { k } \left( t \right) v _ { \ell } \left( s \right) .$$
(5.17)

Since the functional principal components of a square integrable random func-
tion form a basis in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) ,$ the bivariate functions $\left\{ v _ { i } \left( s \right) u _ { j } \left( t \right) \right. ,$ $0 \leq s$ s,t $t \leq 1 ,$
$\left. i , j \geq 1 \right\}$ form a basis in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \times \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) ,$ $\mathrm { c f } .$ Problem 5.2. Therefore, the
kernel $\psi$ admits the expansion

$$\psi \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { k \ell } u _ { k } \left( t \right) v _ { \ell } \left( s \right) .$$

We must show that $\psi _ { k \ell } = E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } \zeta _ { k } \right] / E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } ^ { 2 } \right] .$ Using (5.15) and (5.16), we get

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \zeta _ { j } u _ { j } \left( t \right) = \int \left( \sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { k \ell } u _ { k } \left( t \right) v _ { \ell } \left( s \right) \right) \left( \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i } v _ { i } \left( s \right) \right) d s + \varepsilon \left( t \right)$$
$$= \sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { k \ell } u _ { k } \left( t \right) \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i } \int v _ { \ell } \left( s \right) v _ { i } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon \left( t \right)$$

The orthonormality of the $v _ { i }$ thus implies that

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \zeta _ { j } u _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { k i } \xi _ { i } u _ { k } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon \left( t \right) .$$

Multiplying both sides by $u _ { \ell } \left( t \right)$ and integrating with respect to $d t ,$ we obtain

$$\zeta _ { \ell } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { \ell i } \xi _ { i } + \int u _ { \ell } \left( t \right) \varepsilon \left( t \right) d t .$$
(5.18)

Since $\xi _ { k } = \langle X , v _ { k } \rangle ,$ it is independent of $\int u _ { \ell } \left( t \right) \varepsilon \left( t \right) d t ,$ and since both have
mean $\mathrm { Z e r o } ,$ we conclude that $E \xi _ { k } \int u _ { \ell } \left( t \right) \varepsilon \left( \widetilde { t } \right) d t = 0 .$ Therefore, since the scores
are uncorrelated,

$$E \left[ \xi _ { k } \zeta _ { \ell } \right] = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \psi _ { \ell i } E \left[ \xi _ { k } \xi _ { i } \right] = \psi _ { \ell k } E \left[ \xi _ { k } ^ { 2 } \right] .$$

This completes the verification of (5.17).

Since $E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } ^ { 2 } \right] = \lambda _ { \ell } ,$ setting $\sigma _ { \ell k } = E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } \zeta _ { k } \right] ,$ relations (5.17) leads to the ex-
pression

$$\psi \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { \sigma _ { \ell k } } { \lambda _ { \ell } } u _ { k } \left( t \right) v _ { \ell } \left( s \right)$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="86" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

and an estimator

$$\widehat { \psi } \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { q } \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { p } \frac { \widehat { \sigma } _ { \ell k } } { \widehat { \lambda } _ { \ell } } \widehat { u } _ { k } \left( t \right) \widehat { v } _ { \ell } \left( s \right) .$$
(5.19)

The specific estimators $\widehat { \sigma } _ { \ell k }$ and $\widehat { \lambda } _ { \ell }$ on the structure of the data. The
estimates $\widehat { \lambda } _ { \ell }$ are generally available as output of any FPC software, either in
the dense or sparse cases. The parameters $\sigma _ { \ell k } = E \left[ \xi _ { \ell } \zeta _ { k } \right]$ can be estimated by

$$\widehat { \sigma } _ { \ell k } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { i } , \widehat { v } _ { \ell } \rangle \langle Y _ { i } , \widehat { u } _ { k } \rangle .$$

where the $X _ { i }$ can be functions observed densely, or their basis expansion ap-
proximations, or reconstructions in case of sparse data. The truncation levels
$p$ and $q$ can be selected using the cumulative percentage variance approach.

We conclude this section by showing how the above estimator can be com-
puted in $\mathrm { R }$ using the fda package. We work with the Canadian weather data.
We first define functional objects which contain curves of average daily pre-
cipitation and average daily temperature at the 35 sites in Canada, and their
attributes.

library (fda) ; data (daily)
precav
<- daily$precav; tempav <- daily$tempav
daytime
<- (1:365) -0.5; dayrange <- c (0,365) ; dayperiod <-
365

The daily log-precipitation curves are noisy, even after averaging over the
years 1960 to 1994, see Figure 2.3, so we smooth them before proceeding with
the computation of the estimator. We also smooth the temperature curves, so
that the responses $Y _ { i }$ (precipitation) and the regressors $X _ { i }$ (temperature) have
the same degree of smoothness. The same degree of smoothness is however not
necessarily required. We use the harmonic acceleration penalty and generalized
cross validation (GEV) to find the smoothing parameter, see Section 2.2.

\#
set up the fourier basis
daybasis = create. fourier.basis (dayrange, 365)
\#
set up the harmonic acceleration operator

Lcoef <- c (0, (2*pi/dayperiod) ^2, 0)
harmaccelLfd <- vec2Lfd (Lcoef, dayrange)
\# Do final smooth
lambda = 1e6; # minimum GCV estimate, corresponding to 12
. 3 df
fdParobj = fdPar (daybasis, harmaccelLfd, lambda)
precfd = smooth. basis (daytime, precav, fdParobj) $fd
tempfd = smooth. basis (argvals=daytime, tempav, fdParobj) $fd

Next, we center the smooth functions and compute the EFPC's, four for tem-
perature and six for precipitation.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="87" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

precfd.c = center.fd(precfd); tempfd.c = center.fd(tempfd)
prec.pca = pca. fd (precfd. c, nharm=6) ; temp.pca = pca.fd(
tempfd.c, nharm=4)

Using the output of pca . fd we can compute the coefficients $\widehat { \sigma } _ { \ell k } / \widehat { \lambda } _ { \ell }$ in (5.19).

sigmas = t (temp. pca$scores) %*%prec . pca$scores
cs = diag (1/temp . pca$values [1:4]) % *%sigmas

Now we translate this into a bivariate functional data object, in terms of the
basis expansions used to express the eigenfunctions (called harmonics), and
create a bivariate functional data object

beta. coefs = temp. pca$harmonics$coefs%*%cs%*ot (prec . pca$
harmonics$coefs)
beta.bifd = bifd (beta.coefs, sbasisobj = temp.pca$harmonics$
basis,
tbasisobj = prec.pca$harmonics$basis)

Problem 5.13 explains how to plot the function beta.bifd.


### 5.6 Test of no effect

In this section, we further illustrate inference based on FPC's expansions by
deriving a test of $\psi = 0 ,$ where $\psi$ is the regression kernel in model (5.15). The
testing problem thus is

$$H _ { 0 } : \psi \left( \cdot , \cdot \right) = 0 , \quad \mathrm { v s . } \quad H _ { A } : \psi \left( \cdot , \cdot \right) \neq 0 .$$

The function $\psi$ is treated as an element of $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \times \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) ,$ so $H _ { 0 }$ is equivalent
to $\int \int \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s = 0$ or, equivalently $\psi \left( t , s \right) = 0$ for almost all $t$ t, $s \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ We
assume that the $\left( Y _ { i } , X _ { i } , \varepsilon _ { i } \right)$ are independent copies of $\left( Y , X , \varepsilon \right) ,$ the $\left\{ \varepsilon _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\}$
are independent of the $\left\{ X _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\} ,$ and all random functions are square inte-
grable.

Assume that model (5.15) holds and consider the following operators:

$$C \left( x \right) = E \left[ \langle X , x \rangle X \right] , \quad \Gamma \left( x \right) = E \left[ \langle Y , x \rangle Y \right] , \quad \Delta \left( x \right) = E \left[ \langle X , x \rangle Y \right] .$$

The covariance operators $C$ and $\Gamma$ have nonnegative, summable eigenvalues
defined by

$$C \left( v _ { i } \right) = \lambda _ { i } v _ { i } , \quad \Gamma \left( u _ { j } \right) = \gamma _ { j } u _ { j } .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="88" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Denote by $\widehat { C } ,$ $\widehat { \Gamma }$ and $\widehat { \Delta }$ the sample counterparts of $C ,$ $\Gamma$ and $\Delta$ defined by

$$\widehat { C } \left( x \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { n } , x \rangle X _ { n } ,$$
$$\widehat { \Gamma } \left( x \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle Y _ { n } , x \rangle Y _ { n } ,$$
$$\widehat { \Delta } \left( x \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { n } , x \rangle Y _ { n } .$$

The eigenvalue/eigenfunction pairs of the $\widehat { C }$ are denoted by $\widehat { \lambda } _ { i } ,$ $\widehat { v } _ { i } ,$ and those
of $\widehat { \Delta }$ by $\widehat { \gamma } _ { j } ,$ $\widehat { u } _ { j } .$

The test exploits the interpretation of the EFPC's as an orthonormal sys-
tem that best approximates a set of functions, cf. Section 12.2 and Section 11.4.
The test is also based on the relation

$$\lambda _ { i } \Psi \left( v _ { i } \right) = \Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) ,$$
(5.20)

where $\Psi$ is the integral operator with kernel $\psi \left( \cdot , \cdot \right) ,$ see $\mathrm { P r o b l e m } 5 . 4 .$

By (5.16), $\Psi \left( X \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i } \Psi \left( v _ { i } \right) .$ The sum involves only the indexes $i$ such
☒
that $\lambda _ { i } = E \xi _ { i } ^ { 2 } > 0 ,$ so by $\left( 5 . 2 0 \right) ,$ $\Psi \left( X \right) = 0$ if and only if $\Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) = 0$ for every
☒
$i$ such that $\lambda _ { i } > 0 .$ Since the $u _ { j } ,$ $j \geq 1 ,$ form a basis, $H _ { 0 }$ is thus equivalent
to the condition $\langle \Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) , u _ { j } \rangle = 0$ for any $i ,$ $j \geq 1$ 1. We cannot $t e s t$ an infinite
number of conditions, so we test

$$\langle \Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) , u _ { j } \rangle = 0 , \mathrm { f o r } 1 \leq i \leq p , 1 \leq j \leq q ,$$
(5.21)

where $p$ and $q$ are such that the span of $v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 } , \ldots , v _ { p }$ approximates the support
of $X$ sufficiently well, and the span of $u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { q }$ approximates the support
☒
of $Y$ sufficiently well. We thus reduce $\Psi \left( X \right) = 0$ to $\Psi \left( v _ { i } \right) = 0$ i.e., by (5.20),
☒
to $\Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) = 0 ,$ for $i = 1 , 2 , \ldots , p .$ Note that

$$\Delta \left( v _ { i } \right) \approx \widehat { \Delta } \left( v _ { i } \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { n } , v _ { i } \rangle Y _ { n } ,$$

so the functions $\Delta \left( v _ { i } \right)$ are well approximated by the span of the functions
$Y _ { 1 } , Y _ { 2 } , \ldots Y _ { N } ,$ which, in turn, is well approximated by the span of the FPC's
$u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { q } .$

A suitable test statistic is

$$\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right) = N \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { q } \widehat { \lambda } _ { k } ^ { - 1 } \widehat { \gamma } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle \widehat { \Delta } \left( \widehat { v } _ { k } \right) , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } .$$
(5.22)

It can be shown that if $H _ { 0 }$ is true, then $\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right)$ converges to the chi-square
distribution with $p q$ degrees of freedom, see Problem 5.8. The following algo-
rithm summarizes the test procedure.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="89" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

ALGORITHM 5.6.1 [Chi-square test based on the statistic $\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right)$

1\. Select the number of important FPC's, $p$ and $q ,$ using the scree plot $\mathrm { a n d } / \mathrm { o r }$
the cumulative variance method.

2\. Center the $X _ { n }$ and the $Y _ { n } ,$ i.e. replace them by the $X _ { n } ^ { c }$ and the $Y _ { n } ^ { c }$ defined
in Section 5.5.

3\. Compute the test statistics $\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right)$ (5.22). Note that

$$\langle \widehat { \Delta } \left( \widehat { v } _ { k } \right) , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle = \langle \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { n } , \widehat { v } _ { k } \rangle Y _ { n } , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \langle X _ { n } , \widehat { v } _ { k } \rangle \langle Y _ { n } , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle ,$$

where $\langle X _ { n } , \widehat { v } _ { k } \rangle$ is the kth score of the $X _ { n } ,$ and $\langle Y _ { n } , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle$ is jth score of the
$Y _ { n } .$ These scores and the eigenvalues $\widehat { \lambda } _ { k }$ and $\widehat { \gamma } _ { j }$ are available as output of
suitable $R$ functions.

4\. If $\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right) > \chi _ { p a } ^ { 2 } \left( \alpha \right) ,$ reject the null hypothesis of no linear effect. The
critical value $\left. \chi _ { p q } ^ { 2 } \dot { \left( \right. } \alpha \right)$ is the $\left( 1 - \alpha \right)$ quantile of the chi-squared distribution
with $p q$ degrees of freedom.


### 5.7 Verification of the validity of a functional linear model

Before using a functional linear regression model, its approximate validity
should be checked. This can be done in a similar way as the verification that
a straight line regression, $y _ { i } = a + b x _ { i } + \varepsilon _ { i } ,$ is suitable to describe the de-
pendence between scalar responses $y _ { i }$ and scalar regressors $x _ { i }$ ¿. In this simple
case, the most common approach is to examine the scatter plot of the pairs
$\left( x _ { i } , y _ { i } \right) ,$ $1 \leq i \leq N .$ This plot should not exhibit any obvious nonlinear pat-
tern of the dependence of the $y _ { i }$ on the $x _ { i }$ the points should scatter along a
line. If the pairs $\left( x _ { i } , y _ { i } \right)$ follow a jointly normal distribution, the plot should
be football shaped, with one of the axes being a line with slope b. This ap-
proach carries over to the functional linear models considered in this chapter
and in Chapter 4. Basically, two extensions are needed: 1) in case of more
than one regressors, scatter plots of responses vs. each of the regressors must
be examined, 2) if responses or regressors are functions, they must be re-
placed by their scores with respect to the estimated FPC's. Extension 1) is
not related to functional data, it is a standard approach valid for the scalar
model (4.1). The functional modification is that functions must be replaced
by vectors of scores. Before justifying this approach, we show how it works.
Consider a non-linear functional regression: $Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = H _ { 2 } \left( X _ { i } \left( t \right) \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$ where
$H _ { 2 } \left( x \right) = x ^ { 2 } - 1 .$ For this model, the plot in the $t o p$ left corner of Figure 5.7

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="90" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

exhibits a quadratic trend. In this model, the responses $Y _ { i }$ and the regressors
$X _ { i }$ are functions. We can produce a large number of the scatter plots of the
scores $\langle Y _ { i } , \widehat { u } _ { k } \rangle$ vs. the scores $\langle X _ { i } , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle$ for various choices of $k = 1 , 2 , \ldots .$ and
$j = 1 , 2 , \ldots .$ In this example, already the plot for $k = 1$ and $j = 1$ exhibits a
non-linear pattern.

We now justify the approach explained above in case of the fully functional
linear model (5.10). The derivation will lead us additional useful insights into
this technique. Consider the FPC expansions already used in Sections 5.5 and
5.6, but include the mean functions for completeness. We thus expand the
response and regressor functions in (5.10) as

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu _ { Y } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \zeta _ { i k } u _ { k } \left( t \right) , \quad X _ { i } \left( s \right) = \mu _ { X } \left( s \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i j } v _ { j } \left( s \right) .$$

This leads to the identity

$$\mu _ { Y } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \zeta _ { i k } u _ { k } \left( t \right) = \alpha \left( t \right) + \int \psi \left( t , s \right) \left( \mu _ { X } \left( s \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i j } v _ { j } \left( s \right) \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right)$$
$$= g \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i j } h _ { j } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$$

where

$$g \left( t \right) = \alpha \left( t \right) + \int \psi \left( t , s \right) \mu _ { X } \left( s \right) d s , \quad h _ { j } \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) v _ { j } \left( s \right) d s .$$

Taking the inner product with $u _ { \ell } ,$ and using the fact that the $u _ { k }$ are orthonor-
mal, we obtain

$$\langle \mu _ { Y } , u _ { \ell } \rangle + \zeta _ { i \ell } = \langle g , u _ { \ell } \rangle + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { i j } \langle h _ { j } , u _ { \ell } \rangle + \langle \varepsilon _ { i } , u _ { \ell } \rangle .$$

Setting $a _ { \ell } = \langle g - \mu _ { Y } , u _ { \ell } \rangle$ , $b _ { \ell j } = \langle h _ { j } , u _ { \ell } \rangle ,$ we rewrite the above relation as

$$\zeta _ { i \ell } = a _ { \ell } + b _ { \ell j } \xi _ { i j } + \eta _ { i , \ell j } ,$$
(5.23)

where, setting $\varepsilon _ { i \ell } = \langle \varepsilon _ { i } , u _ { \ell } \rangle ,$

$$\eta _ { i , \ell j } = \sum _ { j ^ { \prime } \neq j } b _ { \ell j ^ { \prime } } \xi _ { i j ^ { \prime } } + \varepsilon _ { i \ell } .$$

To obtain a visually clearer interpretation of (5.23), we suppress the indexes $\ell$
and $j ;$ in each scatter plot, like each of the four scatter plots in Figure 5.7, we
consider a fixed $\ell$ and a fixed $j .$ Doing so, leads to the relation $\zeta _ { i } = a + b \xi _ { i } + \eta _ { i } ,$
which is a scalar straight line regression equation. The errors $\eta _ { i }$ have mean

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

zero and are uncorrelated with the regressors $\xi _ { i }$ , and uncorrelated with each
other, Problem 5.10. If the functions $X$ and $Y$ are jointly Gaussian, then their
projections, respectively, onto $u _ { \ell }$ and $v _ { j }$ are also jointly Gaussian (Chapter 11
discusses Gaussian random functions in detail). Therefore, if model (5.10) is
true with the $X _ { i }$ and $Y _ { i }$ being replications of jointly Gaussian random func-
tions, then the scatter plots discussed in this section should be roughly football
shaped. Problems 5.11 and 5.12 are concerned with analogous straight line
regressions, respectively, for function-on-scalar and scalar-on-function regres-
sions.


#### 5.8 Extensions and further reading

Morris (2015) provides an extensive review of literature on functional regres-
sion. Section 5.1 is based on Faraway (1997). Formulas (5.6) and (5.9) are
derived in Reiss et al. (2010). Section 5.3 is based on Ivanescu et al. (2015)
who discuss connections to mixed models, provide a comparison to other esti-
mation and prediction methods, and explain how to apply the function pffr
if the curves are irregularly and sparsely observed.

The methods described in Sections 5.3 and 5.4 are substantially extended
in Scheipl $e t$ al. (2015) who consider models of the form $Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu _ { i } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$
As in (5.11), the errors $\varepsilon _ { i }$ are assumed to be iid Gaussian random functions,
but the mean functions $\mu _ { i }$ can depend in a complex way on scalar and func-
tional regressors and categorical variables. To illustrate the kind modeling this
more general framework can accommodate, we use the example of a medical
study considered by the authors. The index $i$ has two components, $\iota$ and K.
The index ¿ refers to a patient, and k to a medical examination visit. The
index $K$ introduces temporal dependence of the regressors; in the previous
sections of this chapter the regressors were treated either as deterministic or
as realizations of iid random vectors or functions. For every patient i, two
categorical variables are considered: $d \left( \iota \right) = 1$ if the patient has a disease in
question, $d \left( \iota \right) = 0$ otherwise; $g \left( \iota \right) = 1$ if the patient is a man, $g \left( \iota \right) = 0$ other-
wise. By $u \left( \iota , \kappa \right)$ we denote the age of patient ¿ at his/her kth visit. The mean
function $\mu _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu _ { \iota \kappa } \left( t \right)$ is assumed to have the following form

$$\mu _ { \iota \kappa } \left( t \right) = \alpha _ { 1 } \left( d \left( \iota \right) , t \right) + \alpha _ { 2 } \left( g \left( \iota \right) , t \right) + \alpha _ { 3 } \left( u \left( \iota , \kappa \right) , t \right)$$
$$+ \int _ { \mathcal{S} } \psi _ { 1 } \left( d \left( \iota \right) , t , s \right) X _ { 1 , \iota \kappa } \left( s \right) d s + \int _ { \mathcal{R} } \psi _ { 2 } \left( d \left( \iota \right) , t , r \right) X _ { 2 , \iota \kappa } \left( r \right) d r .$$

What could be called the intercept structure now has a more complex form
than the single function $\alpha$ in (5.11). We must estimate the four functions
$\alpha _ { 1 } \left( 0 , \cdot \right) , \alpha _ { 1 } \left( 1 , \cdot \right) , \alpha _ { 2 } \left( 0 , \cdot \right) , \alpha _ { 2 } \left( 1 , \cdot \right)$ and the bivariate function $\alpha _ { 3 } .$ There are also
four regression kernels to be estimated. In the application considered by
Scheipl et al. (2015), the regressors $X _ { 1 , \iota \kappa }$ and $X _ { 2 , \iota \kappa }$ are functions obtained

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="93" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

from measurements along two tracts in the human brain. In medical research,
it is stipulated that these functions have the potential to explain the response
functions $Y _ { \iota \kappa }$ constructed from measurements along a different tract, and that
the pattern of dependence is different for patients with the disease.

The estimation approach based on FPC's, Section 5.5, is developed in
the context of sparsely observed functions in Yao et al. (2005) and Yao et
al. (2005b). Section 5.6 is based on Kokoszka et al. (2008) who provide an
application of this test to space physics data, see also Chapter 9 of Horváth and
Kokoszka (2012). The approach to verifying the linearity assumption presented
in Section 5.7 was proposed by Chiou and Müller (2007) who also consider
different types of scatter plots derived from FPC scores. Gabrys et al. (2010)
derived hypothesis tests whose null hypothesis is that the error function $\varepsilon _ { i }$ in
(5.10) are iid, and the alternative that they are correlated.


#### 5.9 Chapter 5 Problems

5.1 Show that for any functions $\varphi _ { 1 } , \varphi _ { 2 } , \ldots , \varphi _ { K } ,$ the $K \times K$ matrix $\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi }$ with
the entries $\varphi _ { k \ell } = \int \varphi _ { k } \left( t \right) \varphi _ { \ell } \left( t \right) d t ,$ $1 \leq k ,$ $\ell \leq K ,$ is nonnegative definite, i.e. for
any real numbers $x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { K } ,$

$$\sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { K } \varphi _ { k \ell } x _ { k } x _ { \ell } \geq 0 .$$

The matrix $\mathrm { I } _ { \varphi }$ is obviously symmetric. It is known, e.g. Seber and Lee (2003),
Appendix $\mathrm { A } ,$ that a symmetric nonnegative definite matrix, say $\mathrm { A } ,$ can be
decomposed as $\mathrm { A } = \mathrm { U } \Lambda \mathrm { U } ^ { T } ,$ where $\Lambda$ is a diagonal matrix with nonneg-
ative entries, and $\mathrm { U }$ satisfies $\mathrm { U } ^ { T } \mathrm { U } = \mathrm { I } .$ Then $\mathrm { A } ^ { 1 / 2 } = \mathrm { U } ^ { T } \Lambda ^ { 1 / 2 } \mathrm { U }$ satisfies
$\mathrm { A } ^ { 1 / 2 } \mathrm { A } ^ { 1 / 2 } = \mathrm { A } .$ Many software packages compute the matrix $\mathrm { A } ^ { 1 / 2 } .$

5.2 Show that if $\left\{ u _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ and $\left\{ v _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\}$ are bases in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right)$ (not nec-
essarily orthonormal), then

$$\left\{ v _ { i } \left( s \right) u _ { i } \left( t \right) , 0 \leq s , t \leq 1 , i , j \geq 1 \right\}$$
(5.24)

is a basis in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \times \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) .$ Show that if $\left\{ u _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ and $\left\{ v _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\}$ are
both orthonormal systems, then (5.24) is an orthonormal system as well.

5.3 Consider estimator (5.19). We know that the EFPC's $\widehat { u } _ { k }$ and $\widehat { \upsilon } _ { \ell }$ are
determined only up to a sign. Show that if $\widehat { u } _ { k }$ is replaced by $d _ { k } \widehat { u } _ { k }$ and $\widehat { v } _ { \ell }$ by
$c _ { \ell } \widehat { v } _ { \ell } ,$ with $d _ { k } ^ { 2 } = 1$ $c _ { \rho } ^ { 2 } = 1 ,$ then the estimator $\widehat { \psi } \left( t , s \right)$ does not change. Show
that the statistic (5.22) is also invariant to such a substitution.

5.4 Assuming model (5.15) holds and $X$ is independent of E, verify identity
(5.20).

In Problems 5.5, 5.6 and 5.7, which require background of Chapter 11, assume
that the null hypothesis of Section 5.6 holds and the following two assumptions
hold as well.

ASSUMPTION 5.9.1 The triples $\left( Y _ { n } , X _ { n } , \varepsilon _ { n } \right)$ form a sequence of independent
identically distributed random elements such that $\varepsilon _ { n }$ is independent of $X _ { n }$ and

$$E X _ { n } = 0 \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad E \varepsilon _ { n } = 0 ;$$
$$E \| X _ { n } \| ^ { 4 } < \infty \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad E \| \varepsilon _ { n } \| ^ { 4 } < \infty .$$
(5.25)
(5.26)

ASSUMPTION 5.9.2 The eigenvalues of the operators $C$ and $\Gamma$ satisfy, for
some $p > 0$ and $q > 0 ,$ $\lambda _ { 1 } > \lambda _ { 2 } > \ldots \lambda _ { p } > \lambda _ { p + 1 } ,$ $\gamma _ { 1 } > \gamma _ { 2 } > \ldots \gamma _ { q } > \gamma _ { q + 1 } .$

5.5 Establish the following joint convergence:

$$\sqrt { N } \left\{ \langle \widehat { \Delta } \left( v _ { k } \right) , u _ { j } \rangle , 1 \leq j \leq q , 1 \leq k \leq p \right\}$$
$$\stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \left\{ \eta _ { k j } \sqrt { \lambda _ { k } \gamma _ { j } } , 1 \leq j \leq q , 1 \leq k \leq p \right\} ,$$
(5.27)

with $\eta _ { k j } \sim N \left( 0 , 1 \right)$ such that $\eta _ { k j }$ and $\eta _ { k ^ { \prime } j ^ { \prime } }$ are independent if $\left( k , j \right) \neq \left( k ^ { \prime } , j ^ { \prime } \right) .$

5.6 Verify that $E \| \widehat { \Delta } \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = N ^ { - 1 } E \| X \| ^ { 2 } E \| \varepsilon _ { 1 } \| ^ { 2 } .$

5.7 Show that

$$\sqrt { N } \left\{ \langle \widehat { \Delta } \left( \widehat { v } _ { k } \right) , \widehat { u } _ { j } \rangle , 1 \leq j \leq q , 1 \leq k \leq p \right\}$$
$$\stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \left\{ \eta _ { k j } \sqrt { \lambda _ { k } \gamma _ { j } } , 1 \leq j \leq q , 1 \leq k \leq p \right\}$$
(5.28)

with $\eta _ { k j }$ equal to those in Problem 5.5.

5.8 Using the results in Problems 5.5, 5.6 and 5.7 show that if the null
hypothesis of Section 5.6 holds, then, under Assumptions 5.9.1 and 5.9.2,
$\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \chi _ { p q } ^ { 2 } ,$ as $N \rightarrow \infty .$

5.9 This problem establishes the consistency of the asymptotic test based
on statistic (5.22). Show that if Assumptions 5.9.1 and 5.9.2 hold, and
$\langle \Psi \left( v _ { k } \right) , u _ { j } \rangle \neq 0$ for some $k \leq p$ and $j \leq q ,$ then $\widehat { T } _ { N } \left( p , q \right) \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } \infty ,$ as $N \rightarrow \infty .$

5.10 Consider the errors in (5.23) for fixed $\ell$ and $j ,$ i.e. $\eta _ { i } = \sum _ { j ^ { \prime } \neq j } b _ { \ell j ^ { \prime } } \xi _ { i j ^ { \prime } } +$
$\varepsilon _ { i \ell } .$ Assume that the pairs $\left( X _ { i } , \varepsilon _ { i } \right)$ are id and $\left\{ \varepsilon _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\}$ independent of
$\left\{ X _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\} .$ Show that

$$E \eta _ { i } = 0 , E \left[ \eta _ { i } \xi _ { i j } \right] = 0 , E \left[ \eta _ { i } \eta _ { m } \right] = 0 , i \neq m .$$

Show that $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \eta _ { i } \right] = \sum _ { j ^ { \prime } \neq j } b _ { \ell j ^ { \prime } } ^ { 2 } \lambda _ { j ^ { \prime } } + E \varepsilon _ { i \ell } ^ { 2 } ,$ where $\lambda _ { j ^ { \prime } }$ is the j'th eigenvalue of
the covariance operator of $X .$

5.13 At the time of writing of this book, the fda package does not pro-
vide a custom function for plotting bivariate functional data objects .bifd.
However plotting them is easy by first extracting the values with the function
eval.bifd followed by the application of the plotting commands used in

5.14 This problem explains the application of the function $\mathrm { f o s } \mathrm { r } . \mathrm { p e r m }$ in
the refund package. Consider model (5.1). For $m < q ,$ we wish to test

$$H _ { 0 } : \beta _ { m + 1 } \left( t \right) = \ldots = \beta _ { q } \left( t \right) = 0 , \quad \forall t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$$
(5.29)

For each t, we compute the F-statistic given by (4.8) (with $p =$ q), we de-
note its value by $\widehat { F } \left( t \right) .$ The estimators $\widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( q \right) } \left( t \right)$ and $\widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( m \right) } \left( t \right)$ generally involve a
penalty and so depend all values $Y _ { i } \left( s \right) ,$ $s \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ Therefore, even for a fixed
t, $\widehat { \beta } _ { j } ^ { \left( q \right) }$ will not have an F-distribution. Moreover, the null hypothesis (5.29)
postulates that the functions are zero.

The following approach to testing (5.29) has been used. Consider the test
statistic

$$\widehat { F } _ { \sup } = \sup _ { t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] } \widehat { F } \left( t \right) .$$

The distribution of $\widehat { F } _ { \sup }$ under Ho is unknown, so it is approximated as follows.
For each of the $N !$ permutations, T, of $\left\{ 1 , 2 , \ldots , N \right\}$ consider the permuted
data set

$$\mathcal{D} _ { \pi } = \left( Y _ { \pi \left( i \right) } \left( t \right) , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] , x _ { i 1 } , \ldots x _ { i q } \right) .$$

Using $\mathcal{D} _ { \pi } ,$ we compute the $\widehat { \beta } _ { \pi , j } ^ { \left( m \right) } \left( t \right) , j = 1 , 2 , \ldots , m$ and the $\widehat { \beta } _ { \pi , j } ^ { \left( q \right) } \left( t \right) , j =$
$1 , 2 , \ldots , q ,$ and the statistic $\widehat { F } _ { \sup } ^ { \pi } .$ The $N !$ values $\widehat { F } _ { \sup } ^ { \pi }$ are arranged from the
smallest to the largest, and their 95th percentile is denoted by $c _ { 0 . 9 5 } \left( N \right) .$ The
null hypothesis (5.29) is rejected if $\widehat { F } _ { \sup } > c _ { 0 . 9 5 } \left( N \right) .$ The justification of this
approach (for vector regression) is provided, for example, in Manly (1991),
pp. 156-162.

The function fosr. perm produces the output similar to the one shown
in Figure 5.8. The continuous blue curve is the function $t \mapsto \widehat { F } \left( t \right) .$ The dashed
red line is the critical level $c _ { 0 . 9 5 } \left( N \right) .$ The gray lines are the $N !$ functions
$t \mapsto \widehat { F } ^ { \pi } \left( t \right) .$ If the maximum of the continuous blue curve is above the dashed
critical level, we reject $H _ { 0 } .$

<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Use the following code, adapted from the help file, to assess the significance
of the effects of the climatic regions on the annual temperature curves in
Canada, cf. Figure 5.3. Clearly formulate the null and alternative hypotheses.
Explain what all arguments of fosr . perm are.

require (fda) ; require (refund)
smallbasis <- create. fourier. basis (c (0, 365), 25)
tempfd <- smooth. basis (day. 5, CanadianWeather$dailyAv [,,"
Temperature. C"], smallbasis) $fd
Xreg = cbind (1, model.matrix (~factor (CanadianWeather
$region) -1) )
conreg = matrix (c (0,1,1,1,1), 1) # constrain region effects
to sum to 0

regionperm = fosr. perm (fdobj=tempfd, $\mathrm { X } = \mathrm { X } \mathrm { r } \mathrm { e } \mathrm { q } ,$ con=conreg,
method="OLS", nperm=200, prelim=30)

5.15 This problem further explains the idea of a permutation test using a
two sample testing problem. It is based on Section 10.5.1 of Ramsay et al.
(2009).

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="97" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional response models" -->

We observe two samples of curves defined on the same interval:
$X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots X _ { N }$ and $X _ { N + 1 } , X _ { N + 2 } , \ldots X _ { N + M } .$ The first $N$ curves are iid draws
from a population with mean function $\mu _ { 1 } ,$ the last $M$ curves are iid draws
from a population with mean function $\mu _ { 2 } .$ We want to test

$$H _ { 0 } : \mu _ { 1 } \left( t \right) = \mu _ { 2 } \left( t \right) , \quad \forall \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$$

We want to use the test statistics

$$\widehat { T } _ { \sup } = \sup _ { t \in \left[ 0 , t \right] } \widehat { T } \left( t \right) , \quad \widehat { T } \left( t \right) = \frac { | \bar { X } _ { N } \left( t \right) - \bar { X } _ { M } \left( t \right) | } { \left\{ N ^ { - 1 } \widehat { V } _ { N } \left( t \right) + M ^ { - 1 } \widehat { V } _ { M } \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } } ,$$

where

$$\bar { X } _ { N } \left( t \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad \bar { X } _ { M } \left( t \right) = \frac { 1 } { M } \sum _ { i = N + 1 } ^ { N + M } X _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$$

and where $\widehat { V } _ { N } \left( t \right)$ and $\widehat { V } _ { M } \left( t \right)$ are the corresponding sample variances.

The null hypothesis is rejected if $\widehat { T } _ { \sup }$ is large, i.e. if the normalized dif-
ference between the sample mean functions is large for some t. To compute
exact critical values, we would need to know the distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { \sup }$ under $H _ { 0 }$
This distribution is approximated as follows. Denote by $\pi$ one of the $\left( N + M \right) !$
permutations of the index set $\left\{ 1 , \ldots , N , N + 1 , \ldots , N + M \right\} .$ If $H _ { 0 }$ is true, the
two samples

$$X _ { \pi \left( 1 \right) } , \ldots , X _ { \pi \left( N \right) } \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad X _ { \pi \left( N + 1 \right) } , \ldots , X _ { \pi \left( N + M \right) }$$

have the same mean. The empirical distribution (histogram) of the $\left( N + M \right) !$
values of $\widehat { T } _ { \mathrm { s u p } } ^ { \pi }$ is used as an approximation to the null distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { \sup } .$ (In
numerical implementations, not all $\left( N + M \right) !$ values are used, as this number
can be very large; a random sample of them is used.)

Using the help file of the function $\mathrm { t p e r m } .$ fd in the fda package, and the
Berkeley growth data, test if the mean growth curves for boys and girls are
the same. Obtain a graph analogous to Figure 5.8 and interpret it.

5.16 Perform the test described in Problem 5.14 using the function
$\mathrm { F p e r m } .$ $f d$ in the fda package.

5.17 [Concurrent functional model] Dynamic biometric measurements moti-
vated the early development of functional data analysis. A classical example,
explained in some detail in Section 10.2.3. of Ramsay $e t$ al. (2009), is the gait
data. Figure 5.9 shows suitably defined angles made at matching times by
knees and hips of 39 boys. A question of interest is the extent to which the
hip angle can explain the knee angle.

A model proposed to study this question takes the form

$$Y _ { i } \left( t \right) = \beta _ { 0 } \left( t \right) + \beta _ { 1 } \left( t \right) X _ { i } \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="98" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 5.9: The curves show suitably defined knee and hip angles for a sample of children.</figcaption>
