All plots show roughly the same pattern: a significant increase in VAS as

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="132" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

the study progresses, but the increase levels off. This is likely due to the fact
that all subjects were assigned to some treatment, i.e. there was no control
group. Furthermore, the subjects had not previously received any treatment
for their AMD. Thus it is reasonable to expect an increase in visual acuity
as the study progresses, with a leveling-off period after the treatments have
reached their potential.

One potential point of concern is the baseline measurement. This mea-
surement is made before applying any treatment, and as we move to the first
observation which includes a treatment, we see a sharp jump. Subsequent in-
creases seem to be substantially smaller, and there is a clear leveling off. Thus,
there is a very real concern that there is a discontinuity at the beginning, or at
the very least, a very rapid increase. Such artifacts can make choosing tuning
parameters difficult, as different parameter values might produce estimates
which fit well in certain regions/domains, but not others. As part of Problem
7.8, the reader is asked to explore a potential fix to this problem by omitting
the baseline measurement, and instead subtracting it from each subsequent
observation.


### 7.3 Covariance function estimation

Covariance estimation can be viewed as a bivariate version of mean estimation,
at least in its implementation. Indeed, all of the discussed smoothing tools can
be used for covariance function estimation as well. There are, however, a few
differences between estimating a covariance function vs estimating a bivariate
mean. First, the covariance function is positive definite, a property which is
not a priori guaranteed to hold for smoothing estimates. Second, the data are
assumed to be contaminated with independent noise, this means that one has
to be very careful when estimating the diagonal of the covariance function.

As mentioned, there are two components contributing to the variance of
the $Y _ { n m } ,$ the first is the subject specific error function $\varepsilon _ { n } ,$ which is assumed
to be relatively smooth. We will denote the covariance function of $\varepsilon _ { n } \left( t \right)$ as
$c \left( t , s \right) .$ The second is structural or measurement noise $\delta _ { n m } .$ We will denote the
variance of $\delta _ { n m }$ as $\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { n m } \right) ,$ where $\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ is a continuous function. While we
allow the variance of the structural noise to vary with time, we still assume
that it is independent across both $n$ and $m .$ For many dense settings, this
measurement noise is either negligible or can be smoothed out. For many
longitudinal settings, this is not the case as the noise can be substantial. The
covariance function of $Y$ therefore has a discontinuity on the diagonal:

$$\mathrm { C o v } \left( Y \left( t \right) , Y \left( s \right) \right) = c \left( t , s \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) 1 _ { t = s } .$$

To estimate $c \left( t , s \right)$ we can adapt the methodology presented in Section 7.2,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="133" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Sparse FDA" -->

and use a bivariate smoother on the pairs

$$\widetilde { Y } _ { n m _ { 1 } } \widetilde { Y } _ { n m _ { 2 } } = \left( Y _ { n m _ { 1 } } - \widehat { \mu } \left( t _ { n m _ { 1 } } \right) \right) \left( Y _ { n m _ { 2 } } - \widehat { \mu } \left( t _ { n m _ { 2 } } \right) \right) ,$$

with bivariate arguments $\left( t _ { n m _ { 1 } } , t _ { n m _ { 2 } } \right) .$ However, the discontinuity on the di-
agonal means that this must be done with care. The common approach is to
remove the diagonal terms, and use only the off diagonal terms to estimate the
covariance surface. In this case, one would create a large vector by multiplying
observations

$$\widetilde { \mathrm { Y } } = \left\{ \widetilde { Y } _ { n m _ { 1 } } \widetilde { Y } _ { n m _ { 2 } } : 1 \leq n \leq N , 1 \leq m _ { 1 } \leq M _ { n } , 1 \leq m _ { 2 } \leq M _ { n } , m _ { 1 } \neq m _ { 2 } . \right.$$

and then a large matrix with two columns for the time points

$$\mathrm { T } = \left\{ \left( t _ { n m _ { 1 } } , t _ { n m _ { 2 } } \right) : 1 \leq n \leq N , 1 \leq m _ { 1 } \leq M _ { n } , 1 \leq m _ { 2 } \leq M _ { n } , m _ { 1 } \neq m _ { 2 } \right\} .$$

We can then use any of the discussed smoothers to estimate the covariance
surface. However, two properties must be kept in mind: symmetry and positive
definiteness. The first property is usually not a problem as most smoothers
will produce a symmetric estimate when used for covariance estimation. The
second is more of an issue and it is common to end up with an estimate which
is not positive definite. This is because there is nothing inherent in any of the
smoothers which will guarantee a positive definite estimate. To remedy this,
one usually expands the estimate using an eigenvalue/function decomposition
and then sets any of the negative eigenvalues to zero (Problem 7.14).

Once $c \left( t , s \right)$ has been estimated, one can now estimate $\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ by running
one of the univariate smoothers from Section 7.2 on the diagonal terms only,
call it $\widetilde { c } \left( t , t \right) ,$ and then forming the difference with $\widehat { c } \left( t , s \right) :$

$$\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) = \widetilde { c } \left( t , t \right) - \widehat { c } \left( t , t \right) .$$
(7.12)

Or, equivalently, one could form the differences $\widetilde { Y } _ { n m } \widetilde { Y } _ { n m } - \widehat { c } \left( t _ { n m } , t _ { n m } \right)$ and
run a univariate smoother through them. If the resulting $\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ is negative for
any $t ,$ then these values can be set to zero (since one cannot have a negative
variance) or smoothers that incorporate positivity can be employed (Ramsay
and Silverman, 2002, Section 6.2), though we do not explore this further. If
$\sigma \left( t \right) \equiv \sigma ^ { 2 }$ is assumed to be constant, then it can be estimated more simply as

$$\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \frac { 1 } { M _ { n } } \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M _ { n } } \left( \widetilde { Y } _ { n m } \widetilde { Y } _ { n m } - \widehat { c } \left( t _ { n m } , t _ { n m } \right) \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

To illustrate these points, we return to the CATT data. In Figure 7.5 we
plot the estimated covariance surface using gam. The left panel chose the tun-
ing parameter using CV, while the right panel increases the tuning parameter
slightly after a visual inspection. The plot indicates a similar pattern to the
mean, the variability rapidly increases from baseline, peaking at $s = 1$ and

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="134" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 7.5: Estimated covariance function of CATT data using a spline basis. Left panel uses a tuning a parameter chosen with cross-validation. The right panel increases the left's tuning parameter to produce a slightly smoother estimate.</figcaption>

C(t,s)

C(t,s)

t

00

t

5

</figure>


$t =$ 1, but then levels off. Interestingly, the covariance seems to drop off pretty
substantially as one moves away from the diagonal.

In Figure 7.6 we illustrate the effect of "contamination", i.e. the $\delta _ { n m }$ from
(7.1), on the estimation of the diagonal $c \left( t , t \right) .$ First we take the gam estimates
of $\widehat { c } \left( t , s \right)$ from Figure 7.5 and plot their diagonals using a red dashed line (using
CV) and purple (increased slightly from CV). Next we plot, using blue x, the
raw point-wise variances (computed at each time point) which have not been
corrected. We then smooth (again using gam) these raw values to get the
black line, which is then an estimate of $c \left( t , t \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) .$ The difference between
the black line and the red/purple indicates $\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) ,$ and in this case the noise is
quite noticeable. It is because of features like this that the diagonal terms are
omitted when estimating $c \left( t , s \right) .$


### 7.4 Sparse functional $\mathrm { P C A }$

Another key difference between nonparametric smoothing and sparse FDA
occurs after covariance surface has been estimated as now FPCA can be used.
Recall the Karhunen-Loéve expansion:

$$Y _ { n } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { n j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="135" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Sparse FDA" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 7.6: Estimated variance functions of CATT data using a spline basis. Purple and red lines are computed using (7.12) with the same smoothing pa- rameters as in Figure 7.5, and represent estimates of $\widehat { c } \left( t , t \right) .$ The blue $x$ denote raw point-wise variances, and the black line is a smooth estimate $\widehat { c } \left( t , t \right) + \widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ based on those values. The difference between the black and red/purple lines is $\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ and denotes the level of "contamination" in the data.</figcaption>

400

Variance Function

350

$x$

$x$

300

$x \times$

x

x

250

200

0 20 40 60 80 100

Week

</figure>


where $\xi _ { n 1 } , \xi _ { n 2 } , \ldots .$ are mean zero, uncorrelated, and have variances $\lambda _ { 1 } , \lambda _ { 2 } , \ldots .$
The goal of FPCA then is to replace the observed data $\left\{ Y _ { n } \left( t _ { n m } \right) \right\}$ with esti-
mated/predicted principal component scores $\left\{ \widehat { \xi } _ { n j } \right\}$ or smoothed trajectories.
FPCA accomplishes a few things:

1\. irregular data are now on a "common scale" when working with scores,

2\. the scores are typically of a lower dimension (though not necessarily for
very sparse data),

3\. the scores can be used in further multivariate statistical analyses,

4\. the scores and FPC's can be used to reconstruct individual trajectories.

Estimating the eigenvalues and eigenfunctions is done as before, a spectral
decomposition is applied to $\widehat { c } \left( t , s \right)$ (see Section 12.2). However, obtaining the
scores must now be done with more care. Whereas for densely observed curves
we could form inner products, such an approach cannot be applied to sparse
data. In the case of densely observed curves, step 4 is interpreted as smoothing;
the curves $\widehat { X } _ { n } ^ { \left( p \right) } \left( t \right) : = \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \xi } _ { n j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right)$ are smoother than the observed
curves, and so may be closer to the underlying unobserved curves which are
postulated to be smooth. In case of sparse data, the curve $\widehat { X } _ { n } ^ { \left( p \right) }$ is, in addition,
a remarkable reconstruction of a a curve $X _ { n }$ which is postulated to exist at
any $t ,$ and be smooth, but which is observed only at a handful of time points.

To explain the approach used for sparse functional data, let us work at the
population level. Assume that $\mu \left( t \right) = 0$ and both $\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t \right)$ and $c \left( t , s \right)$ are known.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="136" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

We can then ask, what is the conditional distribution of the first score, $\xi _ { 1 } ,$
given the observations $Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) , \ldots , Y \left( t _ { M } \right)$ If the data are Gaussian, then the
joint distribution of $\left( \xi _ { 1 } , Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) , \ldots , Y \left( t _ { M } \right) \right) ^ { \top }$ multivariate normal. The mean
vector is zero, and the covariance between the score, $\xi _ { 1 } ,$ and the observation
$Y \left( t _ { m } \right)$ is given by

$$\mathrm { C o v } \left( \xi _ { 1 } , Y \left( t _ { m } \right) \right) = \mathrm { E } \left[ \left( \int Y \left( t \right) v _ { 1 } \left( t \right) d t \right) Y \left( t _ { m } \right) \right]$$
$$= \int c \left( t , t _ { m } \right) v _ { 1 } \left( t \right) d t = \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { m } \right) .$$

The covariance matrix of $\left( \xi _ { 1 } , Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) , \ldots , Y \left( t _ { M } \right) \right) ^ { \top }$ is then given by

$$\Xi = \left[ \begin{array}{} \lambda _ { 1 } & \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & \ldots & \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { M } \right) \\ \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 1 } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & \ldots & c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { M } \right) \\ \vdots & & \ddots & \ldots & \vdots \\ \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { M } \right) & \ldots & \ldots & c \left( t _ { M } , t _ { M } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] .$$

Since the observations are jointly normal, this completely defines their joint
distribution. We can now obtain the best linear unbiased predictor of $\xi _ { 1 }$ by
using its conditional expectation. Recall that if two vectors $\mathrm { Z } _ { 1 } ,$ $\mathrm { Z } _ { 2 }$ are jointly
normal, then $\mathrm { Z } _ { 1 } | \mathrm { Z } _ { 2 }$ is normal with mean

$$\mathrm { E } \left[ \mathrm { Z } _ { 1 } | \mathrm { Z } _ { 2 } \right] = \mu _ { 1 } + \Sigma _ { 1 2 } \Sigma _ { 2 2 } ^ { - 1 } \left( \mathrm { Z } _ { 2 } - \mu _ { 2 } \right)$$

and covariance matrix

$$\mathrm { C o v } \left( \mathrm { Z } _ { 1 } | \mathrm { Z } _ { 2 } \right) = \Sigma _ { 1 1 } - \Sigma _ { 1 2 } \Sigma _ { 2 2 } ^ { - 1 } \Sigma _ { 2 1 } .$$

This implies that $\xi _ { 1 } | Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) , \ldots , Y \left( t _ { M } \right)$ is normal with mean

$$\left[ \begin{array}{} \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) \\ \vdots \\ \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] ^ { \top } \left[ \begin{array}{} c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 1 } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & \ldots & c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { M } \right) \\ \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\ c \left( t _ { M } , t _ { 1 } \right) & \cdots & c \left( t _ { M } , t _ { M } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] ^ { - 1 } \left[ \begin{array}{} Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) \\ \vdots \\ Y \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] ,$$

and variance

11 -
A101(tM)
X101(t1)

T

.
c(t1,t1) +o2(t1)
.

. ..

X101(t1)
.

From normal theory, the conditional expectation, call it $\widehat { \xi } _ { 1 } ,$ is the best linear
unbiased predictor (BLUP), and thus we use it to predict $\xi _ { 1 } .$ We can pre-
dict $\xi _ { 1 } , \ldots , \xi _ { p }$ by adding more columns into the first term in the conditional

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="137" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Sparse FDA" -->

expectation:

$$\left[ \begin{array}{} \widehat { \xi } _ { 1 } \\ \vdots \\ \widehat { \xi } _ { p } \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{} \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & \cdots & \lambda _ { p } v _ { p } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) \\ \vdots & \cdots & \vdots \\ \lambda _ { 1 } v _ { 1 } \left( t _ { M } \right) & \cdots & \lambda _ { p } v _ { p } \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] ^ { \top }$$
$$\times \left[ \begin{array}{} c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 1 } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { 1 } \right) & \ldots & c \left( t _ { 1 } , t _ { M } \right) \\ \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\ c \left( t _ { M } , t _ { 1 } \right) & \ldots & c \left( t _ { M } , t _ { M } \right) + \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] ^ { - 1 } \left[ \begin{array}{} Y \left( t _ { 1 } \right) \\ \vdots \\ Y \left( t _ { M } \right) \end{array} \right] .$$ ☒

Turning to the sample versions, one replaces the various parameters with
their estimates, and we end up with $\left\{ \widehat { \xi } _ { n j } : 1 \leq n \leq N , 1 \leq j \leq p \right\} .$ The
trajectories can now be reconstructed using

$$\widehat { Y } _ { n } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \xi } _ { n j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$

though one can also reconstruct $Y _ { n }$ without using FPCA, Problem 7.15.


### 7.5 Sparse functional regression

In this section we will focus on using the basis function methods of Section
7.2 to carry out functional regression. The methodology we use here is based
on FPCA, though other methods are possible. In particular, functions $\mathrm { p f r }$
and $\mathrm { p f } \mathrm { f } \mathrm { r }$ in the $\mathrm { r e f u n d }$ package can be used as well, see Section 6.4 for
more details. In any of the settings, function-on-scalar, scalar-on-function,
or function-on-function, we will carry out FPCA on any of the functional
objects. Once converted to multivariate objects, we then carry out multivariate
regression. Recall that Chapters 4 and 5 deal with functional regression for
densely observed functions.

For function-on-scalar regression, as in Chapter 5, we assume that

$$Y _ { n } \left( t \right) = X _ { n } \beta \left( t \right) + \varepsilon _ { n } \left( t \right) .$$

We assume that both $X _ { n }$ and $Y _ { n }$ have been centered so that no intercept is
required in the model. As before, we observe each function $Y _ { n }$ at time points
$t _ { n 1 } , \ldots , t _ { n M _ { n } } .$ We carry out FPCA on the $Y _ { n } \left( t _ { n m } \right)$ to obtain an $n \times q$ matrix
of scores $\xi ,$ and estimated FPCs $\widehat { u } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$ We expand $\beta \left( t \right) \approx \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \beta _ { j } \widehat { u } _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$ and
then use the least squares estimate

$$\widehat { \beta } = \left( \begin{array}{} \widehat { \beta } _ { 1 } \\ \vdots \\ \widehat { \beta } _ { p } \end{array} \right) = \frac { 1 } { \sum X _ { n } ^ { 2 } } \left( \begin{array}{} \sum X _ { n } \xi _ { n 1 } \\ \vdots \\ \sum X _ { n } \xi _ { n q } \end{array} \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="138" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

The estimate, $\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) ,$ is then given by

$$\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } \widehat { u } _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { q } \frac { \sum X _ { n } \xi _ { n j } } { \sum X _ { n } ^ { 2 } } \widehat { u } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

In the scalar-on-function case, as in Chapter 4, the model is given by

$$Y _ { n } = \int \beta \left( t \right) X _ { n } \left( t \right) d t + \varepsilon _ { n } .$$

We instead use FPCA on the $X _ { n } ,$ resulting in an $n \times p$ matrix of scores $\zeta .$
Once again we approximate $\beta \left( t \right)$ using the FPC's of $\mathrm { X } ,$ $\beta \left( t \right) \approx \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \beta _ { j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$
Using least squares, we again have

$$\widehat { \beta } = \left( \begin{array}{} \widehat { \beta } _ { 1 } \\ \vdots \\ \widehat { \beta } _ { p } \end{array} \right) = \left( \begin{array}{} \frac { \sum \xi _ { n 1 } Y _ { n } } { \xi _ { n 1 } ^ { 2 } } \\ \vdots \\ \frac { \sum \xi _ { n 1 } Y _ { n } } { \sum \xi _ { n } ^ { 2 } } \end{array} \right) ,$$

and the resulting estimate is given by

$$\widehat { \beta } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { q } \widehat { \beta } _ { j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \frac { \sum \zeta _ { j n } Y _ { n } } { \sum \zeta _ { j n } ^ { 2 } } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

Lastly, we turn to a full function-on-function linear model, as in Chapter
5, given by

$$Y _ { n } \left( t \right) = \int \beta \left( t , s \right) X _ { n } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { n } \left( t \right) .$$

Here we carry out FPCA on both $X$ and $Y .$ In this case we approximate
$\beta \left( t , s \right) \approx \sum \sum \beta _ { i j } \widehat { u } _ { i } \left( t \right) \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( s \right) .$ Using least squares we have that

$$\widehat { \beta } _ { i j } = \frac { \sum \zeta _ { j n } \xi _ { i n } } { \sum \zeta _ { j n } ^ { 2 } } ,$$

and the resulting estimate is given by

$$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { q } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \frac { \sum \zeta _ { j n } \xi _ { i n } } { \sum \zeta _ { j n } ^ { 2 } } \widehat { u } _ { i } \left( t \right) \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( s \right) .$$

a function $f \left( t \right)$ and (2) has derivatives $\left\{ f _ { n } ^ { \prime } \left( t \right) \right\}$ that are uniformly convergent

## 8 Functional time series

This Chapter is concerned with functional data observed sequentially over
time. For example, the curves $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots .$ can represent pollution levels at
some location; $X _ { n } \left( t \right)$ is the level of pollution at time $t$ of day n. Such curves
cannot be treated as being independent with the same distribution; they do
not form a simple random sample of curves. Pollution on a given day will
be affected by pollution on previous days, so the curves will be dependent.
A daily pollution pattern will be different on Sunday and on Monday, so the
curves will not have the same distribution every day. We will discuss several
more examples in this chapter.

To understand this chapter fully, some familiarity with basic tools and
objectives of time series analysis is needed. We provide a concise introduction
and list several textbooks in Section 8.1. In Section 8.2, we introduce the most
extensively used functional time series model, the autoregressive process of or-
der 1, the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ model. Sections 8.3 and 8.4 consider forecasting methods
for functional time series which are implemented in easy to use R packages. In
Section 8.5, we discuss the long-run covariance function. While the covariance
function is appropriate for iid functions, many inferential procedures for func-
tional time series are based on the long-run covariance function. Section 8.6
illustrates the application of this concept in the context of stationarity tests.
The chapter concludes with two elaborations on the FAR(1) model. In Sec-
tion 8.7, we show how to estimate it in $\mathrm { R } ,$ in Section 8.8, we prove its existence.
Some topics related to functional time series which are not discussed in this
chapter are briefly discussed in Section 8.9.


### 8.1 Fundamental concepts of time series analysis

The purpose of this section is to review fundamental concepts of time series
analysis in order to facilitate the understanding of the following sections of this
chapter. This section is concerned with scalar time series. Time series analysis
is a very large field which has been developed for over one hundred years.
Modern time series analysis can be viewed as a subfield of statistics, due to its
reliance on probability models. However, many of its fundamental ideas have
been developed by researchers in other fields, most notably signal processing

<!-- PageNumber="141" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="142" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

and econometrics, and it finds applications in practically all fields of science
and engineering. Many excellent textbooks exist, for example Brockwell and
Davis (2002), and its more advanced precursor Brockwell and Davis (1991),
Shumway and Stoffer (2011) and Hamilton (1994).

A time series is a sequence of observations, $x _ { n } ,$ $n = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N ,$ collected
over time. In time series analysis, it is customary to use the index t, instead
of $n$ or $i ,$ to denote time. In this textbook, $t$ is generally used to denote the
argument of a function, so we use $n$ to denote the time index. Two examples of
time series are shown in Figures 8.1 and 8.2. Time series typically show trends,
periodicities or abrupt changes in their general patterns. The objective of
time series analysis is to quantitatively describe the random mechanism which
generates the data. This is done by removing trends, periodicities, structural
change points, so that the resulting time series looks stationary. Intuitively,
every subsection of a stationary time series looks as if $i t$ was generated by
the same random mechanism. The top panel of Figure 8.3 shows a simulated
realization which is stationary. The time series in Figure 8.1 is not stationary
because the mean and variability at the beginning and the end of the period
are very different. The time series in Figure 8.2 is not stationary either because
the variability in the first half of the period is smaller than in the second half.

After the original time series observations have been transformed in such
a way that they can be assumed to be stationary, a suitable time series model
is found. $A$ time series model specifies the distribution of random variables
$X _ { n } ,$ $n \in \mathbb{Z} .$ It is typically stated by formulas which describe how $X _ { n }$ depends
on $X _ { n - 1 } , X _ { n - 2 } , \ldots .$ The most commonly used model is the autoregressive
process of order 1, AR(1), defined by the equations

$$X _ { n } - \mu = \varphi \left( X _ { n - 1 } - \mu \right) + \varepsilon _ { n } .$$

The sequence $\left\{ \varepsilon _ { n } \right\}$ is the white noise, e.g. a sequence of random variables for
which

$$E \varepsilon _ { n } = 0 , \mathrm { V a r } \left[ \varepsilon _ { n } \right] = \sigma ^ { 2 } , \mathrm { C o v } \left( \varepsilon _ { n } , \varepsilon _ { n + h } \right) = 0 , \mathrm { i f } h \neq 0 .$$

The concept of stationarity is actually defined for time series models, not
for the time series observations. The broadest definition of stationarity is as
follows:

DEFINITION 8.1.1 A time series model $\left\{ X _ { n } , n \in \mathbb{Z} \right\}$ is stationary if the ex-
pected value $E X _ { n }$ and the covariances $\mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n + h } \right)$ do not depend on n.

One can show that the AR(1) equations have a stationary solution if and only
if $| \varphi | < 1 .$ A realization of this process with $\varphi = 0 . 6$ is shown in Figure 8.3.

Once a stationary model for the transformed observations is found, it is
used to predict future values or test hypotheses about the data generating
mechanism, e.g. whether there is a trend in the data. The most important
tool that is used for such purposes is the autocovariance function defined by

$$\gamma _ { h } = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n + h } \right) , \quad h = 0 , 1 , 2 , \ldots .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="143" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 8.1: Time series of quarterly earnings of the Johnson &amp; Johnson com- pany. The circles show the actual observations. Reproduced from Shumway and Stoffer (2011).</figcaption>

</figure>


We emphasize that the autocovariance function is defined only for a stationary
model. Only nonnegative $l a g s \quad h$ are used because

$$\gamma _ { - h } = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n - h } \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n - h } , X _ { n } \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n + h } \right) = \gamma _ { h } .$$

Another function used in the analysis of stationary models is the autocorrela-
tion function defined by

$$\rho _ { h } = \mathrm { C o r r } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n + h } \right) = \frac { \gamma _ { h } } { \gamma _ { 0 } } , \quad h = 0 , 1 , 2 , \ldots .$$

Based on the observations $x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { N } ,$ the autocovariance functions is
estimated by the sample autocovariance function

$$\widehat { \gamma } _ { h } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N - h } \left( x _ { n } - \bar { x } \right) \left( x _ { n + h } - \bar { x } \right) , \quad \bar { x } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } x _ { n } .$$

In some work, the denominator $N$ in the formula defining $\widehat { \gamma } _ { h }$ is replaced by
$N - h .$ The autocorrelation function is estimated by the sample autocorrelation

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="144" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Seismogram signal

function, $\widehat { \rho } _ { h } = \widehat { \gamma } _ { h } / \widehat { \gamma } _ { 0 } .$ Even though $\widehat { \rho } \left( 0 \right) = 1$ for every time series, this value
is customarily plotted, as in the lower panel of Figure 8.3.

The sample autocorrelation function, and many other more complex tools,
are used to determine a suitable stationary model for the transformed time
series observations. Predictions of future values are then made using this sta-
tionary model, followed by the inverse transformation to obtain predictions
of the original data. For example, if a suitable stationary model is the $\mathrm { A R } \left( 1 \right)$
model (necessarily with $| \varphi | < 1 ,$ then the one-step-ahead prediction is

$$\widehat { X } _ { n + 1 } = \widehat { \mu } + \widehat { \varphi } \left( X _ { n } - \widehat { \mu } \right) .$$

It is obtained by equating the mean zero errors $\varepsilon _ { n }$ to zero, and replacing the
unknown parameters $\mu$ and $\varphi$ by their estimates.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="145" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

### 8.2 Functional autoregressive process

We say that a sequence $\left\{ X _ { n } , - \infty < n < \infty \right\}$ of mean zero elements of $L ^ { 2 }$
follows a functional (mean zero) AR(1), $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right) ,$ model if

$$X _ { n } = \Phi \left( X _ { n - 1 } \right) + \varepsilon _ { n } ,$$
(8.1)

where $\Phi : L ^ { 2 } \rightarrow L ^ { 2 }$ is an operator transforming a function into another
function, and $\left\{ \varepsilon _ { n } , - \infty < n < \infty \right\}$ is a sequence of id mean zero errors. In
applications, the operator $\Phi$ is an integral operator defined by

$$\Phi \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \int \varphi \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s , \quad x \in L ^ { 2 } .$$

Equations (8.1) can thus be rewritten more directly as

$$X _ { n } \left( t \right) = \int \varphi \left( t , s \right) X _ { n - 1 } \left( s \right) d s + \varepsilon _ { n } \left( t \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="146" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

It is shown in Section 8.8, that there exists a stationary functional sequence
$\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ satisfying the above equation if $\iint \varphi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < \infty ,$ a condition we
assume from now on.

A more general definition of the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ model is sometimes used. Anal-
ogously to the scalar case, it allows the $\varepsilon _ { n }$ to be a suitably defined white
noise in $L ^ { 2 } ,$ i.e. to be uncorrelated in an appropriate Hilbert space sense.
However, statistical inference requires that the errors be iid, so we will work
with this narrower class. To simplify the formulas, we also assume that the
mean function is zero. The general $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ process is defined by the equation
$X _ { n } - \mu = \Phi \left( X _ { n - 1 } - \mu \right) + \varepsilon _ { n } .$ All results stated in this chapter are valid for this
more general process by replacing $X _ { n }$ by $X _ { n } - \mu .$ In applications, the mean

observed functions.
$\mu$
$\widehat { \mu } = \bar { X } _ { N } = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n }$

Equation (8.1) defines the functional autoregressive process of order 1. It
is easy to define $\mathrm { F A R } \left( p \right)$ processes, i.e. autoregressive processes of order $p$ by
including additional terms of the form $\Phi _ { k } \left( X _ { n - k } \right) ,$ $1 \leq k \leq p .$ In the analysis
of functional time series, these processes however play a role smaller than their
scalar counterparts.

We now turn to the estimation of the autoregressive operator $\Phi .$ It is in-
structive to consider first the univariate case $X _ { n } = \varphi X _ { n - 1 } + \varepsilon _ { n } ,$ in which all
quantities are scalars. We assume that $| \varphi | < 1 ,$ so that there is a stationary
solution such that $\varepsilon _ { n }$ is independent of $X _ { n - 1 } .$ Then, multiplying the AR(1)
equation by $X _ { n - 1 }$ and taking the expectation, we obtain $\gamma _ { 1 } = \varphi \gamma _ { 0 } ,$ cf. Sec-
tion 8.1. The autocovariances $\gamma _ { h }$ are estimated by the sample autocovariances
$\widehat { \gamma } _ { h } ,$ so the usual estimator of $\varphi$ is $\widehat { \varphi } = \widehat { \gamma } _ { 1 } / \widehat { \gamma } _ { 0 } .$ To apply this approach to the
functional model, note that by (8.1),

$$E \left[ \langle X _ { n } , x \rangle X _ { n - 1 } \right] = E \left[ \langle \Phi \left( X _ { n - 1 } \right) , x \rangle X _ { n - 1 } \right] , \quad x \in L ^ { 2 } .$$

Define the lag-1 autocovariance operator by

$$C _ { 1 } \left( x \right) = E \left[ \langle X _ { n } , x \rangle X _ { n + 1 } \right]$$

and denote with superscript .* the adjoint operator. Then, $C _ { 1 } ^ { * } = C \Phi ^ { * }$ because,
by a direct verification, $C _ { 1 } ^ { * } = E \left[ \langle X _ { n } , x \rangle X _ { n - 1 } \right] .$ This leads to the identity

$$C _ { 1 } = \Phi C .$$
(8.2)

The above identity is analogous to the scalar case, so we would like to $\mathrm { o b t a i n }$
an estimate of $\Phi$ by using a finite sample version of the relation $\Phi = C _ { 1 } C ^ { - 1 } .$
The operator $C ^ { - 1 }$ cannot, however, be defined on the the whole space $L ^ { 2 } .$ $\mathrm { T o }$
see it, recall that the inverse operator must satisfy $C ^ { - 1 } \left( C \left( x \right) \right) = x .$ Denoting
by $v _ { j }$ the eigenfunctions of $C ,$ we thus need $v _ { j } = C ^ { - 1 } \left( C \left( v _ { j } \right) \right) = C ^ { - 1 } \left( \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } \right) =$
$\lambda _ { j } C ^ { - 1 } \left( v _ { j } \right) .$ This implies that $C ^ { - 1 } \left( v _ { j } \right) = \lambda _ { j } ^ { - 1 } v _ { j } ,$ if $\lambda _ { j } > 0 ,$ an assumption
we must make. Since $\left\{ v _ { j } \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis, every $y \in L ^ { 2 }$ admits

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="147" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

representation $y = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle y , v _ { j } \rangle v _ { j } .$ This implies $t h a t$

$$C ^ { - 1 } \left( y \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle y , v _ { j } \rangle v _ { j } .$$

However, the above sum converges only if

$$\| C ^ { - 1 } \left( y \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } ^ { - 2 } \langle y , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } < \infty .$$
(8.3)

This will not hold for every $y ,$ take e.g. $y = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } .$ The above consider-
ations suggest the following solution. Assume that $\lambda _ { 1 } \geq \lambda _ { 2 } \geq \cdots \geq \lambda _ { p } > 0 .$
Relation (8.3) clearly holds if $y \in \mathrm { s p } \left( v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 } , \ldots , v _ { p } \right) .$ We can define $C _ { p } ^ { - 1 }$ by
$C _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left( y \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \lambda _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle y , v _ { i } \rangle v _ { i } .$ The operator $C _ { p } ^ { - 1 }$ is defined on the whole of
$L ^ { 2 } ,$ but it is not a proper inverse, Problem 8.4. It is often called the pseudo-
inverse and denoted $C _ { p } ^ { + } .$

A sample counterpart of this pseudo-inverse is defined by

$$\widehat { C _ { p } ^ { + } } \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle x , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { j } .$$

The operator $C _ { p } ^ { + }$ is defined on the whole of $L ^ { 2 }$ if $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j } > 0$ for $j \leq p .$ By
judiciously choosing $p ,$ we find a balance between retaining the relevant in-
formation in the sample, and the instability due to the reciprocals of small
eigenvalues $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j } .$ To derive a computable estimator of $\Phi ,$ we use a sample version
of the relation $\Phi \approx C C _ { 1 } ^ { - 1 } .$ Since $C _ { 1 }$ is estimated by

$$\widehat { C } _ { 1 } \left( x \right) = \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \langle X _ { k } , x \rangle X _ { k + 1 } ,$$

we obtain, for any $x \in L ^ { 2 } ,$

$$\widehat { C _ { 1 } C _ { p } ^ { + } } \left( x \right) = \widehat { C } _ { 1 } \left( \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle x , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { j } \right)$$
$$= \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \langle X _ { k } , \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle x , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle X _ { k + 1 }$$
$$= \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle x , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \langle X _ { k } , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle X _ { k + 1 } .$$

The estimator $\widehat { C } _ { 1 } \widetilde { C _ { p } ^ { + } }$ can be used in principle, but typically an additional

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="148" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

dimension reduction $\mathrm { s t e p }$ is introduced by using the approximation $X _ { k + 1 } \approx$
$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X _ { k + 1 } , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { i } .$ This leads $\mathrm { t } \alpha$ the estimator

$$\widehat { \Phi } _ { p } \left( x \right) = \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle x , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \langle X _ { k } , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \langle X _ { k + 1 } , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { i } .$$
(8.4)

The estimator (8.4) is a kernel operator with the kernel

$$\widehat { \varphi } _ { p } \left( t , s \right) = \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \langle X _ { k } , \widehat { v } _ { j } \rangle \langle X _ { k + 1 } , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( s \right) \widehat { v } _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$
(8.5)

All quantities at the right-hand side of (8.5) are available as output of the $\mathrm { R }$
function pca. fd, so this estimator is easy to compute. Section 8.7 shows how
the FAR(1) model can be generated and estimated within the package $f d a .$


### 8.3 Forecasting with the Hyndman-Ullah method

The method of forecasting functional time series which we describe in this
section has been developed in the context of mortality curves. It is known in
actuarial science as the Hyndman-Ullah method. It can however be applied to
any functional time series which can be well approximated with a truncated
Karhunen-Loéve expansion. Its advantage over methods based on the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right.$
model is that it assumes neither stationarity nor a specific autocorrelation
structure, and can be applied almost automatically using many procedures
designed for forecasting of univariate time series. Its optimality has not been
investigated theoretically. We explain this method in a general setting, and
then illustrate its application to the prediction of mortality curves.

Suppose $X _ { n } ,$ $n = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N ,$ are observations of a sufficiently smooth
functional time series. Consider the approximation

$$X _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } \left( t \right) = \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \widehat { \xi } _ { n , j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

The truncation level $J$ can be chosen by the cumulative variance method,
but in the context of forecasting, it is more useful to examine the residual
curves $e _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } = X _ { n } - X _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } .$ These curves should be a functional white noise,
so that they do not contain information useful for prediction. One way of
verifying it is to apply a Portmanteau test described in Chapter 7 of Horváth
and Kokoszka (2012). A simple exploratory tool is to construct projections
$\langle e _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } , u \rangle$ for several test functions $u$ and examine the autocorrelation plots of
these univariate time series.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="149" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

Once the truncation level $J$ has been selected, all that remains is to con-
struct forecasts $\widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , j }$ of the future scores $\widehat { \xi } _ { N + h , j } .$ The curve $X _ { N + h }$ is then
forecasted by

$$X _ { N + h | N } ^ { \left( J \right) } \left( t \right) = \widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , j } \widehat { v } _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$
(8.6)

The easiest way to predict $\widehat { \xi } _ { N + h , j }$ is to use only the available scores of the jth
$\mathrm { E F P C } \widehat { v } _ { j } .$ In other words, we work separately with each univariate time series
$\widehat { \xi } _ { 1 , j } , \widehat { \xi } _ { 2 , j } , \ldots , \widehat { \xi } _ { N , j } .$ Many automated methods of prediction of univariate time
series exist, for example the seasonal ARIMA method with the model selected
by an information criterion, like the $A I C .$ Most time series textbooks treat
prediction, Chapter 3 of Shumway and Stoffer (2011) provides a good account
with $\mathrm { R }$ code. It should be noted that the sample mean $\widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( t \right)$
does not change with the lead time h. The Hyndman-Ullah method assumes
that the mean function does not change. If there is some visual evidence
that the mean changes, its dynamics should be modeled separately, and the
constant $\widehat { \mu }$ replaced by an appropriate forecast.

We now illustrate the general approach described above by applying it
to mortality curves. We follow the exposition in Booth et al. (2014). Certain
actuarial definitions are simplified. Denote by $D _ { n } \left( t \right)$ the count of deaths in
calendar year $n$ of people aged t. We treat $t$ is a continuous variable in some
fixed range, e.g. $\left( 0 , 1 1 0 \right) ;$ but in practice $t$ is measured in years. Denote by $P _ { n } \left( t \right)$
the population of age $t$ in year n. Quantities $D _ { n } \left( t \right)$ and $P _ { n } \left( t \right)$ are available
for specific countries. We will work with the data for the United States. They
can be accessed as follows:

library (RCurl) ; library (demography) ; library (MortalitySmooth)
usa <- hmd. mx ("USA", "username", "password", "USA")
usa1950 <- extract . years (usa, years=19MortalitySmooth50: 2010)

The username and password can be obtained from the Human Mor-
tality Database which can be accessed through the help file of the func-
tion hmd. mx. (Human Mortality Database, University of California, Berke-
ley (USA), and Max Planck Institute for Demographic Research (Germany).
Available at www.mortality.org or www.humanmortality.de, data downloaded
May 5, 2015)

The object usa1950 contains mortality rates for the United States from
1950 to 2010. The mortality rate is defined as $m _ { n } \left( t \right) = D _ { n } \left( t \right) / P _ { n } \left( t \right) .$ For any
fixed year n, $m _ { n } \left( t \right)$ is the proportion of the population of age $t$ that has died.
The curve $m _ { n } \left( t \right)$ increases rapidly with t, so it is usual to work with the $\log$
mortality rate, In $m _ { n } \left( t \right) .$ Except for very large or small t, the curves In $m _ { n } \left( t \right)$
are smooth. To remove the noisy behavior at the ends and create functional
objects, we perform a smoothing step and obtain smooth versions of the curves
$\ln m _ { n } ,$ which we denote by $X _ { n } .$ Our objective is to predict future curves $X _ { N + h }$
based on the available curves $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ These predictions are treated

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="150" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

as the predictions of the curves $\ln m _ { N + h } .$ Smoothing can be done in a number
of ways; a custom function in the demography package can be used as follows
to produce the curve in Figure 8.4:

smus <- smooth. demogdata (usa1950)
plot $\left( u s a 1 9 5 0 , \right.$ $y e a r s = 2 0 0 3 ,$ series="male", type="p", pch=1)
lines (smus, $y e a r s = 2 0 0 3 ,$ $\left. \mathrm { S e r i e s } = { } ^ { \mathrm { H } } \mathrm { m a l } \in { } ^ { \mathrm { H } } \right)$

The implementation of the Hyndman-Ullah method proceeds with the
following code, which also produces Figures 8.5 and 8.6

fdm. male <- fdm (smus, series="male", order=3)
forecast.fdm.male <- forecast.fdm (fdm.male, h=30)
plot (forecast . fdm. male, plot. type="component")
plot (forecast. fdm. male)

The function fdm calculates the EFPC's and the series of scores. The
function forecast . fdm computes univariate forecasts. These are shown in
Figure 8.5. The confidence bands for the predictions of the univariate series
of scores show that a pronounced downward trend is visible only for the first
EFPC scores $\widehat { \xi } _ { n 1 } .$ This trend is reflected in Figure 8.6 which indicates a trend
toward a lower mortality rate, $\widehat { v } _ { 1 } \left( t \right)$ is positive, with particular gains around

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="151" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

the ages of 5-15 and 50-70, which correspond, respectively, to the maximum
and the local maximum of the curve $\widehat { v } _ { 1 } \left( t \right) .$

The above code is highly customized for data sets in the demography
package, but all steps can be implemented using package fda and one of the
packages for time series analysis e.g. $a s t s a .$ In Problem 8.9 we illustrate the
application of the package ftsa, in which the Hyndman-Ullah method is also
implemented.

### 8.4 Forecasting with multivariate predictors

The method of Section 8.3 treats the series of scores $\widehat { \xi } _ { n , j }$ as univariate time
series and constructs predictions $\widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , j }$ separately for every $j .$ A natural
extension is to consider the vectors of scores

$$\Xi _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } = \left[ \widehat { \xi } _ { n , 1 } , \widehat { \xi } _ { n , 2 } , \ldots , \widehat { \xi } _ { n , J } \right] ^ { T }$$

and predict the whole vector $\Xi _ { N + h } ^ { \left( J \right) } .$ This leads to the following general algo-
rithm:

a) Select $J$ by one of the methods discussed in Section 8.3 and compute the
vectors $\Xi _ { n } ^ { \left( J \right) } ,$ $n = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N .$

b) Use a multivariate prediction technique to construct a predictor of the
vector $\Xi _ { N + h } ^ { \left( J \right) } .$ Denote this predictor by

$$\widehat { \Xi } _ { N + h | N } ^ { \left( J \right) } = \left[ \widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , 1 } , \widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , 2 } , \ldots , \widehat { \xi } _ { N + h | N , J } \right] ^ { T } .$$

c) Predict the function $X _ { N + h }$ with (8.6).

We emphasize that even though both the Hyndman-Ullah and the multi-
variate method of this section use the same functional predictor (8.6), the
Hyndman-Ullah method is in fact a special case of the multivariate method.
It uses a specific approach to the prediction of the vector $\Xi _ { N + h } ^ { \left( J \right) }$ each compo-
nent is predicted separately. In some cases, this simpler approach may reduce
the variability of the forecasts, but if the sample cross- covariances of $\widehat { \xi } _ { n , j }$ and
$\widehat { \xi } _ { m , k }$ are large, it may be inferior. Recall that, in general, the above covariances
are zero for $k \neq j$ only if $n =$ m.

In its current implementation, the above algorithm assumes, in addition,
that the process $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ is stationary; the mean function $\mu$ is treated as constant
and the EFPC's $\widehat { v } _ { j }$ are assumed to approximate well the $\mathrm { F P C } ^ { \prime } \mathrm { s } v _ { \imath }$ $v _ { j } ,$ which lead
to optimal approximations, cf. Chapter 3. The method is implemented in
the package $f t s a .$ We illustrate it with the following code which produces
Figure 8.7.

require (ftsa) ; require (vars)
$x = \sec \left( C \right. ,$ 23.5, by = 0.5)
multi_forecast_sqrt_pm10 = farforecast (ftsm (pm_10_GR_sqrt), h
= 30, PI=F)
plot (multi_forecast_sqrt_pm10, ylim=c (5.2, 7.5) , xlab="Hour",
ylab="Square root of pm10", 1w=2)
oneStep_forecast_sqrt_pm10 = farforecast (ftsm (pm_10_GR_sqrt) ,
h=1)
lines(oneStep_forecast_sqrt_pm10, lwd=3, lty=3)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="155" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 8.7: Predicted pm10 pollution curves; one step ahead prediction highlighted with black dots.</figcaption>

Square root of pm10

6.5

6.0

</figure>


The data set we use contains half-hourly measurements of the concentration
of particulate matter of certain size in Graz, Austria, October 1, 2010 -March
31, 2011. The square root transformation is applied to stabilize variance. The
function $\mathrm { f a r f o r e c a s t }$ computes the predicted curves. The "far" in its name
indicates that a vector autoregressive scheme is used to predict the vectors
of scores; these predictions are then used to construct the functional fore-
casts via (8.6). Figure 8.7 shows that these predictions converge to the overall
mean function of the functional time series; this is a well-known property of
predictions of stationary series.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="156" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 8.5 Long-run covariance function

In many formulas and procedures of time series analysis, in place of the usual
variance or sample variance, a quantity known as the long-run variance (LRV),
and its estimators, must be used. We explain in this section how an analog
of the LRV is defined for functional time series and how it can be estimated.
In Section 8.6, we illustrate the application of this concept to testing the
stationarity of functional time series. We begin by introducing the LRV of a
scalar time series.

Suppose $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ is a scalar stationary process, cf. Definition 8.1.1. Using the
autocovariances $\gamma _ { h } = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n + h } \right) ,$ we define the LRV as

$$\sigma ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } = \gamma _ { 0 } + 2 \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } .$$
(8.7)

The motivation for definition (8.7) is as follows. If $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$ are
iid with $E X _ { i } = \mu ,$ $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ X _ { i } \right] = \sigma ^ { 2 } ,$ then the variance of the sample mean
$\bar { X } _ { N } = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n }$ is given by the usual formula

$$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \bar { X } _ { N } \right] = N ^ { - 2 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \mathrm { V a r } \left[ X _ { n } \right] = N ^ { - 1 } \sigma ^ { 2 } .$$

If the $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$ are a realization of a general stationary process, then
the above formula is no longer valid. The variance of the sample mean is
calculated as follows:

$$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \bar { X } _ { N } \right] = N ^ { - 2 } \mathrm { V a r } \left[ \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \right] = N ^ { - 2 } \sum _ { n , m = 1 } ^ { N } \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { m } \right) .$$

Due to stationarity, the covariances depend only on on the differences $h =$
$n \quad - \quad m .$ We can rewrite the above formula by grouping covariances with the
same difference $h :$

$$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ \bar { X } _ { N } \right] = N ^ { - 2 } \sum _ { h = - N + 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \left( N - | h | \right) \gamma _ { h } = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { h = - N + 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \left( 1 - \frac { | h | } { N } \right) \gamma _ { h } .$$
(8.8)

Using the Dominated Convergence Theorem, a frequently used result in prob-
ability theory, see e.g. Rudin (1987), it can be shown that if $\sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } | \gamma _ { h } | < \infty ,$
then

$$\lim _ { N \rightarrow \infty } N \mathrm { V a r } \left[ \bar { X } _ { N } \right] = \sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } .$$

The above convergence is easy to justify intuitively: for each $h ,$ $h / N \rightarrow 0 ,$ and

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="157" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

the summation range expands to all integers, as $N \rightarrow \infty .$ One can also show
that under suitable assumptions on the dependence structure of the process
$\left\{ X _ { n } \right\} ,$

$$\sqrt { N } \left( \bar { X } _ { N } - \mu \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } N \left( 0 , \sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } \right) .$$
(8.9)

Result (8.9) should be contrasted with the usual Central Limit Theorem for
iid $X _ { n }$ instead of $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ X _ { 1 } \right] ,$ the limit variance in (8.9) is the LRV (8.7). As a
consequence, the confidence interval for $\mu$ has the form $\bar { X } _ { N } \pm z _ { 1 - \alpha / 2 } N ^ { - 1 / 2 } \widehat { \sigma } ,$
where $\widehat { \sigma }$ is an estimate of the $\sigma$ defined in $\left( 8 . 7 \right) ,$ rather than the usual sample
variance.

A common approach to the estimation of the LRV (8.7) is to use an esti-
mator of the form

$$\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } = \widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( K , q \right) = \sum _ { h = - N + 1 } ^ { N + 1 } K \left( \frac { h } { q } \right) \widehat { \gamma } _ { h } .$$
(8.10)

The sample autocovariances $\widehat { \gamma } _ { h }$ are defined in Section 8.1. The weight function,
or lag window, $K$ has the following properties: $K \left( x \right) = 0$ if $| x | >$ 1; $K \left( - x \right) =$
$K \left( x \right) ,$ $K \left( 0 \right) = 1 .$ Additional smoothness properties are required to establish
large sample properties of such estimators, but we will not discuss them here;
the function $K$ must be at least continuous. The parameter $q$ is called the
bandwidth. It is a positive integer smaller than $N ;$ asymptotic arguments
require that $q / N \rightarrow 0$ and $q \rightarrow \infty ,$ as $N \rightarrow \infty .$ A large body of research is
dedicated to methods of finding an optimal $q ,$ but we cannot discuss these
issues in this brief section.

The general form of the estimator $\left( 8 . 1 0 \right)$ is motivated by formula (8.8),
which is similar to (8.10) with

$$K \left( x \right) = 1 - | x | , \quad | x | \leq 1 ,$$
(8.11)

and with $q = N .$ Note, however, that (8.8) involves population autocovariances
$\gamma _ { h } ,$ while (8.10) is defined in terms of the sample autocovariances $\widehat { \gamma } _ { h } .$ The
sampling variability of the $\widehat { \gamma } _ { h }$ is the reason why it must be assumed that
$q / N \rightarrow 0$ to ensure that $\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 } \left( K , q \right)$ converges to $\sigma ^ { 2 } .$ The condition $K \left( x \right) = 0$ if
$\left[ x \right] > 1$ implies that we use only the $\widehat { \gamma } _ { h }$ with $| h | \leq q .$ Function (8.11) is called
the Bartlett window. Other commonly used windows are listed in many time
series textbooks, see e.g. Section 10.4 of Brockwell and Davis (1991).

We are now ready to turn to the case of a stationary functional time series.
We will use the following definition:

DEFINITION 8.5.1 A functional time series model $\left\{ X _ { n } , n \in \mathbb{Z} \right\}$ is stationary
if

$$\mu \left( t \right) = E X _ { n } \left( t \right)$$
$$\gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } \left( t \right) , X _ { n + h } \left( s \right) \right) , h \in \mathbb{Z} ,$$
and

do not depend on n.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="158" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Definition 8.5.1 assumes that each function $X _ { n }$ is a square integrable $\mathrm { e l }$
ement of $L ^ { 2 } ,$ i.e. $E \int X _ { n } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t < \infty ,$ cf. Chapter 3. Note also that for func-
tional time series we must carefully distinguish between negative and positive
lags $h ;$ if $X _ { n }$ is a scalar stationary sequence, then $\gamma _ { - h } = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } , X _ { n - h } \right) =$
$\mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n - h } , X _ { n } \right) = \gamma \left( h \right) .$ However, for a functional stationary sequence,

$$\gamma _ { - h } \left( t , s \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n } \left( t \right) , X _ { n - h } \left( s \right) \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X _ { n - h } \left( s \right) , X _ { n } \left( t \right) \right) = \gamma _ { h } \left( s , t \right) .$$

If the $X _ { n }$ are independent, then the autocovariances $\gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right)$ are zero except for
$h =$ 0. The lag zero autocovariance function $\gamma _ { 0 } \left( t , s \right)$ is equal to the covariance
function $c \left( t , s \right) .$

By analogy to the LRV of a scalar sequence, cf. (8.7), the long-run $c o -$
variance function (LRCF) of a stationary functional sequence is defined by

$$\sigma \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right) = \gamma _ { 0 } \left( t , s \right) + 2 \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right) + \gamma _ { h } \left( s , t \right) \right) .$$
(8.12)

In order for the infinite sequence in (8.12) to converge in the space of square
integrable functions on $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] \times \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ we must assume that

$$\sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \iint \gamma _ { h } ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < \infty .$$
(8.13)

As in the scalar case, the LRCF (8.12) is related to the covariances of the
sample mean function. Following the derivation presented in the scalar case,
one can show that

$$N \mathrm { C o v } \left( \bar { X } _ { N } \left( t \right) , \bar { X } _ { N } \left( s \right) \right) = \sum _ { h = - N + 1 } ^ { N - 1 } \left( 1 - \frac { | h | } { N } \right) \gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right) \rightarrow \sum _ { h = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { h } \left( t , s \right) . \left( 8 . 1 4 \right)$$

Analogously to (8.9), one can also show that

$$\sqrt { N } \left( \bar { X } _ { N } - \mu \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } Z ,$$
(8.15)

where $Z$ is a Gaussian random function with $E Z \left( t \right) = 0$ and $\mathrm { C o v } \left( Z \left( t \right) , Z \left( s \right) \right) =$
$\sigma \left( t , s \right) .$ Stationarity and condition (8.13) are not sufficient to imply (8.14) and
(8.15). Additional assumptions on the dependence structure of the sequence
$\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ are needed. References are given in Section 8.9.

We conclude this section by introducing an estimator of the LRCF which
is analogous to the estimator (8.10). For a lag window $K$ and a bandwidth
$q < N ,$ we set

$$\widehat { \sigma } \left( t , s \right) = \sum _ { h = - N + 1 } ^ { N - 1 } K \left( \frac { h } { q } \right) \widehat { \gamma } _ { h } \left( t , s \right)$$
$$= \widehat { \gamma } _ { 0 } \left( t , s \right) + \sum _ { h = 1 } ^ { N - 1 } K \left( \frac { h } { q } \right) \left\{ \widehat { \gamma } _ { h } \left( t , s \right) + \widehat { \gamma } _ { h } \left( s , t \right) \right\} ,$$
(8.16)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="159" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

where

$$\widehat { \gamma } _ { h } \left( t , s \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { N - h } \left( X _ { j } \left( t \right) - \bar { X } _ { N } \left( t \right) \right) \left( X _ { j + h } \left( s \right) - \bar { X } _ { N } \left( s \right) \right) .$$

Under suitable assumptions on the dependence structure of the FTS $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\} ,$
and on the window $K ,$ estimator (8.16) converges to $\sigma \left( t , s \right) :$

$$\iint \left\{ \widehat { \sigma } \left( t , s \right) - \sigma \left( t , s \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t d s \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } 0 , \quad \mathrm { a s } \quad N \rightarrow \infty .$$


### 8.6 Testing stationarity of functional time series

As we have seen in previous sections of this chapter, many procedures require
that a functional time series be transformed to a stationary series. In this
section, we discuss tests which allow us to verify if a given FTS is stationary.
We present heuristic derivations, which illustrate several important techniques
used in the analysis of FTS's. References to research papers which state precise
assumptions and provide the requisite theory are provided in Section 8.9.

We want to test the null hypothesis

$$H _ { 0 } : \quad X _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \eta _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$$

where $\left\{ \eta _ { i } \right\}$ is a mean zero strictly stationary sequence, cf. Definition 8.8.1. The
mean function $\mu \left( t \right) = E X _ { i } \left( t \right)$ is unknown. The general alternative hypothesis
is simply that $H _ { 0 }$ is not true. However, two specific alternatives motivate the
form of the test statistics. The first one is the change point alternative:

$$H _ { A , 1 } : \quad X _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \delta \left( t \right) I \left\{ i > k ^ { * } \right\} + \eta _ { i } \left( t \right) .$$

The change point $1 \leq k ^ { * } < N$ is an unknown integer; the curves up to time
$k ^ { * }$ have mean function $\mu \left( t \right) ,$ after time $k ^ { * }$ their mean function is $\mu \left( t \right) + \delta \left( t \right) .$
The second alternative is the random walk:

$$H _ { A , 2 } : \quad X _ { i } \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { i } u _ { \ell } \left( t \right) .$$

The sequence $\left\{ u _ { \ell } \right\}$ is stationary with mean zero, the $u _ { \ell }$ are often assumed $\mathrm { t o }$
be even iid.

We emphasize, that each function $t \mapsto X _ { i } \left( t \right)$ is typically a realization
of a continuous time nonstationary process observed at some discrete points
$t _ { j } .$ The stationarity refers to the sequence of functions $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ This
point is illustrated in Figure 8.8. The top panel shows price curves on five
consecutive days. Each of these curves can be denoted $X _ { i } ,$ and only $N = 5$
curves are shown. In typical applications, $N$ is much larger, from several dozen

<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

to several thousand. The sequence of price curves is in general not stationary.
Even for the five displayed curves an upward trend is seen, such a trending
or random walk behavior is much more pronounced for longer series. The
Bottom panel of Figure 8.8 shows the same curves, but suitably normalized.
Even though each curve is a realization of a nonstationary stochastic process,
such normalized curves, known as cumulative intraday return curves, form $\mathrm { a }$
stationary functional time series.

Test statistics are based on the partial sum process

$$S _ { N } \left( x , t \right) = N ^ { - 1 / 2 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \left[ N x \right] } X _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad 0 \leq x \leq 1 ,$$

where $\left[ y \right]$ is the integer part of $y ,$ e.g. $\left[ 9 . 5 7 \right] = 9 .$ A convenient way to look at
the process $S _ { N }$ is to treat it as a stochastic process with index $x \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ and
values in the space $L ^ { 2 } ,$ i.e. for each x, $S _ { N } \left( x \right)$ is a random function. Realizations
of the process $\left\{ S _ { N } \left( x \right) \right. ,$ $\left. x \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right\}$ have jumps at points $x _ { k } = k / N .$ Observe
that

$$S _ { N } \left( \frac { k } { N } , t \right) = N ^ { - 1 / 2 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k } X _ { i } \left( t \right) , \quad k = 1 , 2 , \ldots N .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="161" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

The next step in the construction of test statistics is to define the process

$$U _ { N } \left( x \right) = S _ { N } \left( x \right) - x S _ { N } \left( 1 \right) , \quad 0 \leq x \leq 1 .$$

Under $H _ { 0 } ,$ the mean function $\mu$ cancels, and

$$U _ { N } \left( \frac { k } { N } \right) = \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { N } } \left\{ \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k } \eta _ { i } - \frac { k } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \eta _ { i } \right\} .$$
(8.17)

This cancelation occurs only under $H _ { 0 } .$ For example, under the change point
alternative $H _ { A , 1 } ,$

$$U _ { N } \left( \frac { k ^ { * } } { N } \right) = \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { N } } \left\{ \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k ^ { * } } \eta _ { i } - \frac { k ^ { * } } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \eta _ { i } \right\} + \frac { k ^ { * } \left( N - k ^ { * } \right) } { N ^ { 3 / 2 } } \delta .$$

The term involving the jump function $\delta$ has a more complicated form for
$k \neq k ^ { * } ,$ Problem 8.13. The point is that under $H _ { A , 1 } ,$ the process $U _ { N }$ consists
of two terms: the right-hand side of (8.17) and an additional term whose size is
approximately $N ^ { 1 / 2 } \delta .$ The same is true under $H _ { A , 2 } ,$ except that the additional
term is random, it involves the partial sums of the $u _ { \ell } .$ The idea of testing is
that under $H _ { 0 }$ functionals of the process $U _ { N }$ converge to some distributions,
but under various alternatives they diverge in probability to infinity, exceeding
the critical values found under $H _ { 0 }$ with increasing probability as the sample
size $N$ tends to infinity.

Our first test statistic has the form

$$\widehat { T } _ { N } = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \| U _ { N } \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } d x = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \left\{ \int U _ { N } ^ { 2 } \left( x , t \right) d t \right\} d x .$$

It tells us how large the process $U _ { N }$ is when averaged over all x. If $H _ { 0 }$ is true,
$\widehat { T } _ { N }$ can be computed using the normalized partial sums $N ^ { - 1 / 2 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k } \eta _ { i } .$ These
partial sums converge to Gaussian random functions, cf. (8.15). The statistic
$\widehat { T } _ { N }$ therefore converges to a certain limit distribution. Under $H _ { A , 1 }$ or $H _ { A , 2 } ,$
the extra terms cause $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ to be so large that it exceeds critical values with
probability approaching 1, as $N \rightarrow \infty .$ The derivation of the limit distribution
of $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ requires more background than can be presented in this section, $\mathrm { S O }$ we
merely formulate the final result.

Denote by $\sigma _ { \eta }$ the LRCF of the $\eta _ { i } ,$ cf. (8.12). Next, denote by $\lambda _ { j }$ and $\varphi _ { j } ,$
respectively, the eigenvalues and the eigenfunctions of $\sigma _ { \eta } ,$ which are defined
by

$$\int \sigma _ { \eta } \left( t , s \right) \varphi _ { j } \left( s \right) d s = \lambda _ { j } \varphi _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad 1 \leq j < \infty .$$

To specify the limit distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ we also need to introduce the Brownian
bridge. It is a real-valued Gaussian stochastic process $\left\{ B \left( x \right) , x \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right\}$ which

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="162" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

is discussed in some detail in Section 11.4. Its value at point $x _ { k } = k / N$ can
be approximated as

$$B \left( \frac { k } { N } \right) \approx \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { N } } \left\{ \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k } Z _ { i } - \frac { k } { N } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } Z _ { i } \right\} ,$$
(8.18)

where the $Z _ { i }$ are iid standard normal random variables. The similarity of
this expression to (8.17) is obvious. An approximate trajectory of a Brownian
bridge over $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is easy to simulate using $Z _ { 1 } , Z _ { 2 } , \ldots , Z _ { N } .$

With the above background, we can state that, as $N \rightarrow \infty ,$ the distribution
of $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ approaches the distribution of the random variable

$$T : = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } B _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( x \right) d x ,$$

where the $B _ { j }$ are independent Brownian bridges. This approximation is valid
if $H _ { 0 }$ is true. The random variable $T$ is defined in terms of an infinite sum
which involves unknown eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ and independent random variables
$\int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } B _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( x \right) d x .$ The distribution of $T$ must therefore be approximated. We ex-
plain in some detail how a suitable approximation to the distribution of $T$
is constructed. Similar issues arise in many tests developed for functional
data. The reader should keep in mind that the distribution of $T$ is already an
asymptotic approximation to the distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ .

There are two levels of the approximation to the distribution of $T .$ First, we
replace $T$ by an approximation $T ^ { \star }$ which can be computed from the observed
functions $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ In the second step, we generate a large number, say
$R = 1 0 E 4 ,$ of replications of $T ^ { \star }$ *. The empirical distribution of the $R$ values
of $T ^ { \star }$ is used in place of the distribution of $T .$ For example, if 382 values $T ^ { \star }$
are greater than $\widehat { T } _ { N } ,$ then the P-value is 0.0382, assuming $R = 1 0 E 4 .$ We
therefore focus on the first $s t e p ,$ finding a computable approximation to $T .$

The unknown $\lambda _ { j }$ must be replaced by their estimates $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j }$ which are defined
by

$$\int \widehat { \sigma } _ { \eta } \left( t , s \right) \widehat { \varphi } _ { j } \left( s \right) d s = \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } \widehat { \varphi } _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad 1 \leq j < \infty .$$

The $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j }$ are the eigenvalues of an estimator of the LRCF $\sigma _ { \eta } \left( t , s \right)$ estimator
(8.16) can be used. The number of available $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j }$ depends on a specific numerical
implementation of the above eigenvalue problem. Assuming that $D$ eigenvalues
are computed, the distribution of $T$ is approximated by the distribution of
$T ^ { \star } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { D } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } I _ { j } ^ { \star } ,$ where $I _ { j } ^ { \star }$ is an approximation to $\int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } B _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( x \right) d x .$ A simple way
to compute $I _ { j } ^ { \star }$ is to use the Riemann sum

$$I ^ { \star } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } B ^ { 2 } \left( \frac { k } { N } \right) ,$$

with $B \left( k / N \right)$ defined in (8.18).

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="163" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

The test described above is often referred to as a Monte Carlo test because
the critical values have to be found by simulation for each specific data set.
With modern computing devices, tests of this form do not pose problems.
However, tests which use the same critical values for every data sets are often
more convenient to apply. To illustrate the difference in a familiar setting,
suppose we observe a sample of iid scalar random variables $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$
which have normal distribution with unknown expectation $\mu$ and unknown
variance $\sigma ^ { 2 } .$ If we want to test $H _ { 0 } : \mu = 0 ,$ we use the fact that under $H _ { 0 } ,$
$\sqrt { N } \bar { X } _ { N } \sim N \left( 0 , \sigma ^ { 2 } \right) .$ The usual approach is to $\sigma ^ { 2 }$ by the sample
variance $\widehat { \sigma } ^ { 2 }$ and approximate the distribution of $\sqrt { N } \bar { X } _ { N } / \widehat { \sigma }$ by the standard
normal distribution. The test uses the same normal quantiles as critical values.

In the present testing problem, the eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ collectively play a role
analogous to the unknown variance. We would like to normalize the test statis-
tic $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ by somehow dividing it by their estimators $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j }$ to get a limit distribution
which does not depend on the distribution of the data. It turns out that an
appropriate test statistic has the form

$$\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \langle U _ { N } \left( x , \cdot \right) , \widehat { \varphi } _ { j } \rangle d x$$
$$= \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ^ { - 1 } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \left\{ \int U _ { N } \left( x , t \right) \widehat { \varphi } _ { j } \left( t \right) d t \right\} d x .$$

As $N \rightarrow \infty ,$ the distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right)$ approaches the distribution of the
random variable

$$T ^ { 0 } \left( d \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } B _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( x \right) d x .$$

The distribution of $T ^ { 0 } \left( d \right)$ does not depend on any unknown parameters, but it
depends on $d .$ Critical values are given in Table 8.1. They were computed using
(8.18) with $N = 1 , 0 0 0$ and 10E5 replications. The selection of an "optimal"
$d$ is not always obvious. A simple rule is to use the cumulative variance ap-
proach: we choose the smallest $d$ for which $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } > 0 . 8 5 \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { D } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ,$ where $D$
is the maximum number of eigenvalues available from solving numerically the
eigenvalue problem. The number $d$ is typically much smaller than $D .$ While
$D$ is comparable to $N ,$ $d$ is often a single digit number. If $d$ is chosen too
large, the statistic $\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right)$ will involve division by small eigenvalues $\widehat { \lambda } _ { j } ,$ and
this will increase its variability. On the other hand, if $d$ is too small, the space
of functions which are orthogonal to the functions $\widehat { \varphi } _ { j } ,$ $j \leq d ,$ will be large. The
test statistic will not detect departures from stationarity which take place in
this large space.

Both tests described above are implemented in the package $f t s a .$ We
illustrate their application using the functional time series $p m _ { - } 1 0 _ { - } G R _ { - } s q r t$
introduced in Section 8.4. The call

T_stationary (pm_10_GR_sqrt$y)

is the default application of the test based on the statistic $\widehat { T } _ { N } .$ It produces the
following output

Monte Carlo test of stationarity of a functional time series
null hypothesis: the series is stationary

p-value = 0.082
N (number of functions) = 182
number of MC replications = 1000

The P-value of 8.2% indicates that the series can be treated as stationary.
Since the P-value is less than 10%, using a larger sample size might reveal some
nonstationarity due to seasonal effects. The last line indicates that $R = 1 0 E 3$
replications, the default value, were used to approximate the distibution of
the limit $T .$ The function $T \_ s t a t i o n a r y$ uses the approximation

$$\int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } B ^ { 2 } \left( x \right) d x x \approx I ^ { \star } \left( J \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \frac { Z _ { j } ^ { 2 } } { j ^ { 2 } \pi ^ { 2 } } ,$$

where the $J$ is an argument with the default value $J = 5 0 0 .$ A reader
interested in the derivation of this approximation is referred to Shorack
and Wellner (1986), pp. 210-211. By default, it uses the approximation
$T ^ { \star } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } I _ { j } ^ { \star } \left( J \right) ,$ where $d$ is chosen to explain 90% of the cumulative
variance. This default can be changed with the argument $\mathrm { c u m u l a t i v e } _ { - } \mathrm { v a r } .$

The argument pivotal chooses between the tests based on $\widehat { T } _ { N }$ and $\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right) .$
The default $p i v o t a l = F$ chooses $\widehat { T } _ { N } .$ The following code illustrates the
application of the test based on $\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right) .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="165" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

T_stationary (pm_10_GR_sqrt$y, J=100, MC_rep=5000, h=20,
pivotal=T)

It produces the (part) output

p-value = 0.1188
N (number of functions) = 182
number of MC replications = 5000

The argument h is the kernel bandwidth used in the estimation of the LRVF
$\sigma _ { \eta } ,$ see (8.16). As implemented in the function $T \_ s t a t i o n a r y$ , the pivotal
test does not use the critical values in Table 8.1, but rather replications of
$T ^ { \star 0 } \left( d \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } I _ { j } ^ { \star } \left( J \right)$ to approximate the null distribution of $\widehat { T } _ { N } ^ { 0 } \left( d \right) .$


### 8.7 Generation and estimation of the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ model us- ing package fda

This section shows how to generate and estimate the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ model intro-
duced in Section 8.2 using basic functions available in $R$ and the package fda.
In addition to enhancing the familiarity with the $F A R \left( 1 \right)$ model, it provides
additional practice in using many important function in that package. The
package $\mathrm { f a r } ,$ see Problem 8.6, provides a quicker route, but additional tech-
nical background is needed to understand the implementation it uses.

Our first objective is to generate a realization of the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ process (8.1)
of length $N = 2 0 0 .$ We use the kernel $\varphi \left( t , s \right) = \alpha s t$ with $\alpha = 9 / 4$ so that $\| \Phi \| =$
$\left\{ \iint \varphi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } = 3 / 4 ,$ cf. Example 8.8.1 in Section 8.8. As innovations,
we use

$$\varepsilon _ { n } \left( t \right) = Z _ { n 1 } \sin \left( \pi t \right) + \frac { 1 } { 2 } Z _ { n 2 } \cos \left( 2 \pi t \right) , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$$

where the $Z _ { n 1 }$ and $Z _ { n 2 }$ are independent standard normal random variables.
Simulation of any stationary time series typically requires a burn in period
which allows the simulated variables to settle into a stationary pattern. We
will thus generate 250 functions and discard the first 50. The following code
accomplishes our objective.

library (fda)

$m = 1 0 0$ # each function is observed at m+1 points, including 0
and 1
burnin=50 # the first 50 functions are a burn in period
N=200 # number of functions to simulate
$N 1 = N + b u r n i n$
$a l p h a = 9 / 4$
\# Create 2 matrices, whose entries are 0s.
\# Each column represents one function.
X <- $- \mathrm { m a t } r i x \left( r e p \left( 0 , \left( m + 1 \right) * N 1 \right) , m + 1 , N 1 \right)$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="166" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 8.9: Ten observations of a simulated FAR(1) process.</figcaption>

2

1

I

0

S

4

-2

-3

1
