Our next objective is to represent the functions forming the realization

### 8.8 Conditions for the existence of the $\mathrm { F A R } \left( 1 \right)$ process

This section requires familiarity with the theory developed in Chapters 10 and
11. We formulate conditions which ensure that the FAR(1) process defined
by equations (8.1) exists and is stationary. As noted in Section 8.1, for the
scalar $\mathrm { A R } \left( 1 \right)$ equations, the required condition is $| \varphi | < 1 .$ Stationary time
series models are typically defined by equations which relate the $X _ { n }$ to past
$X _ { k } ,$ $k < n .$ The first question that must be answered is whether there exists
a stationary stochastic process $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ which satisfies the specified equations.
Our goal in this section is to establish sufficient conditions for a stationary
FAR(1) process to exist. We will use the strongest form of stationarity known
as the strict stationarity. The following definition is valid if the $X _ { n }$ take values
in any metric space, in particular if they are scalars or functions in $L ^ { 2 } .$ To
lighten the notation, we set in this section, $\| \cdot \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \| \cdot \| .$

DEFINITION 8.8.1 A sequence $\left\{ X _ { n } , - \infty < n < \infty \right\}$ is said to be strictly sta-
tionary if for any $d$ and any time points $n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , \ldots , n _ { d } ,$ the distribution of the
vector $\left[ X _ { n _ { 1 } + h } , X _ { n _ { 2 } + h } , \ldots , X _ { n _ { d } + h } \right]$ does not depend on $h .$

THEOREM 8.8.1 $I f \| \Phi \| < 1 ,$ then there is a unique strictly stationary solution
to (8.1). This solution is given by

$$X _ { n } = \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) .$$
(8.19)

The series (8.19) converges almost surely:

$$\| X _ { n } - \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { m } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| \stackrel { a . s . } { \rightarrow } 0 , \quad \mathrm { a s } m \rightarrow \infty$$
(8.20)

and in the squared mean:

$$E \| X _ { n } - \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { m } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } \rightarrow 0 , \quad \mathrm { a s } m \rightarrow \infty .$$
(8.21)

PROOF: All random functions are defined on the same probability space $\Omega .$

We first establish the a.s. convergence of the sequence

$$X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) } = \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { m } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) .$$

The index $n$ is fixed. Set $b = \| \Phi \| ,$ so that for any $j \geq 0 ,$ $\| \Phi ^ { j } \| \leq \| \Phi \| ^ { j } = b ^ { j }$ .

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="172" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Observe that

$$E \left( \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } \| \Phi ^ { j } \| \| \varepsilon _ { n - j } \| \right) ^ { 2 } \leq \sum _ { j , k = 0 } ^ { \infty } \| \Phi ^ { j } \| \| \Phi ^ { k } \| E \| \varepsilon _ { 0 } \| ^ { 2 }$$
$$\leq E \| \varepsilon _ { 0 } \| ^ { 2 } \left( \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } b ^ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } < \infty .$$

It follows that $\sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } \| \Phi ^ { j } \| \| \varepsilon _ { n - j } \| < \infty$ a.s. This implies that $\sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } \| \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| <$
$\infty$ a.s. so the sequence $X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) }$ is Cauchy a.s. Its limit is denoted $X _ { n } .$

To establish (8.21), we work with the space $L ^ { 2 } \left( \Omega , L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) \right) ,$ which is a
Hilbert space with the inner product $E \langle X , Y \rangle ,$ $X ,$ $Y \in L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) .$ Thus, to
show that the sequence $X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) }$ has a limit in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \Omega , L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) \right) ,$ it again suffices
to check that it is Cauchy. By Lemma 12.1.1,

$$E \| \sum _ { j = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| _ { j = m } ^ { 2 } \sum _ { k = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } \sum _ { k = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } E \langle \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) , \Phi ^ { k } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - k } \right) \rangle$$
$$= \sum _ { j = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } E \| \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$$

Note that $E \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) = 0$ because the expectation commutes with bounded
operators. Therefore,

$$E \| \sum _ { j = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } \leq \left( \sum _ { j = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } \| \Phi ^ { j } \| ^ { 2 } \right) E \| \varepsilon _ { 0 } \| ^ { 2 } \leq E \| \varepsilon _ { 0 } \| ^ { 2 } \sum _ { j = m } ^ { m ^ { \prime } } b ^ { 2 j } .$$

The series (8.19) is strictly stationary and it satisfies equation (8.1), Prob-
$\mathrm { l e m } 8$ 8.16. Suppose $\left\{ X _ { n } ^ { \prime } \right\}$ is another strictly stationary sequence satisfying (8.1).
Then, iterating (8.1), we obtain, for any $m \geq 1 ,$

$$X _ { n } ^ { \prime } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { m } \Phi ^ { j } \left( \varepsilon _ { n - j } \right) + \Phi ^ { m + 1 } \left( X _ { n - m + 1 } ^ { \prime } \right) = X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) } + \Phi ^ { m + 1 } \left( X _ { n - m + 1 } ^ { \prime } \right) .$$

Therefore,

$$E \| X _ { n } ^ { \prime } - X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) } \| \leq \| \Phi ^ { m + 1 } \| E \| X _ { n - m + 1 } ^ { \prime } \| \leq b ^ { m + 1 } E \| X _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } \| .$$

Thus $X _ { n } ^ { \prime }$ is equal a.s. to the limit of $X _ { n } ^ { \left( m \right) }$ i.e. to $X _ { n } .$

.

The condition $\| \Phi \| < 1$ can be weakened to the condition that $\| \Phi ^ { j } \| < 1 ,$
for some $j \geq 0 ,$ Problems 8.17 and 8.18. In the scalar case both conditions are
equivalent. In practice, we assume $\| \Phi \| < 1 ,$ or an even stronger condition, as
the following example shows.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="173" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Functional time series" -->

EXAMPLE 8.8.1 Consider an integral Hilbert-Schmidt operator on $L ^ { 2 }$ defined
by

$$\Phi \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \int \varphi \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s , \quad x \in L ^ { 2 } ,$$
(8.22)

which satisfies

$$\int \int \varphi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < 1 .$$
(8.23)

Recall that the left-hand side of (8.23) is equal to the Hilbert-Schmidt norm
$\| \Phi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } .$ Since $\| \Phi \| \leq \| \Phi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ,$ we see that condition (8.23) is sufficient for the
existence of an FAR(1) process whose autoregressive operator is the integral
operator (8.22).
☐


### 8.9 Further reading and other topics

The theory of functional autoregressive processes is developed in depth in
Bosq (2000) who also provides references to several applications. Background
presented in Chapters 10 and 11 is needed to understand it. The FAR(1) pro-
cess is also referred to as the ARH(1) process; the "H" refers to an arbitrary
Hilbert space. The method described in Section 8.3 was introduced by Hynd-
man and Ullah (2007) and elaborated on in Hyndman and Booth (2008) and
Hyndman and Shang (2009). These references contain more details, the last
one contains a discussion of forecasting methods for functional time series. The
method of Section 8.4 was introduced by Aue $e t$ al. (2015) who provide large
sample arguments justifying it, as well as several extensions. Shang (2017)
proposes an updating approach to forecasting FTS.

Several chapters in Horváth and Kokoszka (2012) are devoted to functional
time series. They cover order determination and change point detection in
the FAR process as well as the theory of general weakly dependent stationary
functional processes. The central limit theorem for such processes states that
$N ^ { 1 / 2 } \left( \bar { X } _ { N } - \mu \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } G ,$ in the space $L ^ { 2 } ,$ where $G$ is a Gaussian random func-
tion. Gaussian random functions are defined in Chapter 11. It follows that
$\| \bar { X } _ { N } - \mu \|$ converges in probability to zero, i.e. the functional sample mean is
a consistent estimator of the mean function for weakly dependent stationary
processes. Estimation of the long-run variance is also studied in greater detail,
see also Horváth et al. (2013). Theory of stationarity tests for functional time
series is developed in Horváth et al. (2014). Tests of trend stationarity are
proposed in Kokoszka and Young (2016).

An important aspect of time series analysis is the spectral methodology. It
is based on the decomposition of a series into sine and cosine functions. Ex-
tension of these methods to FTS's are complex, but they provide some addi-
tional insights. The relevant theory was developed by Panaretos and Tavakoli
(2013b, 2013a). Using spectral methods, Hörmann et al. (2015) introduced

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="174" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

orthonormal functions, which they call dynamic functional principal compo-
nents. These basis functions lead to optimal expansions for dependent station-
ary processes (EFPC's introduced in Chapter 3 are optimal if the observed
functions are iid).


### 8.10 Chapter 8 problems

8.1 It can be shown that if $| \varphi | < 1 ,$ then the scalar mean zero AR(1) process
$X _ { n } = \varphi X _ { n - 1 } + \varepsilon _ { n }$ admits the expansion

$$X _ { n } = \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } \varphi ^ { j } \varepsilon _ { n - j } ,$$

where the series converges in mean square, e.g. $\lim _ { m \rightarrow \infty } E | X _ { n } - \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { m } \varphi ^ { j } \varepsilon _ { n - j } | ^ { 2 }$
$= 0 .$ Using the above expansion, find the autocovariance and the autocorrela-
tion functions.

8.2 The least squares estimate $\widehat { \varphi }$ in the scalar AR(1) process $X _ { n } = \varphi X _ { n - 1 } +$
$\varepsilon _ { n }$ is defined as the the value of $\varphi$ which minimizes $S _ { N } \left( \varphi \right) = \sum _ { n = 2 } ^ { N } \left( x _ { n } - \right.$
$\left. \varphi x _ { n - 1 } \right) ^ { 2 } .$ Find $\widehat { \varphi } .$

8.3 The scalar $\mathrm { A R } \left( 1 \right)$ process $X _ { n } = \varphi X _ { n - 1 } + \varepsilon _ { n }$ with $\varphi = 1$ and $X _ { 0 } = 0$ is
called the random walk. Show that this process is not stationary.

8.4 Recall the partial inverse operator $C _ { p } ^ { - 1 }$ defined in Section 8.2. Find func-
tions $x$ and $y$ such that $C _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left( C \left( x \right) \right) \neq x$ and $C \left( C _ { p } ^ { - 1 } \left( y \right) \right) \neq y .$

of the simulated FAR(1) process considered in Section 8.7. It assumes that
the code displayed in that section has been entered.

variance explained by the first four EFPC's:

axes=F)

b " ,

tralian fertility rate curves observed from 1921 to 2006. The value $X _ { n } \left( t \right)$ is
the count of live births in year $n$ per 1,000 females of age $t \in \left[ 1 5 , 4 9 \right] .$ The top

panel shows the predictions obtained using the "exact method" and the bot-
tom panel those obtained with the "recursive method". In the exact method,
the forecasts of $X _ { N + 1 } , X _ { N + 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N + h }$ are computed all at once using the
data $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ In the recursive method, the one step ahead prediction
$\widehat { X } _ { N + 1 | N }$ is obtained and treated as a real observation to predict $X _ { N + 2 } .$ This
process is continued until the prediction of $X _ { N + h }$ is obtained.

Reproduce Figure 8.12 using the following code:

library ("ftsa") ; par (mfrow=c (2, 1) )
\# Plot the historical data in gray

ylab = "Births per 1, 000 females",
main = "Forecasted fertility rates (2007-2026) ")
\# Plot the forecasts in rainbow color
plot (forecast (ftsm (Australiasmoothfertility, order = 2), h =
20), add = TRUE)
legend ("topright", c("2007", "2026") , $c o l = c \left( ^ { \prime \prime } r e d ^ { \prime } \right. ,$ "blue") ,
lty = 1)

\# Repeat the above steps but use recursive forecasts

"Births per 1, 000 females", main = "Recursive Forecasts")
plot (ftsmiterativeforecasts (Australiasmoothfertility,
components = 2, iteration = 20), add = TRUE)
legend ("topright", c("2007", "2026") , col $= c \left( ^ { n } r e c ^ { j } \right. ,$ "blue") ,
$\left. l t y = 1 \right)$

What conclusions can you draw from these predictions? Comment on the
difference between the predictions obtained using the two methods.

8.10 This problem compares the Hyndman-Ullah method described in Sec-

## 9 Spatial functional data and models

This chapter provides an introduction to the analysis of functions indexed by
spatial locations. While there are other types of spatial functional data, this
chapter considers data which take a form of a collection of curves, $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) ,$ $k =$
$1 , 2 , \ldots , K ,$ where the $\mathrm { s } _ { k }$ are spatial locations. Each $t \mapsto X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right)$ is a curve, a
function. For example, the $\mathrm { S } _ { k }$ can be locations of meteorological stations, and
$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t \right)$ can be some measurement, for example temperature or wind speed at
time t. In traditional spatial statistics, the observations are scalars, for example
temperatures at the same time. The most common problem of spatial statistic
is to predict a value at a location at which no measurement is available using
the available measurements. Prediction in the context of spatial data is called
kriging. In our temperature example, if we consider $t$ to be day, it would
mean that we want to predict the value $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 0 } , t _ { 0 } \right)$ for some location $\mathrm { s } _ { 0 }$ using
the values $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } , t _ { 0 } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } , t _ { 0 } \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { K } , t _ { 0 } \right) .$ For a large spatial domain, like
a continent, it is clear that $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 0 } , t _ { 0 } \right)$ depends also on the values $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t \right)$ for
$t \neq t _ { 0 } .$ For example, high temperatures early in the spring at some locations
may predict high summer time temperatures at different locations. Similar
arguments may apply to wind speeds, strong winds at certain times and certain
locations may predict strong winds at later times at different locations. It is
therefore important to present fundamentals of prediction of the whole curve
$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 0 } , \cdot \right)$ from a collection of curves $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } , \cdot \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } , \cdot \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { K } , \cdot \right) .$ This is the
objective of this chapter.

In Section 9.1, we present the fundamental concepts of spatial statistics
of scalar observations. Section 9.2 introduces the framework of this chapter
by defining stationary and isotropic random fields of functions. Functional
kriging and the estimation of the mean function of spatially indexed curves
are considered, respectively, in Sections 9.3 and 9.4. Implementation of these
procedures in the R package $\mathrm { g e o f d }$ is presented in Section 9.5. The chap-
ter concludes with Section 9.6 which discusses some extensions and provides
references to a few more advanced topics.

<!-- PageNumber="179" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="180" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 9.1 Fundamental concepts of spatial statistics

There are many excellent monographs and textbooks on spatial statistics.
The monograph of Cressie (1993) provides a comprehensive account of the
subject. The more recent monograph of Cressie and Wikle (2011) focuses on
spatio-temporal data emphasizing Bayesian methods, which are also treated
in detail by Banerjee et al. (2004). The book of Schabenberger and Gotway
(2005) is a modern textbook with end-of chapter problems. Initial chapters of
Wackernagel (2003) and Sherman (2011) also provide accessible introductions
to the subject of spatial statistics. The collection of Gelfand $e t$ al. (2010)
presents recent developments in the field. This section summarizes the main
concepts of spatial statistics. In the following sections of this chapter, we will
see how they are extended to functional data. In this section, we assume that
all data are scalars.


#### Geostatistical spatial data

$\mathrm { A }$ sample of geostatistical spatial data has the form

$$\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) , \mathrm { s } _ { k } \in \mathrm { S } , k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N \right\} .$$

The spatial domain $\mathrm { S }$ is typically a subset of the two-dimensional plane or
sphere. The points $\mathrm { s } _ { k }$ at which observations are available are generally scat-
tered over $\mathrm { S }$ in some irregular manner. In some applications, e.g. satellite
pictures, these points form a grid.

The observed value $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ is viewed as a realization of a random variable,
so $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathrm { S } \right\}$ is a scalar random field. Just as in time series analysis,
stationary random fields play a fundamental role in modeling spatial data. To
define arbitrary shifts, we must assume that $\mathrm { S }$ is either the whole Euclidean
space $\mathbb{R} ^ { d } ,$ or the whole sphere. The random field $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathrm { S } \right\}$ is then strictly
stationary if

$$\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } + \mathrm { h } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } + \mathrm { h } \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { m } + \mathrm { h } \right) \right\} \stackrel { d } { = } \left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { m } \right) \right\}$$
(9.1)

for any points $\mathrm { s } _ { 1 } , \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } , \ldots , \mathrm { s } _ { m } \in \mathrm { S }$ and any shift $\mathrm { h } .$ If we assume only that the
means $E X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ and the covariances $\mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } \right) \right)$ do not depend on s,
then the field is called second-order stationary. For such a field, we define the
covariance function

$$C \left( \mathrm { h } \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } \right) . \right.$$

In spatial statistics, the concept of intrinsic stationarity is useful. The field
$\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathrm { S } \right\}$ is said to be intrinsically stationary if $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right]$ does
not depend on s. $\mathrm { A }$ second-order stationary field is intrinsically stationary, cf.
(9.2). The converse is not true, Problem 9.4. If $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathrm { S } \right\}$ is intrinsically
stationary, we define the semivariogram by

$$\gamma \left( \mathrm { h } \right) = \frac { 1 } { 2 } \mathrm { V a r } \left[ X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right] .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="181" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

(The variogram is defined as $2 \gamma \left( \cdot \right)$ The semivariogram of a second order
stationary field with the covariance function $C \left( \cdot \right)$ is given by

$$\gamma \left( \mathrm { h } \right) = C \left( 0 \right) - C \left( \mathrm { h } \right) .$$
(9.2)

If $C \left( \mathrm { h } \right)$ depends only on the length $h$ of $\mathrm { h } ,$ we say that the random field
is isotropic. The covariance function of an isotropic random field is typically
parametrized as

$$C \left( h \right) = \sigma ^ { 2 } \phi \left( h \right) , \quad h \geq 0 , \phi \left( 0 \right) = 1 .$$

The function $\phi$ is then called the correlation function. It quantifies the strength
of linear dependence between observations distance $h$ apart. The following
correlation functions are frequently used. The powered exponential correlation
function is defined by

$$\phi \left( h \right) = \exp \left\{ - \left( \frac { h } { \rho } \right) ^ { p } \right\} , \quad \rho > 0 , 0 < p \leq 2 .$$

If $p = 1 ,$ this correlation function is called exponential, if $p = 2 ,$ it is
called Gaussian. A very general family of correlation functions is the so-called
Matérn class. The Matérn class correlation functions are defined as

$$\phi \left( h \right) = \frac { 2 ^ { 1 - \nu } } { \Gamma \left( \nu \right) } \left( \frac { h } { \rho } \right) ^ { \nu } K _ { \nu } \left( h / \rho \right) , \quad \rho > 0 , \nu > 0 ,$$

where $K _ { \nu }$ is is a special function called the modified Bessel function, see Stein
(1999). The function $K _ { \nu }$ decays monotonically and approximately exponen-
tially fast; numerical approximations show that $K _ { \nu } \left( s \right)$ practically vanishes if
$s > \nu .$

It follows from Problem 9.2 that every correlation function must be non-
negative definite in the sense that $\sum _ { i , j = 1 } ^ { m } a _ { i } a _ { j } \phi \left( d \left( \mathrm { s } _ { i } , \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) \right) \geq 0 ,$ where $d \left( \mathrm { s } _ { i } , \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right)$
is the distance between $s _ { i }$ and $s _ { j } .$ Such a nonnegative definite function $\phi$ is
called valid. It is difficult to verify directly that any specific correlation func-
tion is valid. Theory of spatial Fourier transforms must be used, see Stein
(1999).

Estimation of the covariance function proceeds through the estimation
of the semivariogram. Very often isotropy is assumed, and we explain the
procedure in this case. First, an empirical variogram is computed at several
available lags $h > 0 .$ There are several versions of the empirical variogram.
The simplest estimator is given by

$$\widehat { \gamma } \left( d \right) = \frac { 1 } { | N \left( d \right) | } \sum _ { N \left( d \right) } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) \right) ^ { 2 } ,$$
(9.3)

where $N \left( d \right)$ is the set of pairs $\left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ approximately distance $d$ apart, and
$| N \left( d \right) |$ is the count of pairs in $N \left( d \right) .$ The function $\widehat { \gamma } .$ It is approximated
by a function $C \left( 0 \right) - \quad C \left( d \right) ,$ cf. (9.2), where $C$ is a valid covariance function.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="182" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

For example, if we wish to fit an exponential model, we must find $\sigma$ and $\rho$
such that

$$\widehat { \gamma } \left( d \right) \approx \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( 1 - \exp \left\{ - d / \rho \right\} \right) .$$

The details of this process and its variations are described in spatial statistics
textbooks. Section 9.5 illustrates this process by suitable graphs in the context
of functional data.


##### Kriging

In spatial spatistics, the term kriging refers to linear prediction. Using the
observed values $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) ,$ we want to predict the value $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ at a location s
which is not one of the $\mathrm { s } _ { k } ,$ $1 \leq k \leq N ,$ at which data are available. The
procedure is named after $D .$ $\mathrm { G } .$ Krige (1919-2013), a South African mining
engineer. We describe it in the simplest case which assumes that the data are
observations of a second order stationary random field. Such a field can be
represented as

$$X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = \mu + e \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \quad \mu = E X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , E e \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = 0 .$$

We want to find weights $w _ { k }$ such that the predictor

$$\widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = \mu + \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu \right)$$
(9.4)

minimizes the expected squared error of prediction $E \left( \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right) ^ { 2 }$ . It can
be shown, Problem 9.5, that

$$\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) : = E \left( \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right) ^ { 2 } = C \left( 0 \right) + \sum _ { k , j = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } w _ { j } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) - 2 \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } \right) .$$
(9.5)

Minimizing $\sigma ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = \sigma ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } ; w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { N } \right)$ with respect to the weights
$w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { N }$ shows that they must satisfy the system of $N$ linear equations

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { N } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) w _ { j } = C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } \right) , \quad k = 1 , 2 , \ldots N ,$$
(9.6)

Problem 9.6. If the matrix $\left[ C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) \right. ,$ $1 \leq j ,$ $k \leq N$ is nonsingular, these
kriging weights are unique. In practice, the unknown covariances must be
replaced by their variogram-based estimates.


##### Kriging the mean

If the observations $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$ form a simple random sample, i.e. are iid,
with common mean p, then the most natural estimator of $\mu$ is the sample
mean $\bar { X } _ { N } = N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { k } .$ Intuitively, in case of iid observations, there is no

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="183" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

difference between them, and each contains the same information about the
unknown mean p. This intuition has been formalized in classical estimation
theory discussed in many textbooks, see e.g. Chapter 7 of Casella and Berger
(2002). If the observations are taken at spatial locations, then, in most cases,
observations at close by locations are similar and those at locations far apart
may be very different. This intuition is captured by the spatial covariances
which decay with distance. For this reason, if $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { N } \right)$ are
☒
observations of a second-order stationary random field, the mean $\mu$ is esti-
mated by the weighted average

$$\widehat { \mu } = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) .$$

If a location $\mathrm { s } _ { \ell }$ is close to another location $\mathrm { s } _ { k } ,$ then adding $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ to the sample
does not increase much the information about the mean already available in
$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ because $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ is close to $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) .$ Both $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ and $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ should thus
☒
get smaller weights than values at locations further away. We now explain
how the weights are computed assuming $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right\}$ is second order stationary.
The first requirement is that $\widehat { \mu }$ be an unbiased estimator, i.e. $E \widehat { \mu } = \mu .$ This
implies that $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } = 1 .$ Then, the criterion that determines the $w _ { k }$ is that
$E \left( \widehat { \mu } - \mu \right) ^ { 2 }$ be minimum subject to $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } = 1 .$ It can be shown that the
weights can be found by solving the system of $N + 1$ equations:

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { N } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) w _ { j } - r = 0 , k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N , \quad \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { j } = 1 ,$$
(9.7)

where $r \quad i s \quad a n$ additional unknown. Details of the argument leading the above
equations are explained in Problem 9.7.


##### Other spatial data structures

Our focus in this chapter will be on geostatistical functional data, so the in-
troduction presented above focused on geostatistical scalar data. Other data
structures however naturally occur in spatial statistics. Regional data are mea-
sured not at a point, but over a region. For example, the count of shooting
deaths in a county in a given year is not associated with a point in space but
with the whole region, a county. Lattice data are available at a regular grid.
For example, a satellite picture of a region consists of values at pixels on a
rectangular grid. Each pixel can represent, e.g., the intensity of vegetation
over a region it captures. In this sense, lattice data can be considered as re-
gional data, and both terms are sometimes used interchangeably. Spatial point
process data are concerned with locations at which events occur. For example,
the points at which lightning strikes occur during a storm form a pattern. To
describe such patterns, point processes are used. If a value is associated with
each point, e.g. if the intensity of a discharge can be determined, then point

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="184" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

process data can be, in principle, viewed as geostatistical data. However, in
the analysis of geostatistical data, the locations are treated as fixed; for point
process data, they are considered random.


### 9.2 Functional spatial fields

We focus on geostatistical functional data. Just as in the case of scalar data
of this type, the model for functional geostatistical data is the random field
$\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathrm { S } \right\} ,$ with the difference that now each $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ is a random function.
We assume that each $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ is an element of $L ^ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) .$ The value of $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$
at time $t$ is denoted by $X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) .$ We assume that each $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ is square integrable,
i.e.

$$E \| X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \int E X ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) d t < \infty .$$

If we assume that $\mathrm { S }$ is the whole Euclidean space, we can define strict station-
arity by condition (9.1). The equality of distributions is now in the product
space $\left( L ^ { 2 } \right) ^ { m }$ rather than in $\mathbb{R} ^ { m } .$ If this is the case, then $E \| X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 }$ does not
depend on s. Square integrability and strict stationarity imply that the mean
function

$$\mu \left( t \right) = E X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right)$$

and the covariances

$$C \left( \mathrm { h } ; t , u \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } ; u \right) \right.$$

exist and do not depend on s. When working with functional geostatisti-
cal data, it is important to keep in mind that the function $\mathrm { h } \mapsto C \left( \mathrm { h } ; t , u \right)$
is nonnegative definite, see (9.18) in Problem 9.2, only if $t =$ u. In that
case, $C \left( \cdot ; t \right) : = C \left( \cdot ; t , t \right)$ is the spatial covariance function of the scalar field
$\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) \right\} .$

We will also assume that the covariances are isotropic, i.e. $C \left( \mathrm { h } ; t , u \right) =$
$C \left( h ; t , u \right)$ depends only on $h = \| \mathrm { h } \| .$ The assumptions listed above can be
weakened, but to keep the exposition simple, in the remainder of this chapter,
unless stated otherwise, we assume that the field $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right\}$ is square integrable,
$s t r i c t l y$ stationary and isotropic.


### 9.3 Functional kriging

We observe functions $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ at spatial locations $\mathrm { s } _ { 1 } , \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } , \ldots , \mathrm { s } _ { N } .$ Suppose $\mathrm { s }$ is a
different location, and we want to predict the function $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ using the func-
tions $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 1 } \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } \right) , \ldots , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { N } \right) .$ This is the kriging problem introduced for

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="185" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

scalar data in Section 9.1. In the functional setting, the available data can
be represented as a matrix of scalars

$$\left[ X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t _ { j } \right) , k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , N , j = 1 , 2 , \ldots , J \right] ,$$ ☒ ☒

assuming all functions are observed at the same points $t _ { j } .$ In some situations,
the points $t _ { j }$ may depend on the location $\mathrm { s } _ { k } ,$ so the scalar data points are
$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { k j } \right) .$ There are many approaches to computing a predictor $\widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ of the
☒ ☒
unobserved curve $X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) .$ We discuss one of them, with additional references
☒

given in Section 9.6.

Taking a functional point of view, we treat each curve $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ as an indi-
☒
visible data object. We want to determine the weights $w _ { k }$ for the predictor
of the form (9.4), but we keep in mind that the $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ are now functions. In
☒
the least squares criterion, we must replace $\left( \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right) ^ { 2 }$ by the squared $L ^ { 2 }$
☒ ☒
norm. We thus want to find weights $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { N }$ which minimize

$$E \| \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = E \int \left( \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) \right) ^ { 2 } d t .$$ ☒ ☒

Observe that

$$\| \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \| \mu + \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 }$$
$$= \langle \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu \right) - \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu \right) , \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { \ell } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) - \mu \right) - \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu \right) \rangle$$
$$= \sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } w _ { \ell } \langle X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu , X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) - \mu \rangle$$
$$- 2 \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \langle X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu , X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu \rangle + \langle X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu , X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu \rangle .$$ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒ ☒

Introduce functional spatial covariances

$$C \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \right) = E \left[ \langle X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \mu , X \left( \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \right) - \mu \rangle \right] .$$ ☒
(9.8)

Using this notation, we see that

$$E \| \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { k , \ell = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } w _ { \ell } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) - 2 \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } \right) + C \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } \right) .$$ ☒

Finding the kriging weights thus formally reduces to minimizing (9.5), just as
for scalar observations, but using the functional covariances (9.8). Therefore,
the weights $w _ { k }$ are found by solving the system of equations

$$\sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { N } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) w _ { \ell } = C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } \right) , \quad k = 1 , 2 , \ldots N .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="186" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

The argument leading to the above system is the same as in the scalar case,
cf. (9.6) and Problem 9.6. It only requires computing the partial derivatives
and equating them to zero.

To implement this approach in practice, we must estimate the covariances
$C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ and $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } \right) .$ We now explain how this can be done. Using the as-
sumptions of stationarity and isotropy, we see that

$$C \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \right) = E \int \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } ; t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) d t$$
$$= \int C \left( \| \mathrm { s } - \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \| ; t \right) d t ,$$

where

$$C \left( h ; t \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } + \mathrm { h } ; t \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) \right) , \quad h = \| \mathrm { h } \| ,$$

is the spatial covariance function of the scalar field $\left\{ X \left( \cdot ; t \right) \right\}$ obtained by fixing
$t .$ For each t, the covariance function $h \mapsto C \left( h ; t \right)$ can be estimated by any
method that is applicable to covariance estimation for scalar spatial data. $\mathrm { A s }$
noted in Section 9.1, this is typically done by estimating the semivariogram.

The kriging predictor is often expressed in a different way. We want to
find a predictor $X ^ { \star } \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ of the form $\widetilde { X } ^ { \star } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } \lambda _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) ,$ subject to the
condition

$$\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } \lambda _ { k } = 1 .$$
(9.9)

By contrast, equations (9.6) do not imply that $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { j } = 1 .$ In many applica-
tions, the weights $w _ { j }$ are very close to the weights $\lambda _ { j } ,$ and the predicted func-
tion $\widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ and $X ^ { \star } \left( \mathrm { s } \right)$ are also very close. This is illustrated in Problem 9.12.


### 9.4 Mean function estimation

We now explain how to estimate the mean function of spatially indexed curves
$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) , 1 \leq k \leq N .$ Estimation of the mean function is needed to implement
the kriging method described in Section 9.3. As explained in Section 9.1, even
for scalar data, a proper approach is to use a weighted average rather than
a simple average $N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) .$ In the functional context, a weighted
average takes the form

$$\widehat { \mu } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) ,$$
(9.10)

and the problem becomes how to compute the optimal weights $w _ { k } .$ As in Sec-
tion 9.3, in (9.10) we treat the curves $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ as indivisible statistical objects,
and so we apply one weight to the whole curve. One could consider weights

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="187" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 9.1: Gray lines: annual temperature curves at 35 locations shown in Figure 9.2 (averaged over several decades). Dashed line: simple average of these curves. Continuous black line: mean function estimated by functional kriging.</figcaption>

20

10

0

-10

-20

-30

0

100

200

300

Day

</figure>


$w _ { k } \left( t \right) ,$ defined as functions, but the simple approach we describe in this sec-
tion is easy to implement, and produces estimates which significantly improve
on the simple average. Figure 9.1 illustrates the difference between the simple
average $\left( w _ { k } = 1 / N \right)$ and the weighted average we are about to derive, using
annual temperature curves in Canada. It is helpful to refer to Figure 9.2 which
shows the locations at which the curves are available. There are plenty of lo-
cations in the southern part of Canada, especially in the densely populated
South-East of the country, and very few in the North. If we postulate the
model $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \varepsilon \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) ,$ then $\mu$ should represent the typical annual
temperature profile for the whole country. The simple average will mostly
reflect temperature curves in the region where there are many stations, i.e.
mostly in the South. To obtain a more informative kriged mean function,
stations far in the North must receive larger weights. Some stations in the
south will receive even slightly negative weights. In this section, we present
the derivation of these weights, Problem 9.13 shows how to implement this
procedure in $\mathrm { R } .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="188" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

We define the optimal weights as those that minimize

$$E \| \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu \| ^ { 2 } = \int \left\{ \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right\} ^ { 2 } d t$$
(9.11)

subject to the condition $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { n } w _ { k } = 1$ which ensures that $E \widehat { \mu } = \mu .$ As in the
scalar case, the method of the Lagrange multiplier, Problem 9.7, shows that
the weights $w _ { k }$ must satisfy the following system of $N + 1$ linear equations:

$$\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } = 1 , \quad \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { n } \right) - r = 0 , n = 1 , 2 , \ldots N ,$$
(9.12)

in which $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { N }$ are $N + 1$ unknowns, Problem 9.9. The functional
covariances $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { n } \right)$ can be estimated as explained in Section 9.3. Another
approach, based on Functional variogram, is explained in Problem 9.10.


### 9.5 Implementation in the R package geofd

Methods for spatial functional data discussed in this chapter, as well as several
other methods, are implemented in the $\mathrm { R }$ package $\mathrm { a e o f } \mathrm { d } .$ In this section, we
provide an example of the code which implements the kriging method derived
in Section 9.3. It includes the code for the estimation of the functional mean.
In addition to the package fda, the implementation we present requires two
more packages: fda. usc and maps. For illustration, we use the well-known
Canadian weather data set available in the package fda. The assumptions of
stationarity and isotropy may not hold for this data set, but the predictions
are still fairly good, and our objective is merely to provide guidance on how
to use the relevant packages.

The initial lines of the code are:

library (fda) ; library (fda. usc) ; library (geofd) ; library (maps)

data ("CanadianWeather")

dailyAv <- CanadianWeather$dailyAv
dim (dailyAv)
Temperature <- dailyAv [ , , 1]
Precipitation <- dailyAv [ , , 2]
log10Precip
<- $d a i l y A v \left[ , , 3 \right]$
place <- CanadianWeather$place
coordinates <- CanadianWeather$coordinates
coordinates <- coordinates [ , 2 : 1]
coordinates [, 1] <- - coordinates [, 1]

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="189" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 9.2: The 35 Canadian weather stations, Calgary marked with a red square.</figcaption>
</figure>


geo. dist. 35 <- dist (coordinates)
Day <- 1:365
n <- dim (Temperature) [2] ; nt <- dim (Temperature) [1]

Next, we produce a map of Canada and mark the 35 locations at which
data are available. After that, "Calgary" is removed from sample locations
and treated as a location at which functions are to be predicted. It is marked
with a red square in Figure 9.2.

map ('world', ylim=c (42, 78) , xlim = c (-170, -40) )
map ( 'world' , region="Canada", add=TRUE)
points (coordinates, col='black ' , pch=19)
i. 0 <- which (place="Calgary")
coord.0 <- coordinates [i. 0, ]
points (coord . 0 [1] , coord. 0 [2], pch= 15)

In the next step, using the Fourier basis, we define the functional object
Tempe. 34 which contains the temperature curves for the remaining 34 sta-
tions. The code below also plots these temperature curves.

Tempe. 34 <- Temperature [, -i. 0]
coord.34 <- coordinates [-i.0, ]
K <- min (99, max (49, 1 + 4 * round (sqrt (nt) ) ) )
fourier.basis <- create. fourier . basis (rangeval=range (Day) ,
nbasis=K)
temp.fd.Fb.34 <- Data2fd (argvals=Day, y=Tempe. 34, basisobj=
fourier.basis)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="190" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

temp.fd.Fb.0 <- Data2fd (argvals=Day, y=Temperature [, i. 0 ] ,
basisobj=fourier.basis)
plot (temp. fd. Fb. 34, col="grey",
xlab="Day", ylab="Temperature (degrees C) ",
main="Average daily temperatures")
lines (temp.fd.Fb.0, lwd=2)

The following code fits the exponential model to the empirical variogram
and plots Figure 9.3. Binning is used to reduce the chance variability of the
variogram cloud; each point in the variogram cloud is based on only one pair
of points $\left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) .$ We group these pairs into bins that contain points of roughly
the same distance $d \approx \| \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \| ,$ and then compute the empirical variogram
(9.3) with $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ replaced by $\| X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) \| .$ Such a variogram is
called the trace variogram in the package $\mathrm { q e o f d } .$

\# computing L2 norms between functions, using Fourier basis
expansions

L2norm.Fb.34 <- dist(t(temp.fd.Fb.34$coefs) ) ^2
\# Calculating the empiricial trace bin variogram
emp . trace . vari . 34 <- trace. variog (coords=coord. 34, L2norm=
as.matrix (L2norm.Fb.34), bin=TRUE)

\# fitting an exponential vriogram
sigma2.0 <- quantile (emp.trace.vari.34$v, 0.75)
phi.0
<- quantile (emp.trace.vari. 34$Eu.d, 0.75)
fit.vari.34 <- variofit (emp.trace.vari. 34, ini.cov.pars=c (
sigma2.0, phi.0), cov.model="exponential")

plot (as.dist (emp.trace.vari.34$Eu.d) , L2norm. Fb. 34, col="grey",
xlab="Geographical distances", ylab="L2 distances",
main="Empirical variogram")
points (emp. trace. vari. 34$u, emp. trace. vari . 34$v, col="black", pch
=19)
lines(fit.vari.34,col="black",lwd=2)
legend ( "topleft", c ("Variogram cloud", "Binned variogram", "
Fitted variogram") ,
col=c(8,1,1), lwd=c(-1,-1,2), pch=c (1,19,-1) )

Now we compute the kriging weights $w _ { \ell } .$ First, using the exponential var-
iogram model, we find the estimates of the covariances $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ and $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } \right) ,$
where s is the location of Calgary and the $\mathrm { s } _ { k }$ are the remaining 34 locations.
Since we use the functional version of model (9.4), we also need to find an
estimate of the mean function p. This is done in after finding the weights $w _ { \ell } .$
\# computing the covariances

hat. C. 34 <- cov. spatial (emp. trace. vari. 34$Eu.d,
cov.model= fit. vari. 34$cov.model,
cov.pars=fit . vari. 34$cov.pars)
geo. dist. 0.34 <- as.matrix (geo.dist.35) [-i. 0, i. 0]
hat.C.0 <- cov.spatial (geo.dist.0.34,

$$\mathrm { c o v } . \mathrm { m o d e l } = \mathrm { f i t } . \mathrm { v a r i } . 3 4 \mathrm { S c o v } . \mathrm { m o d e l } _ { \prime } ,$$

\# computing the weights for the mean of 34 stations
inv.hat.C.34 <- solve (hat.C.34)
v. 34 <- apply (inv. hat. C. 34, 1, sum)
w.m.34 <- v.34/ sum (v. 34)
\# computing the weights for kriging
w0.k <- solve(hat.C.34, hat.C. 0)
w. k <- w0.k + w.m. 34 * (1-sum (w0 . k) )
\# computing the weighted functional mean of 34 stations
mean.temp.Fb.34 <- mean(temp.fd.Fb.34)
w.mean.temp.Fb.34 <- mean.temp.Fb.34
w.mean.temp.Fb.34$coefs <- apply(temp.fd.Fb. 34$coefs, 1,
weighted.mean, w=w.m. 34)
temp.fd.Fb.34.ctrd <- temp.fd.Fb.34
temp. fd. Fb. 34. ctrd$coefs <- temp. fd. Fb. 34. ctrd$coefs
\- matrix (w.mean.temp.Fb. 34$coefs, ncol=34, nrow=77, byrow=FALSE)

### 9.6 Other topics and further reading

In addition to the method outlined in Section 9.3, Delicado et al. (2010) give
references to several other methods of kriging functional data, and review
research on regional and point process functional data. $A$ relatively simple
kriging method they discuss, which gives predictions as good as more complex
methods can be summarized as follows. The predictor has the form

$$\widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \left( t \right) X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right)$$

where the unknown functions $w _ { k } \left( \cdot \right)$ satisfy $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } \left( t \right) = 1 ,$ for each t. The ob-
served functions $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right)$ and the unknown weight functions $w _ { k } \left( \cdot \right)$ are expanded
using some basis system:

$$X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right) = \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } c _ { k m } B _ { m } \left( t \right) , \quad w _ { k } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } b _ { k m } B _ { m } \left( t \right) .$$

In particular, one can use the EFPC's $\widehat { v } _ { m }$ as the $B _ { m } .$ The coefficient ma-
trix $\mathrm { B } = \left[ b _ { k m } , 1 \leq k \leq N , 1 \leq m \leq M \right]$ is also found by minimizing
$E \| \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$ This approach is similar to the method of co-kriging
used for multivariate functional data, e.g. Wackernagel (2003). It is more
computationally expensive than the method explained in Section 9.3, but
it gives more accurate predictions in the example considered by Delicado
et al. (2010). We have assumed in this chapter that $E X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) = \mu \left( t \right)$ does
not depend on the location s. Caballero et al. (2013) and Menafoglio et al.
(2013) show how to perform kriging under a more flexible assumption that
$E X \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) = \sum _ { \ell = 1 } ^ { L } \beta _ { \ell } \left( t \right) f _ { \ell } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) .$ In Section 9.3 we described a method of the esti-
mation of the mean functions of spatially distributed curves. Other methods,
as well as the estimation of the FPC's are presented in Chapter 17 of Horváth
and Kokoszka (2012). The problem of testing the equality of two mean func-
tions, each defined for functions observed over a different spatial region, is
considered in Gromenko and Kokoszka (2012).

In the remainder of this section, we describe in some detail several problems
involving inference for spatially indexed functional data which require applica-
tions of more advanced statistical methodology and mathematical derivations.
We therefore do not describe all details, but provide enough information to
give the reader an idea what a problem is and how it is solved. All problems
in the remainder of this section lead to specific significance tests for functions,
or time series of functions, indexed by spatial locations.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="195" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

<figcaption>FIGURE 9.6: Gray lines represent ionosonde measurements obtained at ob- servatories located in mid-latitude northern hemisphere, with the scale on the left-hand side. The black line represents the observed solar radio flux with the scale on the right-hand side.</figcaption>

#### Global cooling in the ionosphere

The first test is motivated by an interesting and extensively studied prob-
lem of space physics. The account presented here is based on Gromenko and
Kokoszka (2013) and Gromenko et al. (2016). We first describe the space
physics problem, and then explain the idea of the test. A chief difficulty is that
the functions are observed with sometimes huge gaps. An analogous problem
for iid functions is studied by Kraus (2015).

Increased concentration of greenhouse gases in the upper atmosphere is
associated with global warming in the lower troposphere (the atmosphere
roughly below 10 km). Roble and Dickinson (1989) suggested that the increas-
ing amounts of these radiatively active gases, mostly $\mathrm { C O } _ { 2 }$ and $\mathrm { C H } _ { 4 } ,$ would
lead to a global cooling in the ionosphere (atmosphere roughly 300 km above
the Earth's surface). Rishbeth (1990) pointed out that this would result in
a thermal contraction of the ionosphere. The height of the ionosphere can
be approximately computed using data from a radar-type instrument called
the ionosonde. Relevant measurements have been made for many decades by
globally distributed ionosondes. In principle, these observations could be used
to quantitatively test the hypothesis of Roble and Dickinson (1989). The dif-
ficulty in testing the contraction hypothesis comes from several sources. The
height of the ionosphere depends on magnetic coordinates, the season, long
term changes in the strength and direction of the internal magnetic field, and,
most importantly, on the solar cycle; more solar radiation leads to greater
ionization. This is illustrated in Figure 9.6. Another difficulty stems from the

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="196" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 9.7: Number of available stations in the mid-latitude northern hemi- sphere.</figcaption>

40

Number of stations

30

20

10

0

1960

1970

1980

1990

2000

2010

</figure>


fact that ionosonde records are not complete. Most observation stations do not
operate continuously for many decades. They start and end operation at dif-
ferent times, some of them are out of service for many years, or even decades.
In the mid-latitude northern hemisphere, there are 81 ionosonde stations, but
at any given time, data from no more than 40 are available, as shown in Fig-
ure 9.7. This means that the estimation methods designed for fully observed
curves cannot be used, as they require complete records to compute various
integrals. More complex methods that work for incomplete records are needed.

Let $Y \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; \tau _ { i } \right)$ be the original record at location $\mathrm { s } _ { k } ,$ measured from 1958 to
2015, possibly with long gaps. The set of all locations is $\left\{ \mathrm { s } _ { k } , 1 \leq k \leq K \right\} ,$
and the set of time points at which measurements may be available is
$\left\{ \tau _ { i } , 1 \leq i \leq T \right\}$ in Gromenko $e t$ al. (2016) these are months from January
1958 to December 2015. The following spatio-temporal model is postulated:

$$Y \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) = \mu \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) + \varepsilon \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) + \theta \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) ,$$
(9.13)

where $\mathrm { s }$ is a generic location in a region of interest, and $\tau$ is continuous time.
$A$ simple form of the mean function relevant to the space physics problem is

$$\mu \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) = \beta _ { 1 } + \beta _ { 2 } \tau + \beta _ { 3 } \mathrm { S R F } \left( \tau \right) + \beta _ { 4 } M \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) ,$$

where $\mathrm { S R F } \left( \tau \right)$ is the solar radio flux, cf. Fig. 9.6, and $M \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right)$ is a suitable
function computed from the coordinates of the internal magnetic field. The
interest lies in the estimation of the mean function, and testing if it contains
a linear trend, i.e. testing $H _ { 0 } : \beta _ { 2 } = 0 .$ The function $\mu \left( \cdot , \cdot \right)$ is treated as an un-
known deterministic functional parameter. The second term, $\varepsilon \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right) ,$ describes

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="197" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

the spatio-temporal variability away from the mean function. Stochastic mod-
eling of this term is needed to develop inferential procedures. The term $\theta \left( \mathrm { s } ; \tau \right)$
represents a random error, which can be associated with measurement error.
The details of the estimation and testing procedures are too complex to de-
scribe here. The conclusion is that $\beta _ { 2 }$ is significantly negative, confirming the
hypothesis of global ionospheric contraction. The software to perform the test
is available and can be used to test for the presence of global trends in other
data of this type, for example in near-surface temperatures.


#### Detection of change in the mean function

We now turn to the problem of testing for a change in the mean function of
functional time series observed at a number of spatial locations. The presen-
tation is based on Gromenko et al. (2016). General theory for change point
detection for scalar data is presented in Csörgő and Horváth (1997), several
extensions to functional data are presented in Horváth and Kokoszka (2012).
In the spatio-temporal setting we consider here, the data are assumed to
follow the model

$$X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } , t \right) = \mu _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) + \varepsilon _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) .$$

In most applications, $n$ denotes year, s and $t$ time within a year. For a fixed
s, $\left\{ X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , n = 1 , 2 , \ldots \right\}$ is a time series of functions, one function per year.
While we assume that the curves are observed densely in time, they are only
observed at a finite number of spatial locations $\left\{ \mathrm { s } _ { k } : k = 1 , \ldots , K \right\} .$ The goal
is to determine if the mean functions, $\mu _ { n } ,$ are the same across n, or if there is
a change in the mean at some unknown time point (year). In other words, we
aim to evaluate the null hypothesis

$$H _ { 0 } : \mu _ { 1 } = \mu _ { 2 } = \cdots = \mu _ { N }$$
$$\mathrm { a g a i n s t } \quad H _ { A } : \mu _ { 1 } = \ldots \mu _ { n ^ { * } } \neq \mu _ { n ^ { * } + 1 } = \cdots = \mu _ { N } .$$

The alternative of a sudden change at a single year $n ^ { * }$ is to be viewed as a
mathematical approximation. As with all testing problems, the null hypothesis
of no change is tested, and the alternative specifies the violations of the null
hypothesis which will be detected with the highest power. The tests described
below, will also detect different violations of $H _ { 0 } ,$ including gradual changes
taking place over a few years.

To carry out the tests, we assume that the covariance structure of the $\varepsilon _ { n }$
is separable, a test of this assumption is described below. Separability implies
that

$$\mathrm { C o v } \left( \varepsilon _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) , \varepsilon _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } ; t ^ { \prime } \right) \right) = v \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right) u \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \right) .$$

The decomposition above is unique only up to a constant, so we assume, wlog,
that $\int v \left( t , t \right) d t = 1 .$ Gromenko et al. (2016) do not require a specific procedure
for estimating $v \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right)$ and $\mathrm { U } = \left\{ u \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k ^ { \prime } } \right) \right\} ,$ instead formulating their tests
assuming arbitrary consistent estimators. Examples of how to estimate these
quantities are given in their appendix, as well as in Aston et al. (2016) and
Constantinou et al. (2015). The first test involves using a temporal FPCA,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="198" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

normalizing by the corresponding eigenvalues, and then pooling across space.
The tests statistics is

$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 1 } = \frac { 1 } { N ^ { 2 } } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { w } _ { k } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { i } ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { r = 1 } ^ { N } \langle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { r } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \frac { r } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle .$$

The weights, $\widehat { w } _ { k } ,$ can be chosen however the user prefers, but specific recom-
mendations are given. It has now become well recognized in the FDA com-
munity that dividing by estimated eigenvalues can cause stability problems
with certain data. Thus a second test statistic was also proposed which omits
normalizing by the $\widehat { \lambda } _ { i } :$

$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 2 } = \frac { 1 } { N ^ { 2 } } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { w } _ { k } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \sum _ { r = 1 } ^ { N } \langle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { r } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \frac { r } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle .$$

For $p$ large, $\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 2 }$ is essentially an approximation of the third and final test
statistic

$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 2 } ^ { \infty } = \frac { 1 } { N ^ { 2 } } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { w } _ { k } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \widehat { \lambda } _ { i } ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { r = 1 } ^ { N } \| \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { r } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \frac { r } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$$

Each statistic has a slightly different limiting distribution. In particular, under
$H _ { 0 }$

$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 1 } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } w \left( k \right) \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { d } \int B _ { i k } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t$$
$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 2 } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } w \left( k \right) \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { d } \lambda _ { i } \int B _ { i k } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t$$
$$\widehat { \Lambda } _ { 2 } ^ { \infty } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } w \left( k \right) \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { i } \int B _ { i k } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t ,$$

where $B _ { i k } \left( t \right)$ are Brownian bridges which satisfy

$$\mathrm { C o v } \left( B _ { i k } \left( t \right) , B _ { i ^ { \prime } k ^ { \prime } } \left( t ^ { \prime } \right) \right) = 1 _ { i = i ^ { \prime } } \min \left\{ t , t ^ { \prime } \right\} \left( 1 - \min \left\{ t , t ^ { \prime } \right\} \right) \sigma \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k ^ { \prime } } \right) .$$

These asymptotics can be used for choosing rejection regions with proper
Type 1 error. To do so, one can use Monte Carlo, though Gromenko et
al. (2016) also provide a normal approximation which works well when
considering a relatively large number of spatial points. These tests can
be implemented using the scpt package in $\mathrm { R } ,$ which is available from
http://www.personal.psu.edu/ mlr36/codes . html and includes
an example code for implementing the methodology.

If a change point is detected, its location can be identified as the year

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="199" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

Locations close to 2 and 3 do not show a large change nor a consistent pattern.</figcaption>
