</figure>


when a test statistics attains maximum. Gromenko et al. (2016) applied the
above test procedures (with suitable finite sample calibration) to test if the
precipitation pattern over the midwest US states has changed. All tests led
to the same conclusion: one change point in second half of the sixties. The
pattern of the change is shown in Fig. 9.8. The biggest changes are in the area
around Michigan Lake and south-west of the lake. To visualize the spatial
distribution of change, the authors used the spatial field

$$\widehat { \phi } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) = \| \widehat { \mu _ { 1 } } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - \widehat { \mu } _ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \| ,$$

where

$$\widehat { \mu } _ { 1 } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) = \widehat { r } ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \widehat { r } } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) , \quad \widehat { \mu } _ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) = \left( N - \widehat { r } \right) ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { n = \widehat { r } + 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } ; t \right) .$$

They performed kriging with the exponential covariance model to obtain the
heat map shown in Fig. 9.8. The application of the significance tests confirms
that the heat map shows a statistically significant change over a region, not a
variation in the magnitude of change which may be due to chance.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="200" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


##### Separability $t e s t s$

The second order structure of a random field of functions is described by
the covariance function $\sigma \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } ; t , t ^ { \prime } \right) = \mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( \mathrm { s } , t \right) , X \left( \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } , t ^ { \prime } \right) \right) .$ Theoretical and
computational aspects of most procedures can be significantly simplified if one
can assume that

$$\sigma \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } ; t , t ^ { \prime } \right) = u \left( \mathrm { s } , \mathrm { s } ^ { \prime } \right) v \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right) ,$$
(9.14)

that is, if the spatio-temporal covariance function factors into the product of a
purely spatial and purely temporal covariance functions. The above condition
is, for example, required to develop the change point methodology described
above. If (9.14) holds, we say that the functional random field is separable.
In particular, the spatial dependence structure is the same for any time $t$ t.
Tests whose null hypothesis is (9.14) have been proposed by Liu et $a l .$ (2014),
Aston et al. (2016) and Constantinou et al. (2015). We discuss the latter two
approaches here.

We begin by discussing two different procedures for estimating $v \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right)$ and
$\mathrm { U } = \left\{ u \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k ^ { \prime } } \right) \right\} .$ Aston $e t$ al. (2016) used partial trace operators to estimate
these quantities, while Constantinou et al. (2015) combined bases expansions
with a "flip-flop" approach, which basically iterates between estimating $v$ and
$\mathrm { U } .$ Partial trace operators are used to eliminate either the temporal or spatial
component of the covariance, in particular, define the estimated covariance
function as

$$\widehat { \sigma } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k } ^ { \prime } , t , t ^ { \prime } \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t \right) X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k ^ { \prime } } , t ^ { \prime } \right) .$$

(In the above formula, and in the remainder of this section, we assume that
the mean function is zero.) Then the spatial covariance is estimated using

$$\widehat { \mathrm { U } } _ { k k ^ { \prime } } = \int \widehat { \sigma } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k } ^ { \prime } , t , t \right) d t ,$$

and the temporal covariance is estimated using

$$\widehat { v } \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right) = \frac { \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \widehat { \sigma } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t , t ^ { \prime } \right) } { \sum _ { k } \int \widehat { \sigma } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t , t \right) d t } .$$

The normalization above ensures that the trace of $v \left( t , t ^ { \prime } \right)$ is normalized to be
1, though other normalizations are possible. One thing to note is that the
full covariance $\widehat { \sigma }$ does not actually need to be estimated, as one can move to
estimating the $\widehat { \mathrm { U } }$ and $\widehat { v }$ directly.

The second approach to estimating $v$ and $U$ comes form Constantinou $e t$
al. (2015) and begins $w i t h$ basis expansions. In particular, one approximates
each temporal curve using $J$ basis functions:

$$X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t \right) \approx \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \xi _ { j n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="201" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

The basis $e _ { j } \left( t \right)$ can either be deterministic or data driven, such as FPCA. Test-
ing separability of $X _ { n }$ is then translated into testing separability of $\xi _ { j n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) ,$
so that the null becomes

$$H _ { 0 } : \mathrm { C o v } \left( \xi _ { j n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) , \xi _ { j ^ { \prime } n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k ^ { \prime } } \right) = \mathrm { V } _ { j j ^ { \prime } } \mathrm { U } _ { k k ^ { \prime } } . \right.$$

However, this is now a multivariate problem and we can utilize multivari-
ate methods for estimating $\mathrm { v }$ and $\mathrm { U } ,$ in particular, the maximum likelihood
estimators, assuming the data are Gaussian, satisfy

$$\widehat { \mathrm { V } } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \Xi _ { n } \widehat { \mathrm { U } } ^ { - 1 } \Xi _ { n } ^ { \top } \quad \widehat { \mathrm { U } } = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \Xi _ { n } ^ { \top } \widehat { \mathrm { V } } ^ { - 1 } \Xi _ { n } ,$$

where $\Xi _ { n } = \left\{ \xi _ { j n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) \right\}$ is a $J \times K$ matrix. To compute these estimators, one
chooses an initial value, for example $\widehat { \mathrm { U } } _ { 0 } = \mathrm { I } ,$ and then iterates between the
two estimates, hence the name "flip-flop method".

With these estimators in hand, one can now carry out a number of different
tests. To construct these tests first define

$$\Sigma : = \mathrm { E v e c } \left( \Xi _ { n } \right) \mathrm { v e c } \left( \Xi _ { n } \right) ^ { \top } ,$$

where vec is the vectorization operator, which stacks the columns of a matrix
into a vector. Under $H _ { 0 }$ one can show that

$$\Sigma = \mathrm { V } \otimes \mathrm { U } . ,$$

where $\bigotimes$ is the Kronecker product. Constantinou $e t$ al. (2015) consider three
different approaches

$$T _ { W } = N \left( \widehat { \mathrm { V } } \otimes \widehat { \mathrm { U } } - \widehat { \Sigma } \right) ^ { \top } \widehat { \mathrm { W } } ^ { + } \left( \widehat { \mathrm { V } } \otimes \widehat { \mathrm { U } } - \widehat { \Sigma } \right) ,$$
$$T _ { L } = N \left( J \log \det \left( \widehat { \mathrm { U } } \right) + K \log \det \left( \widehat { \mathrm { V } } \right) - \log \det \left( \widehat { \Sigma } \right) \right) ,$$
$$T _ { F } = N \| \widehat { \mathrm { V } } \otimes \widehat { \mathrm { U } } - \widehat { \Sigma } \| _ { F } ^ { 2 } .$$ ☒

The first test uses the Moore-Penrose generalized inverse of the estimated
covariance matrix of $\widehat { \mathrm { V } } \otimes \widehat { \mathrm { U } } - \widehat { \Sigma } ,$ $\widehat { \mathrm { W } } ^ { + } ,$ to normalize the difference and create
a Wald type test statistic. The form for $\widehat { \mathrm { W } }$ is complex, but can be found
in Constantinou $e t$ al. (2015). The generalized inverse is necessary since the
symmetry of the matrices and the non-uniqueness of $\mathrm { U }$ and $\mathrm { v }$ create a number
of linear constraints. This turns out to be a fairly unstable test statistic in
terms of Type 1 error, and works well only for very small $J$ and $K .$ The second
test is derived from the likelihood ratio and had previously been explored
in the multivariate literature in Lu and Zimmerman (2005); Mitchell et al.
(2006). This test is also quite unstable, but only when using a chi-squared
distribution as an asymptotic approximation. Mitchell $e t$ al. (2006) provided
a Monte-Carlo method which provides a much more stable (in terms of type 1
error) test. The last test forgoes normalizing the test statistic and instead uses

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="202" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

an asymptotic distribution given by a weighted sum of chi-squares. This test
is very stable and, in the settings considered by Constantinou $e t$ al. (2015)
exhibited excellent power. Lastly, we note that Aston et al. (2016) provided
a testing procedure that focussed on testing the separability in the directions
dictated by the eigenfunctions. They utilized a bootstrap approach instead of
asymptotic distributions, and their individual tests can be pooled to construct
a global test for evaluating separability overall.

The tests described above assume that the functions $X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } , \cdot \right)$ are indepen-
dent across n. Extension of the tests of Constantinou $e t$ al. (2015) to functional
time series are are developed in Constantinou $e t$ al. (2016).


#### Spatio-temporal extremes

In this section, we summarize the work of French et al. (2016) which deals with
the computation of probabilities of heat waves. The raw data are spatially-
indexed time series of daily temperature measurements, $X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , j \right) ,$ denoting the
temperature at a spatial location $\mathrm { s } _ { k } ,$ $k = 1 , \cdots , K ,$ on day $j .$ As argued above,
due to the natural annual climate cycle, for each $\mathrm { s i t } \epsilon ,$ we partition $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { i } , j \right) \right\}$
into years, and view the resulting 365-dimensional vectors as samples from a
functional time series:

$$X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; \cdot \right) = \left\{ X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t _ { i } \right) , i \in \left\{ 1 , 2 , \ldots 3 6 5 \right\} \right\} .$$
(9.15)

Here, $t \mapsto X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } ; t \right)$ is the temperature curve at site $\mathrm { s } _ { k }$ for year n, viewed as a
function of time $t$ in days. In contrast to the setting of previous sections, the
data used by French et al. (2016) are not historical records, but data generated
by a computer climate model. These artificial data are of much higher quality
than historical records; there are no gaps, and the daily records are available
at 16,100 locations forming a grid covering much of North America. It is, at
this point, not clear how to extend the methodology of French $e t$ al. (2016) to
historical records. The advantage of using computer model data is that they
are predicted future temperatures (French et al. (2016) work with the period
2041-2070), which are more relevant to the prediction of future heat waves.
On the other hand, these data do depend on a model, and the poor quality,
geostatistical historical records are the real data.

French $e t$ al. (2016) propose many functionals that can quantify a heat
wave, but here we focus on one specific approach that explains the general
idea. A heat wave is characterized by its spatial and temporal extents and by
its intensity. The intensity is typically quantified by a threshold. Public health
concerns call for a fixed threshold, like $1 0 5 ^ { 0 } F .$ However, in climate studies of
large spatial regions, with many climatic zones, such a fixed threshold is not
appropriate. Also the variability of temperatures depends greatly on the geo-
graphical location, with coastal locations exhibiting much smaller variability
than locations far away from large bodies of water. It is therefore reasonable

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="203" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

to work with standardized temperatures

$$Z _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) = \frac { X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) - \bar { X } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) } { \mathrm { S D } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) } ,$$
(9.16)

where

$$\bar { X } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) = \frac { 1 } { N } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) \mathrm { a n d } \mathrm { S D } ^ { 2 } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) = \frac { 1 } { N - 1 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \left( X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) - \bar { X } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) \right) ^ { 2 } .$$
(9.17)

If the $Z _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right)$ exceed a fixed threshold z, e.g. $z = 2 ,$ for a number of neigh-
boring locations and over a period of time, then we have observed a heat wave
(the $Z _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right)$ are practically normal). The severity of a heat wave increases
with the size of the region, the duration and the threshold $z$ that is exceeded.
Suppose the $X _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right)$ are maximum daily temperatures, and set

$$Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { j } \right) = \frac { 1 } { \ell } \sum _ { t _ { j } - \ell < t _ { i } \leq t _ { j } } Z _ { n } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { i } \right) .$$

This is the average maximum temperature over the $\ell$ days preceding day $t _ { j } .$
Next, define

$$Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) = \min _ { 1 < k < K } Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , t _ { j } \right) .$$

If $Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) > z ,$ then the average maximum temperature over $\ell$ days over $K$
(neighboring) locations exceeds, z; this corresponds to a heat wave defined by
this specific functional. We are interested in the probability of a heat wave in
any given year. We assume that this probability does not depend on year n.
We thus want to compute, for some relevant $z > 0 ,$

$$p \left( z \right) = P \left( \exists j : Z _ { n } \left( t _ { j } \right) > z \right) = P \left( \max _ { 1 \leq j \leq J } Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) > z \right) = P \left( M _ { J } > z \right) ,$$

where $J = 3 6 5 ,$ and

$$M _ { J } \stackrel { d } { = } M _ { J , n } : = \max _ { 1 < j \leq J } Z _ { n } ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right) .$$

The concatenated sequence $Z ^ { \star } \left( t _ { j } \right)$ is stationary and weakly dependent, so (see
e.g. Beirlant $e t$ al. (2006), Chapter 10), there are sequences $a _ { J }$ and $b _ { J }$ such
that

$$\lim _ { J \rightarrow \infty } P \left( \frac { M _ { J } - b _ { J } } { a _ { J } } \leq z \right) \rightarrow H \left( z \right) ,$$

where $H$ is a univariate Generalized Extreme Value distribution function. The
function $H$ depends on three parameters, which can be estimated, together
with the constants $a _ { J }$ and $b _ { J } ,$ using now standard $\mathrm { R }$ implementations.

Figure 9.9 shows examples of regions corresponding to 50, 150 and 450
neighboring locations. Figure 9.10 shows a map of the probability of a heat
wave for $d = 5 0$ for three durations $\ell ,$ with (a) corresponding to $\ell = 3 ,$ (b)

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="204" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 9.9: A map of the neighborhood structures for different locations using 50, 150, and 450 nearest neighbors. Each x marks a neighborhood cen- troid and the sequences of grey shading mark the extents of the increasing neighborhood sizes.</figcaption>

$\times$

$\tau$

X

$\times$

</figure>


to $\ell = 1 0 ,$ and (c) to $\ell = 3 0 .$ When $\ell = 3 ,$ there is a surprisingly high
probability of localized heat waves over the Labrador Peninsula. Such short
heat spells may occur with probability approaching 50%, that is on average
every second year. While our EVT approximation may break down for such
high probabilities, it is nevertheless obvious that that part of Canada will see
heat spells much more frequently than in the past. Generally, we see that the
area around the Hudson Bay will experience an increased frequency of hot
spells lasting a few days. There is a noticeable drop in the probability of such
a heat wave around the Rocky Mountain range. The probability is also very
low along the Eastern seaboard of the United States. Increasing the duration
to $\ell = 1 0$ days, dramatically reduces the probability of a heat wave of the
corresponding magnitude. The reader will note the different probability scale.
Many parts of Canada once again show an increased probability of a heat
wave of this magnitude, as well as parts of Iowa and Illinois, certain regions
in Texas, and, most visibly, the Pacific Ocean off the Southern California
coast. Increasing the duration to approximately 1 month $\left( \ell = 3 0 \right) ,$ causes the
probability of a heat wave to drop even further; generally, throughout North
America, heat waves of this magnitude will occur with probability of less than
1%, i.e. once per one hundred years, on average. Over the Canadian plains and
the Canadian Rockies, this probability increases only slightly to about 1.5%.
There are two patches, in Arizona and Southern Texas, with probabilities
elevated to 2-3%.

<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

### 9.7 Chapter 9 problems

9.1 [Elementary properties of the covariance function] Suppose $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { d } \right\}$
is a second order stationary scalar random field and $C$ is its covariance func-
tion defined in Section 9.1. Verify the following properties: 1) $C \left( 0 \right) \geq 0$ 2)
$\left. C \left( - h \right) = C \left( h \right) ; 3 \right)$ $| C \left( \mathrm { h } \right) | \leq C \left( 0 \right) .$

9.2 [Valid covariance function] Suppose $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { d } \right\}$ and $C$ are the same
as in Problem 9.1. Show that for any $m ,$ any locations $\mathrm { s } _ { 1 } , \mathrm { s } _ { 2 } , \ldots , \mathrm { s } _ { m }$ and any
real numbers $a _ { 1 } , a _ { 2 } , \ldots , a _ { m } ,$

$$\sum _ { i , j = 1 } ^ { m } a _ { i } a _ { j } C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { i } - \mathrm { s } _ { j } \right) \geq 0 .$$
(9.18)

We have referred to a function $C$ with the above property as nonnegative
definite. In spatial statistics, a function $C$ satisfying (9.18) is called valid.
The covariance function is thus always valid, yet the term a valid covariance
function is often used. This is because very often some preliminary estimates or
models lead to expressions for $C$ which do not satisfy (9.18). In such situation,
some further regularization is needed to obtain a valid covariance function.

9.3 [Semivariogram] Suppose $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) , \mathrm { s } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { d } \right\}$ and $C$ are the same as in $P r o b -$
$\mathrm { l e m } 9 . 1 .$ Verify relation (9.2).

9.4 Suppose $\varepsilon _ { i } ,$ $i \geq 1 ,$ are iid random variables with mean zero and variance
$\sigma ^ { 2 } .$ The random walk is defined as $X \left( j \right) = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { j } \varepsilon _ { i } ,$ $j \geq 1 .$ It can be viewed
as random field defined on the grid $\left\{ 1 , 2 , 3 , \ldots \right\} .$ For such a domain, one can
consider only nonnegative shifts $h ,$ so $X$ is stationary if for every $h \geq 0 ,$
$\mathrm { C o v } \left( X \left( j \right) , X \left( j + h \right) \right)$ does not depend on $j ,$ and intrinsically stationary if
$\mathrm { V a r } \left[ X \left( j \right) - X \left( j + h \right) \right]$ does not depend on $j .$ (In both cases the mean function
cannot depend on j.) Show that the random walk is intrinsically stationary,
but not stationary.

9.5 Show that predictor (9.4) satisfies $E \left[ \widehat { X } \left( \mathrm { s } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right] = 0$ and verify relation
(9.5).

9.6 Show that the weights $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { K }$ minimizing (9.5) satisfy equations
(9.6).

9.7 [Equations for kriging the mean] Suppose $\left\{ X \left( \mathrm { s } \right) \right\}$ is second order station-
ary. To derive equations (9.7), we must find $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , \ldots , w _ { N }$ which minimize
$E \left( \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - \mu \right) ^ { 2 }$ subject to the constraint $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { N } w _ { k } = 1 .$ Problems of
this type are solved using the method of Lagrange multipliers, which is ex-
plained in many multivariate calculus textbooks, see e.g. Chapter 7 of Apostol

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="207" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Spatial functional data and models" -->

(1957). If there is only one constraining equation, this method can be formu-
lated as follows:

If a function $f \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { n } \right)$ has an extremum subject to the constraint
$g \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { n } \right) = 0 ,$ then at any point $\mathrm { x } _ { 0 }$ at which an extremum is reached,
there exists $\lambda$ such that

$$\frac { \partial f } { \partial x _ { \ell } } \left( \mathrm { x } _ { 0 } \right) = \lambda \frac { \partial g } { \partial x _ { \ell } } \left( \mathrm { x } _ { 0 } \right) , \quad \ell = 1 , 2 , \ldots n .$$

To find potential extremal points $\mathrm { x } _ { 0 } ,$ we thus set up a system consisting of
the $n$ equations above and the equation $g \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots , x _ { n } \right) = 0 .$

Use the above method to derive equations (9.7).

9.8 This problem illustrates the application of equations (9.7) in a very sim-
ple setting. Consider a second order stationary random field $\left\{ X \left( s \right) , s \in \mathbb{R} \right\} ,$
☒
the domain is the real line. Suppose the covariance function is given by $C \left( h \right) =$
$\exp \left( - h \right) .$ Observations are available at three locations: $s _ { 1 } = 0 ,$ $s _ { 2 } = 1 ,$ $s _ { 3 } = 1 0 ,$

and their values are $X \left( s _ { 1 } \right) = 1 , X \left( s _ { 2 } \right) = 2 , X \left( s _ { 3 } \right) = 3 .$ Find the weights
☒
$w _ { 1 } ,$ $w _ { 2 } ,$ $w _ { 3 }$ and compute the estimated mean $\widehat { \mu } .$ Provide intuition explaining
why $\widehat { \mu }$ is greater than the sample mean $\left( 1 + 2 + 3 \right) / 3 = 2 .$

9.9 Show that weights $w _ { k }$ which minimize (9.11) satisfy equations (9.12).
Hint: work through Problem 9.7 first.

9.10 Define the functional semivariogram by

$$\gamma \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) = \frac { 1 } { 2 } E \| X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$$

Show that under the assumptions of Section 9.2 (stationarity and isotropy),

$$\gamma _ { f } \left( h \right) = C _ { f } \left( 0 \right) - C _ { f } \left( h \right) ,$$
(9.19)

where

$$h = \| \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \| , \quad \gamma _ { f } \left( h \right) = \gamma \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) , \quad C _ { f } \left( h \right) = C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) ,$$

with the functional spatial covariances $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ defined by (9.8).

The above relations imply that the covariances $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right)$ can be estimated
as follows. First we compute an empirical variogram $\widehat { \gamma } _ { f } ,$ using e.g. (9.3), with
$\left( X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) \right) ^ { 2 }$ replaced by $\| X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } \right) - X \left( \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$ Then we fit a parametric
☒
covariance model to $\widehat { \gamma } _ { f }$ using relation (9.19). Finally, we set $C \left( \mathrm { s } _ { k } , \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \right) = C _ { f } \left( h \right)$
with $h = \| \mathrm { s } _ { k } - \mathrm { s } _ { \ell } \| .$

9.13 This problem presents the steps required to computed the weighted

Use the following code to estimate and plot the variogram:

temp. fd <- fdata (t (Temperature) , argvals=Day, names=list $\left( m a i n = \right.$
Canadian Weather. Temperature", xlab="Day", ylab="
Temperature") )

\# computing L2 norms between functions, using fda. usc library
L2norm.raw <- as.dist(metric.lp(temp.fd, lp=2) ) ^2
\# Calculating the empirical trace bin variogram
emp. trace. vari <- trace. variog (coords=coordinates,
L2norm=as.matrix (L2norm.raw) , bin=TRUE)
\# fitting an exponential variogram
sigma2.0 <- quantile (emp.trace.vari$v, 0.75)
phi. 0 <- quantile (emp.trace.vari$Eu.d, 0.75)
fit.vari <- variofit (emp.trace.vari, ini.cov.pars=c (sigma2.0,
phi. 0) , cov.model="exponential")
plot (geo. dist, L2norm. raw, col="grey")
points (emp. trace. vari$u, emp. trace. vari$v, col="red", pch=19)
lines (fit.vari, col="blue", lwd=2)

Use the following code to compute the weights and plot them as a function
of the station index.

hat.C <- cov. spatial (emp. trace. vari$Eu. d, cov. model= fit. vari$
cov.model,

cov.pars=fit . vari$cov.pars)
inv.hat.C <- solve (hat.C)
v <- apply (inv.hat. C, 1, sum) ; w <- v/ sum (v)
plot (w)
abline (h=1/35, col="gray", lwd=2) ; abline (h=0, col="blue")

lightyellow")

## 10 Elements of Hilbert space theory

In Chapter 3, we described the main elements of the framework of random
functions which is commonly used in FDA. In this Chapter, and in Chapter 11,
we present the concepts introduced in Chapter 3 in much greater detail. These
two chapters are recommended to readers who would like to gain a fairly solid
grounding in the mathematical theory underlying FDA, but who do not wish
to study extensive, and even more detailed, mathematical monographs

Most FDA tools make heavy use of Hilbert space theory, and thus it is
important to gain comfort using some tools from functional analysis, a field
of mathematics that studies objects in abstract vector spaces, rather than in
the Euclidean space. We do not intend (nor could we) cover all of the ma-
terial found in a traditional functional analysis course. Instead, we present
basic definitions and facts of Hilbert space theory which have been exten-
sively used in functional data analysis. A reader with some familiarity with
multivariate statistics should be able to quickly pick up the core ideas in this
chapter. The problems of this section are simple exercises intended to help the
reader become familiar with these definitions and theorems by using them. The
textbook of Debnath and Mikusinski (2005) is an accessible exposition that
presents a broader picture and applications to other areas of mathematics and
science. The monograph of Akhiezier and Glazman (1993) is an extensive sur-
vey of the classical theory. Chapter 4 of Rudin (1987) provides a concise and
rigorous introduction to the most important ideas. There are dozens of other
textbooks on Hilbert spaces; the monographs of Hsing and Eubank (2015) is
an exposition taylored specifically to the needs of researchers working in the
field of $\mathrm { F D A } .$


### 10.1 Hilbert space

A vector space is defined over a field of scalars. In this book, the scalars
are either the real or the complex numbers. A vector space is a set $\mathcal{V}$ whose
elements are called vectors, and in which two operations are defined: addition
and scalar multiplication. The following are the axioms of a vector space.

AXIOM 10.1.1 To every pair of vectors $\mathrm { x }$ and $\mathrm { y } ,$ there corresponds a vector

<!-- PageNumber="211" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="212" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

$\mathrm { x } + \mathrm { y } ,$ and

$$x + y = y + x , x + \left( y + z \right) = \left( x + y \right) + z$$

The space $\mathcal{V}$ contains a unique vector 0 such that $x + 0 = x$ for every $\mathrm { x } \in \mathcal{V} .$
For each $\mathrm { x } \in \mathcal{V} ,$ there is a unique vector $- \mathrm { x }$ such that $\mathrm { x } + \left( - \mathrm { x } \right) = 0 .$
☒

AXIOM 10.1.2 To every pair $\left( a , \mathrm { x } \right) ,$ where $a$ is $a$ scalar and $\mathrm { x }$ is a vector,
there corresponds $a$ vector $a x ,$ and

$$1 \mathrm { x } = \mathrm { x } , \quad a \left( b \mathrm { x } \right) = \left( a b \right) \mathrm { x } ;$$
$$a \left( \mathrm { x } + \mathrm { y } \right) = a \mathrm { x } + a \mathrm { y } , \quad \left( a + b \right) \mathrm { x } = a \mathrm { x } + b \mathrm { x } .$$

All the usual intuitive properties follow from these axioms. For example, if 0
is the scalar $z e r o ,$ then $0 \mathrm { x } = 0 .$

A set $\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } \subset \mathcal{V}$ is called a linear subspace , or sometimes a vector subspace of
$\mathcal{V}$ if it is a vector space with respect to the operations defined on $\mathcal{V} .$ Clearly,
$\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } \subset \mathcal{V}$ is a subspace if and only if for any scalars a, $b$ and x, $\mathrm { y } \in \mathcal{V} _ { 1 } ,$ $a \mathrm { x } + b \mathrm { y } \in$
$\mathcal{V} _ { 1 } .$

We say that a set of vectors $\mathrm { e } _ { 1 } , \ldots , \mathrm { e } _ { d }$ of $\mathcal{V}$ are linearly independent if

$$a _ { 1 } \mathrm { e } _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } \mathrm { e } _ { 2 } + \ldots + a _ { d } \mathrm { e } _ { d } = 0 \Rightarrow a _ { 1 } = \cdots = a _ { d } = 0 .$$

Furthermore, if every element of $\mathcal{V}$ can be expressed as a linear combination
of $\mathrm { e } _ { 1 } , \ldots , \mathrm { e } _ { d } ,$ then we call that set of vectors a basis of $\mathcal{V}$ and say that $\mathcal{V}$ has
dimension $d .$

In the following, we drop the bold face to denote vectors, as it will readily
follow from the context which objects are vectors and which are scalars. We
will reserve the bold face for finite dimensional vectors (and matrices), as is
usual in statistics. This book is primarily concerned with infinite dimensional
vector spaces, i.e. those that do not have a finite basis.

DEFINITION 10.1.1 $A$ vector space, $\mathcal{V} ,$ is called an inner product space if for
each pair of vectors, $\left( x , y \right) ,$ there is a scalar $\langle x , y \rangle ,$ called the inner product of
$x$ and $y ,$ which satisfies, for all $\left( x , y \right) \in \mathcal{V} \times \mathcal{V} ,$

a) $\langle x , y \rangle = \bar { \langle y , x \rangle }$

b) $\langle a _ { 1 } x _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } x _ { 2 } , y \rangle = a _ { 1 } \langle x _ { 1 } , y \rangle + a _ { 2 } \langle x _ { 2 } , y \rangle ;$

c) $\langle x , x \rangle \geq 0$ with equality only for $x =$ 0.

The bar in $\bar { \langle y , x \rangle }$ denote the complex conjugate. An inner product in a vector
space over the field of real numbers is symmetric: $\langle x , y \rangle = \langle y , x \rangle .$

In an inner product space, one can define the inner product norm by

$$\| x \| = \sqrt { \langle x , x \rangle } .$$

The following proposition follows from the definitions stated above

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="213" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

PROPOSITION 10.1.1 The inner product defines a norm, $\| \cdot \| ,$ on the inner
product space which has the following properties:

a) $\| a x \| = | a | \| x \|$

b) | $| \langle x , y \rangle | \leq \| x \| \| y \|$ (Cauchy-Schwarz inequality);

c) $\| x + y \| \leq \| x \| + \| y \|$ (triangle inequality);

d) $d \left( x , y \right) = \| x - y \|$ is a metric (distance) , i.e. $d \left( x , y \right) = d \left( y , x \right) > 0 ,$ if
$x \neq y ,$ $d \left( x , x \right) = 0 ,$ and $d \left( x , y \right) \leq d \left( x , z \right) + d \left( z , y \right) .$

The last part of Proposition 10.1.1 implies that every inner product space is a
metric space. In fact, every inner product space is a normed space. $A$ normed
space is a vector space with the norm which satisfies conditions 1 and 3 in
Proposition 10.1.1 and $\| x \| = 0$ if and only if $x = 0 .$ Every normed space is a
metric space with the metric defined by $d \left( x , y \right) = \| x - y \| .$

Recall that a sequence $\left\{ x _ { n } \right\}$ of points in a metric space converges to a point
$x$ if $d \left( x _ { n } , x \right) \rightarrow 0 ,$ as $n \rightarrow \infty .$ A sequence $\left\{ x _ { n } \right\}$ is said to be a Cauchy (or
a fundamental) sequence if $d \left( x _ { n } , x _ { m } \right) \rightarrow 0 ,$ as n, $m \rightarrow \infty .$ Since $d \left( x _ { n } , x _ { m } \right) \leq$
$d \left( x _ { n } , x \right) + d \left( x , x _ { m } \right) ,$ every convergent sequence is a Cauchy sequence. The
converse statement is not true in general. This leads to the following definition.

DEFINITION 10.1.2 A metric space in which every Cauchy sequence has a
limit is called complete.

Under Euclidean distance, the open interval $\left( 0 , 1 \right)$ is not a complete metric
space, but the closed interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is. The Euclidean space $\mathbb{R} ^ { d }$ is complete,
see e.g. Chapter 3 of Rudin (1976) for a proof.

DEFINITION 10.1.3 $A$ complete inner product space is called a Hilbert space.

EXAMPLE 10.1.1 The space $\ell ^ { 2 }$ is defined as the collection of sequences $x =$
$\left\{ x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots \right\}$ such that $\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } | x _ { i } | ^ { 2 } < \infty .$ It $i s \quad a$ vector space with the operation
defined componentwise, i.e.

$$x + y = \left\{ x _ { 1 } + y _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } + y _ { 2 } , \ldots \right\} ; \quad a x = \left\{ a x _ { 1 } , a x _ { 2 } , \ldots \right\} .$$

The inner product is defined by

$$\langle x , y \rangle = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } x _ { i } \bar { y } _ { i } .$$
(10.1)

Problem 10.3 asks for a verification that $\ell ^ { 2 }$ with the operations so defined is
a Hilbert space. The Cauchy-Schwarz inequality takes the form

$$| \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } x _ { i } \bar { y } _ { i } | \leq \left( \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } | x _ { i } | ^ { 2 } \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } \left( \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } | y _ { i } | ^ { 2 } \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="214" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

In this book, we will extensively work with the space of square integrable
real functions defined on an interval. Without any loss of generality we can
assume that this interval is $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ We therefore state the following definition.

DEFINITION 10.1.4 The space $L ^ { 2 }$ is the collection of Lebesgue measurable
real-valued functions $x$ defined on the interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ such that

$$\int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } x ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t < \infty .$$

The vector space operations are defined via

$$\left( x + y \right) \left( t \right) = x \left( t \right) + y \left( t \right) , \left( a x \right) \left( t \right) = a x \left( t \right) .$$

The inner product is defined by

$$\langle x , y \rangle = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } x \left( t \right) y \left( t \right) d t .$$

The $L ^ { 2 }$ space of complex-valued functions is defined with obvious mod-
ifications. The inequalities $\left( a + b \right) ^ { 2 } \leq 2 a ^ { 2 } + 2 b ^ { 2 }$ and $2 | a b | \leq a ^ { 2 } + b ^ { 2 } ,$ which
hold for any real numbers $a$ and $b$ show that vector operations and the inner
product are well-defined. The axioms of the vector space and of the inner
product space are then easy to verify. One can also show that the space $L ^ { 2 }$ is
complete. This is more technical, an interested reader is referred. e.g., to Sec-
tion 10 of Akhiezier and Glazman (1993). Measurable functions and abstract
$L ^ { p } \left( \mu \right)$ spaces are studied, e.g., in Rudin (1987). We summarize this discussion
in the following theorem.

THEOREM 10.1.1 The space $L ^ { 2 }$ of Definition 10.1.4 is a Hilbert space.

Throughout the book, if the limits of integration are not indicated, it is
assumed that the integral is over the entire domain on which the functions
are define (typically $\left. \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) .$ The Cauchy-Schwarz inequality in the space $L ^ { 2 }$
thus takes the form

$$| \int x \left( t \right) y \left( t \right) d t | \leq \left( \int x ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } \left( \int y ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$

A sequence $\left\{ x _ { n } \right\}$ converges to a function x in $L ^ { 2 }$ if $\int \left[ x _ { n } \left( t \right) - x \left( t \right) \right] ^ { 2 } d t \rightarrow 0 .$
We now review several of the most commonly encounter Hilbert spaces for
functional data.

EXAMPLE 10.1.2 While $L ^ { 2 } \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is the most commonly countered space, there
are many examples which move beyond the $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ interval. For example $L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{D} \right)$
where $\mathcal{D}$ is a compact subset of $\mathbb{R} ^ { d } .$ For spatial processes, one commonly uses
$d = 2 ,$ while for applications such as fMRI one can take $d = 3 .$ For space time
processes one may take $L ^ { 2 } \left[ \mathcal{T} \times \mathcal{D} \right]$ where the first argument represents time
and second space.
☐

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="215" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

EXAMPLE 10.1.3 Given that FDA is often applied to smooth process, it may
also be of interest to incorporate derivatives. This leads to the Sobolev spaces,
$H ^ { K } \left( \mathcal{T} \right) ,$ which consist of all functions over $\mathcal{T}$ which are square integrable and
whose derivatives up to order $K$ are also square integrable. Focussing on square
integrable functions ensures the resulting space is a Hilbert space. The inner
product is then given by

$$\langle x , y \rangle _ { K , 2 } = \sum _ { k = 0 } ^ { K } \int x ^ { \left( k \right) } \left( t \right) y ^ { \left( k \right) } \left( t \right) d t ,$$

where $x ^ { \left( k \right) } \left( t \right)$ and $y ^ { \left( k \right) } \left( t \right)$ are the $k ^ { t h }$ order derivatives of $x$ and $y ,$ respectively,
with respect to t.
☐

EXAMPLE 10.1.4 Multivariate functional data are commonly encountered,
e.g. when examining panels of functional time series. Given two Hilbert
space, $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $\mathcal{H} _ { 2 } ,$ the cartesian product $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ is also a Hilbert space.
If $\left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \right) \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ then one defines the inner product norm on the product
space as

$$\| \left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \right) \| _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } ^ { 2 } = \| x _ { 1 } \| _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } } ^ { 2 } + \| x _ { 2 } \| _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } ^ { 2 } .$$

Given that the cartesian product between two Hilbert spaces is a Hilbert
space, one can readily generalize beyond one product.
☐

EXAMPLE 10.1.5 The space $\mathcal{C} \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ denotes the set of all continuous functions
over the interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$ Commonly, this space is equipped with the "sup norm"
$\| x \| = \sup _ { t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] } | x \left( t \right) | .$ Under the sup norm, $\mathcal{C} \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is a complete normed vector
space (i.e. a Banach space), but it is not an inner product space. Alternatively,
one can equip $\mathcal{C} \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ with the $L ^ { 2 }$ norm defined in Definition 10.1.4, however,
under the $L ^ { 2 }$ norm, $\mathcal{C} \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is not complete. Any metric space can be completed
by expanding the space so that all Cauchy sequences have an element to which
they converge. The completion of $\mathcal{C} \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ with respect to the $L ^ { 2 }$ norm is $L ^ { 2 } \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$
☐


### 10.2 Projections and orthonormal sets

Functional data, typically a collection of functions $x _ { n } \in L ^ { 2 }$ defined on a com-
mon interval, are viewed as infinite dimensional objects. A common technique
is to project these functions onto finite dimensional subspaces to facilitate
statistical calculations. In this section, we therefore review in some detail
the relevant Hilbert space theory background. Proofs of Theorems 10.2.1 and
10.2.2 can be found in many textbooks, e.g. in Rudin (1987).

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="216" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

THEOREM 10.2.1 Suppose $\mathcal{G}$ is a closed subspace of a Hilbert space $\mathcal{H} .$ For
any $x \in \mathcal{H}$ define

$$\delta = \inf _ { z \in \mathcal{G} } \| x - z \| .$$

Then there exists a unique $y \in \mathcal{G}$ such that $\| x - y \| = \delta .$

Recall that a set is closed if it contains all of its limit points, i.e, if for any
convergent sequence in $\mathcal{G} ,$ say an, we have $x : = \lim x _ { n } \in \mathcal{G}$ as well, then we
say $\mathcal{G}$ is closed.

The vector $y$ is called the projection of $x$ onto $\mathcal{G} .$ It is the point of $\mathcal{G}$
which is nearest to x, but another interpretation is based on the concept of
orthogonality. We say that $x$ is orthogonal to $y$ if $\langle x , y \rangle = 0 .$ If $\mathcal{G}$ is a subspace,
then the orthogonal complement, $\mathcal{G} ^ { \perp } ,$ denotes the set of all $y \in \mathcal{H}$ which are
orthogonal to every vector in $\mathcal{G} .$ Recall that a mapping $L : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ is linear if
for any scalars a, $b$ and vectors x, $y ,$

$$L \left( a x + b y \right) = a L \left( x \right) + b L \left( y \right) .$$
(10.2)

THEOREM 10.2.2 Suppose $\mathcal{G}$ is a closed subspace of a Hilbert space $\mathcal{H} .$

a) Every $x \in \mathcal{H}$ has $a$ unique decomposition $x = P \left( x \right) + Q \left( x \right) ,$ where $P \left( x \right) \in \mathcal{G}$
and $Q \left( x \right) \in \mathcal{G} ^ { \perp } .$

b) $P \left( x \right)$ and $Q \left( x \right)$ are the nearest points to $x$ in $\mathcal{G}$ and $\mathcal{G} ^ { \perp } ,$ respectively.

c) The mappings $P : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{G}$ and $Q : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{G} ^ { \perp }$ are linear.

d) $\| x \| ^ { 2 } = \| P \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } + \| Q \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$

The linear operator $P$ is called the projection onto $\mathcal{G} .$ The following corol-
lary is often used.

COROLLARY 10.2.1 The projection $P \left( x \right)$ is orthogonal to $x \quad - \quad P \left( x \right) .$

Theorem 10.2.2 leads to the following important result, stated as Theo-
rem 10.2.3 and known as the Riesz representation theorem. $I \dagger$ is easy to see
that for every $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ the function $x \mapsto \langle x , y \rangle$ is linear and continuous on $\mathcal{H} .$
In fact, there are no other continuous linear functions.

THEOREM 10.2.3 If $L : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow R$ is linear and continuous, then there is a
unique $y \in \mathcal{H}$ such that $L \left( x \right) = \langle x , y \rangle .$

PROOF: If $L \left( x \right) = 0$ for all x, take $y = 0 .$ For L which is not identically 0,
define

$$\mathcal{G} = \left\{ x : L \left( x \right) = 0 \right\} .$$

By the linearity of $L ,$ $\mathcal{G}$ is a subspace, and by the continuity of $L$ it is closed.
We can thus use Theorem 10.2.2. We will show that there is $y \in \mathcal{G} ^ { \perp }$ such that
$L \left( x \right) = \langle x , y \rangle .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="217" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

Take $z \in \mathcal{G} ^ { \perp }$ with $\| z \| = 1$ and observe that

$$L \left( x \right) = L \left( x \right) \langle z , z \rangle = \langle L \left( x \right) z , z \rangle = \langle L \left( z \right) x + u _ { x } , z \rangle ,$$

where

$$u _ { x } = L \left( x \right) z - L \left( z \right) x .$$

Note that $u _ { x } \in \mathcal{G} .$ Since $z \in \mathcal{G} ^ { \perp } ,$ $\langle u _ { x } , z \rangle = 0 .$ Therefore,

$$L \left( x \right) = \langle L \left( z \right) x , z \rangle = \langle x , \bar { L \left( z \right) } z \rangle .$$

Thus, we take $y = \bar { L \left( z \right) } z .$

To establish uniqueness, suppose $\langle x , y \rangle = \langle x , y ^ { \prime } \rangle$ for every x. Taking $x =$
$y - y ^ { \prime } ,$ this implies $\| y - y ^ { \prime } \| = 0 ,$ so $y = y ^ { \prime } .$
.

As noted at the beginning of this section, in $\mathrm { F D A }$ we often work with pro-
jections on finite dimensional subspaces. Let $\mathcal{G}$ be a d-dimensional subspace of
$\mathcal{H}$ with a basis $e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , \ldots , e _ { d } .$ Every vector in $\mathcal{G}$ is a unique linear combination
of $e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , \ldots , e _ { d } .$ In particular, for every $x \in \mathcal{H} ,$

$$P \left( x \right) = a _ { 1 } e _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } e _ { 2 } + \ldots , a _ { d } e _ { d } .$$

By Theorem 10.2.2, $x \quad - \quad P \left( x \right)$ is orthogonal to G. Therefore, for each $e _ { j } ,$
$\langle x - P \left( x \right) , e _ { j } \rangle = 0 .$ Consequently,

$$\langle e _ { 1 } , e _ { j } \rangle a _ { 1 } + \langle e _ { 2 } , e _ { j } \rangle a _ { 2 } + \ldots \langle e _ { d } , e _ { j } \rangle a _ { d } = \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle , \quad j = 1 , 2 , \ldots , d .$$

This is a system of $d$ linear equations in the unknowns $a _ { 1 } , a _ { 2 } , \ldots , a _ { d }$ which
can be uniquely determined if we know the inner products $\langle e _ { i } , e _ { j } \rangle$ and $\langle x , e _ { j } \rangle .$
The above system simplifies significantly if $\langle e _ { i } , e _ { j } \rangle = 0 ,$ whenever $i \neq j .$ If, in
addition, $\langle e _ { j } , e _ { j } \rangle = 1 ,$ then $a _ { j } = \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle .$ This motivates the following defini-
tions.

DEFINITION 10.2.1 Let $A$ be an arbitrary index set. We say that $\left\{ e _ { \alpha } , \alpha \in A \right\}$
is an orthonormal system if

$$\langle e _ { \alpha } , e _ { \beta } \rangle = \left\{ \begin{array}{} 1 & \mathrm { i f } \alpha = \beta , \\ 0 & \mathrm { i f } \alpha \neq \beta . \end{array} \right. ,$$

DEFINITION 10.2.2 A Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}$ is said to be separable if there is a
countable orthonormal system $\left\{ e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , \ldots \right\}$ such that every $x \in \mathcal{H}$ admits the
expansion

$$x = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } a _ { j } e _ { j } .$$

Such a system is said $\mathrm { t } \alpha$ be complete (in $\mathcal{H} .$

DEFINITION 10.2.3 A complete orthonormal system in a separable Hilbert
space is called an orthonormal basis.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="218" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

A weaker (topological) definition of separability is often used which states
that a separable topological space is one which contains a countably dense
subset, and then the property stated in Definition 10.2.2 is proven as an equiv-
alent characterization for Hilbert spaces, see e.g. Theorem 3 in Akhiezier and
Glazman (1993). Notice that a set $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis if there
is no nonzero element of $\mathcal{H}$ which is orthogonal to every $e _ { j } .$

The expansion in Definition 10.2.2 is assumed to converge in the norm of
$\mathcal{H} ,$ i.e.

$$\lim _ { J \rightarrow \infty } \| x - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } a _ { j } e _ { j } \| = 0 .$$
(10.3)

By Theorem 10.2.2,

$$\| x \| ^ { 2 } = \| \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } a _ { j } e _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } + \| x - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } a _ { j } e _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } .$$

The orthonormality of the $e _ { j }$ implies that
$\| \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } a _ { j } e _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } | a _ { j } | ^ { 2 }$
\=
J
2
To-
gether with (10.3), this implies that $\| x \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } | a _ { j } | ^ { 2 } .$ Finally, observe that
$a _ { j } = \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle .$ We summarize these results in the following theorem:

THEOREM 10.2.4 [Parseval's Theorem] Suppose $\mathcal{H}$ is a separable Hilbert
space and $\left\{ e _ { j } \right\}$ is a complete orthonormal system. Then, for any $x \in \mathcal{H} ,$

$$x = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle e _ { j }$$

and

$$\| x \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } | \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle | ^ { 2 } .$$
(10.4)

Identity (10.4) is known as Parseval's equality.

We conclude this section by giving examples of orthonormal bases which
are often used, the bases consisting of trigonometric functions. The most com-
monly used basis of this type, often referred to as the Fourier basis is

$$e _ { 1 } \left( t \right) = 1 ;$$
$$e _ { 2 } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( 2 \pi t \right) ;$$
$$e _ { 3 } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \cos \left( 2 \pi t \right) ;$$

$$e _ { 4 } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( 2 \cdot 2 \pi t \right) ;$$
$$e _ { 5 } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \cos \left( 2 \cdot 2 \pi t \right) ;$$
(10.5)

.

In applications, we typically project functions on the subspace spanned by the

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="219" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

constant function and the functions $\sin \left( k \cdot 2 \pi t \right) ,$ $\cos \left( k \cdot 2 \pi t \right) ,$ $1 \leq k \leq K ,$ so
we use $2 K + 1$ Fourier basis functions to construct approximations.

A real function in $L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right)$ can be expanded using only cosine functions
and the constant function. The functions

$$e _ { 0 } \left( t \right) = 1 , \quad e _ { k } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \cos \left( \pi k t \right) , \quad k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$$
(10.6)

form an orthonormal basis. Another orthonormal basis consists of sine func-
tions:

$$e _ { k } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \pi k t \right) , \quad k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$$
(10.7)

Note that the trigonometric functions in $\left( 1 0 . 5 \right)$ have arguments that are
multiples of $2 \pi t$ and those in (10.6) and (10.7) have arguments which are
multiples of $\pi t .$ It is easy to verify that these three systems are orthonormal.
The proof that they are complete is more delicate, an interested reader is
referred to Section 2.4 of Hsing and Eubank (2015)

Observe that the members of the basis (10.7) are zero at both ends of
the interval $\left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ so one may wonder is all functions in $L ^ { 2 }$ can indeed be
expanded using $\mathrm { o n l y }$ sine functions. For example, how can we expand the
function $x \left( t \right) = t ?$ The point to keep in mind is that we do not claim that a
uniform approximation is possible; we only claim the convergence in the $L ^ { 2 }$
norm, i.e., in the case of the basis (10.7),

$$\lim _ { K \rightarrow \infty } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \left( x \left( t \right) - \sqrt { 2 } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } b _ { k } \sin \left( \pi k t \right) \right) ^ { 2 } d t = 0 ,$$

where $b _ { k } = \sqrt { 2 } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } x \left( t \right) \sin \left( \pi k t \right) d t .$ Having said that, expanding the function
$x \left( t \right) = t$ using the sine basis would not be optimal, see Fig. 10.1, reproduced
from Hunter and Nachtergaele (2001). Finding optimal basis expansions for
a set of observed functions is an important issue in FDA which will be given
due attention.


### 10.3 Linear operators

$A$ function $L : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ is called a linear operator, or a linear transformation,
if for any scalars $a ,$ $b$ and any x, $y \in \mathcal{H}$ relation (10.2) holds. We will call a
linear operator simply an operator. An operator $L$ is bounded if there is a
constant $K$ such that for any $x \in \mathcal{H} ,$ $\| L \left( x \right) \| \leq K \| x \| .$ The smallest such $K$
is called the operator norm of L and denoted $\| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} }$ . It is clear that

$$\| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \sup _ { \| x \| = 1 } \| L \left( x \right) \| .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="220" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


<figure>
<figcaption>FIGURE 10.1: Approximation of the function $x \left( t \right) =$ t,t $t \in \left[ 0 , \pi \right] ,$ using sine (left) and cosine (right) functions.</figcaption>
