</figure>


In general, computing the operator norm is not easy. One can show that an
operator is bounded if and only if it is continuous, see Problem 10.7. Hence,
bounded operators are often called continuous operators.

Bounded operators form a normed vector space with the operations defined
pointwise, i.e.

0

0

$$\left( L _ { 1 } + L _ { 2 } \right) \left( x \right) = L _ { 1 } \left( x \right) + L _ { 2 } \left( x \right) , \quad \left( a L \right) \left( x \right) = a L \left( x \right) .$$

3

With the norm $\| \cdot \| _ { \mathcal{L} } ,$ this vector space is a Banach space, see Problem 10.8.
Since it is more convenient to work in a Hilbert space than in a Banach space,
the class of Hilbert-Schmidt operators is often used.

Hilbert-Schmidt operators are defined on a separable Hilbert space, cf.
Definition 10.2.2. In the remainder of this section, we assume that $\mathcal{H}$ is a
separable Hilbert space over the field of real numbers. The following version
of Parseval's equality is useful to define Hilbert-Schmidt operators:

$$\langle x , y \rangle = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle \langle e _ { j } , y \rangle ,$$
(10.8)

where $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is any orthonormal basis in $\mathcal{H} .$ Equality (10.8) follows from
Theorem 10.2.4:

$$\left. \langle x , y \rangle = \langle \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle e _ { j } , \sum _ { j ^ { \prime } = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle y , e _ { j ^ { \prime } } \rangle e _ { j ^ { \prime } } \right)$$
$$= \sum _ { j , j ^ { \prime } = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle \langle y , e _ { j ^ { \prime } } \rangle \langle e _ { j } , e _ { j ^ { \prime } } \rangle$$
$$= \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle \langle e _ { j } , y \rangle ,$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="221" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

where in the last equality we used the fact that the $e _ { j }$ are orthormal and the
inner product is symmetric.

A bounded linear operator $\Psi$ is said to be Hilbert-Schmidt if for an or-
thonormal basis $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$

$$\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } < \infty .$$

The above relation defines the Hilbert-Schmidt norm $\| \cdot \| _ { \mathcal{S} } .$ Using (10.8), it
is easy to check that this definition is correct, i.e. it does not depend on the
choice of the basis, see Problem 10.10. We denote the vector space of Hilbert-
Schmidt operators by $\mathcal{S} .$ The space $\mathcal{S}$ is a separable Hilbert space with the
inner product

$$\langle \Psi , \Phi \rangle _ { \mathcal{S} } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) , \Phi \left( e _ { j } \right) \rangle .$$
(10.9)

If $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis in $\mathcal{H} ,$ then the operators $e _ { j } \otimes e _ { i }$ defined
by

$$\left( e _ { j } \otimes e _ { i } \right) \left( x \right) = \langle e _ { i } , x \rangle e _ { j }$$

form an orthonormal basis in $\mathcal{S} ,$ see Problem 10.11. The object $e _ { j } \otimes e _ { i }$ is called
a tensor and will be discussed in greater detail in Section 10.5.

EXAMPLE 10.3.1 [Integral operator] Consider $\mathcal{H} = L ^ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) ,$ and de-
fine an operator $\Psi$ by

$$\Psi \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \int \psi \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s ,$$

where $\psi \left( \cdot , \cdot \right)$ is a bivariate function, called the kernel, which satisfies

$$\int \int \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s < \infty .$$

We first show that the operator $\Psi$ is bounded. It is convenient to introduce
the functions $\psi _ { t }$ defined by $\psi _ { t } \left( s \right) = \psi \left( t , s \right) .$ By Fubini's theorem, $\psi _ { t } \in L ^ { 2 }$ for
almost all t. For such t, the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality implies that for any
$x \in L ^ { 2 } ,$

$$\left\{ \int \psi _ { t } \left( s \right) x \left( s \right) d s \right\} ^ { 2 } \leq \int \psi _ { t } ^ { 2 } \left( s \right) d s \int x ^ { 2 } \left( s \right) d s .$$

Therefore,

$$\| \Psi \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \int \left\{ \int \psi \left( t , s \right) x \left( s \right) d s \right\} ^ { 2 } d t$$
$$\leq \int \left\{ \int \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d s \int x ^ { 2 } \left( s \right) d s \right\} d t$$
$$= \left( \iint \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s \right) \| x \| ^ { 2 } .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="222" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

We see that

$$\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \leq \left( \iint \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$

Now we will verify that $\Psi \in \mathcal{S} .$ For any orthonormal basis $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\} ,$ by
Theorem 10.2.4,

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \int \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left\{ \int \psi \left( t , s \right) e _ { j } \left( s \right) d s \right\} ^ { 2 } d t$$
$$= \int \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle \psi _ { t } , e _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } d t$$
$$= \int \| \psi _ { t } \| ^ { 2 } d t$$
$$= \iint \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s .$$

The above calculation shows that

$$\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } = \left( \iint \psi ^ { 2 } \left( t , s \right) d t d s \right) ^ { 1 / 2 } .$$
(10.10)

☐

We now consider a simple example of an operator which is bounded, but not
Hilbert-Schmidt.

EXAMPLE 10.3.2 Let $\mathcal{H}$ be an infinite dimensional separable Hilbert space.
The identity operator $\Psi \left( x \right) = x ,$ is bounded, but not Hilbert-Schmidt. Clearly,
the operator norm of $\Psi \text { is } 1$ since

$$\sup _ { \| x \| = 1 } \| \Psi \left( x \right) \| = \sup _ { \| x \| = 1 } \| x \| = 1 .$$

However, the Hilbert-Schmidt norm is infinite since

$$\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| e _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } 1 = \infty .$$

The same arguments show that if $\mathcal{H}$ is finite dimensional, then the identity
will be Hilbert-Schmidt. This equivalence holds more generally, as all bounded
operators over a finite dimensional Hilbert space are also Hilbert-Schmidt.
☐

The integral operator in Example 10.3.1 satisfies $\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \leq \| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } .$ This
relation holds in general, as stated in Theorem 10.3.1.

THEOREM 10.3.1 If $\Psi \in \mathcal{S} ,$ then $\Psi \in \mathcal{L}$ and $\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \leq \| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="223" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

Perhaps the easiest way to prove Theorem 10.3.1 is by introducing the
concept of the adjoint operator.

DEFINITION 10.3.1 Let $L : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ be a bounded operator. The operator $L ^ { * }$
defined by

$$\langle L \left( x \right) , y \rangle = \langle x , L ^ { * } \left( y \right) \rangle$$

is called the adjoint operator of $L .$

Problem 10.12 asks for the verification that $L ^ { * }$ is bounded and

$$\| L ^ { * } \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$$

To prove Theorem 10.3.1, it thus suffices to verify that

$$\| \Psi ^ { * } \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } \leq \left( \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } \right) \| x \| ^ { 2 } .$$

The above relation follows from Parseval's equality and Cauchy-Schwarz in-
equality:

$$\| \Psi ^ { * } \left( x \right) \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle \Psi ^ { * } \left( x \right) , e _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \rangle ^ { 2 } \leq \| x \| ^ { 2 } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) \| ^ { 2 } .$$


### 10.4 Basics of spectral theory

Many concepts and results discussed in this Section do not require that as-
sumption that $\mathcal{H}$ space; the general framework of a vector space is
often sufficient. Motivated by applications to FDA, we assume that the vector
space is defined over the filed of real numbers, but all results of this section
remain true in the complex case, with minimal adjustments.

Suppose $L$ is an operator on a vector space. A number $\lambda$ is called an
eigenvalue of $L$ if there is a nonzero vector $x$ such that

$$L \left( x \right) = \lambda x .$$

Every such vector $x$ is called an eigenvector of $L$ corresponding to $\lambda .$ If the
operator acts on a function space, $x$ is often called an eigenfunction.

If $x$ is an eigenvector corresponding to $\lambda ,$ then $a x$ is clearly also an eigen-
vector corresponding to $\lambda .$ In fact, the set of all eigenvectors corresponding to
$\lambda$ forms a linear subspace, as can be verified using the definition of the eigen-
vector. This space is called the eigenvalue space of $\lambda$ its dimension is called
the multiplicity of $\lambda .$ An eigenvalue of multiplicity greater than one is called

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="224" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

multiple. In the remainder of this section, we state several simple theorems
which will be used throughout the book.

We say that $T$ is an invertible operator if there is an operator $T ^ { - 1 }$ defined
on the whole vector space such that $T \left( T ^ { - 1 } \left( x \right) \right) = T ^ { - 1 } \left( T \left( x \right) \right) = x .$ If $T ^ { - 1 }$
exists, it is unique.

THEOREM 10.4.1 Let $T$ be an invertible operator on a vector space, and let
$L$ be any operator. The operators $L$ and $T L T ^ { - 1 }$ have the same eigenvalues.

PROOF: Suppose $\lambda$ is an eigenvalue of $L$ and $x$ a corresponding eigenvector.
Since $T$ is invertible, $T \left( x \right) \neq 0 .$ Observe that

$$T L T ^ { - 1 } \left( T \left( x \right) \right) = T L \left( x \right) = T \left( \lambda x \right) = \lambda T \left( x \right) ,$$

so $\lambda$ is an eigenvalue of $T L T ^ { - 1 }$ (with a corresponding eigenvector $\left. T \left( x \right) \right) .$
Since $L = T ^ { - 1 } \left( T L T ^ { - 1 } \right) T ,$ the same argument shows that every eigenvalue of
$T L T ^ { - 1 }$ is also an eigenvalue of $L .$

DEFINITION 10.4.1 $\mathrm { A n }$ operator $L$ over an inner product space is called
nonnegative definite if for every vector x,

$$\langle L \left( x \right) , x \rangle \geq 0 .$$

It is positive definite, if the equality can hold only for $x = 0 .$

Nongetive definite operators are also called: nonnegative, positive, positive-
definite, positive semidefinite. Positive definite operators are also called:
strictly positive and strictly positive-definite.

THEOREM 10.4.2 All eigenvalues $o f$ a nonnegative definite operator are non-
negative. All eigenvalues of $a$ positive definite operator are positive.

PROOF: Suppose $L$ is a nonnegative definite operator, $\lambda$ its eigenvalue and
$x$ a corresponding eigenvector. Then

$$\lambda \| x \| ^ { 2 } = \lambda \langle x , x \rangle = \langle \lambda x , x \rangle = \langle L \left( x \right) , x \rangle \geq 0 .$$

Thus $\lambda \geq 0 .$ As an eigenvector, $x$ is nonzero, so $\| x \| > 0 .$ If $L$ is positive
definite, then $\lambda \| x \| ^ { 2 } > 0 ,$ so $\lambda > 0 .$

DEFINITION 10.4.2 An operator $L$ over an inner product space is called $s y m -$
metric if for any x, $y ,$

.
$$\langle L \left( x \right) , y \rangle = \langle x , L \left( y \right) \rangle .$$

In the context of a complex Hilbert space, symmetric operators are called
self-adjoint, cf. Definition 10.3.1.

THEOREM 10.4.3 Eigenvectors corresponding to distinct eigenvalues of a
symmetric operator are orthogonal.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="225" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

PROOF: Let $\lambda _ { 1 } \neq \lambda _ { 2 }$ be eigenvalues with eigenvectors $x _ { 1 }$ and $x _ { 2 } ,$ respectively.
Observe that

$$\lambda _ { 1 } \langle x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \rangle = \langle L \left( x _ { 1 } \right) , x _ { 2 } \rangle = \langle x _ { 1 } , L \left( x _ { 2 } \right) \rangle = \lambda _ { 2 } \langle x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \rangle .$$

It follows that $\langle x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \rangle = 0 .$

We now state an important result known as the Hilbert-Schmidt theorem.

THEOREM 10.4.4 Suppose $\Psi \in \mathcal{S}$ is symmetric. Then, there is an or-
thonormal system $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ consisting of eigenvectors of $\Psi$ corresponding
to nonzero eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ such that every $x \in \mathcal{H}$ has $a$ unique representation

$$x = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } a _ { j } e _ { j } + v ,$$

where $v$ satisfies, $\Psi \left( v \right) = 0 .$

The proof of Theorem 10.4.4 is more complex than the other proofs pre-
sented in this section, and is not given here. An interested reader is refereed
to Debnath and Mikusinski (2005), or any other textbook covering operators
in a Hilbert space. The following corollary is known as the spectral theorem
or Mercer's theorem.

COROLLARY 10.4.1 Suppose $\Psi \in \mathcal{S}$ is symmetric. Then, there is an orthonor-
mal basis $\left\{ v _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ such that for every $x \in \mathcal{H}$

$$\Psi \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle v _ { j } ,$$

where $\lambda _ { j }$ is an eigenvalue corresponding to the eigenfunction $v _ { j }$ (of $\Psi$ ).

PROOF: Set $\mathcal{V} = \left\{ x \in \mathcal{H} : \Psi \left( x \right) = 0 \right\} .$ The subspace $\mathcal{V}$ is a separable Hilbert
space; let $\left\{ v _ { k } ^ { * } , k \geq 1 \right\}$ be an orthonormal basis in $\mathcal{V} .$ The required basis
$\left\{ v _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is the union of $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ and $\left\{ v _ { k } ^ { * } , k \geq 1 \right\} .$ The result follows be-
cause $a _ { j } = \langle x , e _ { j } \rangle$ and each $v _ { k } ^ { * }$ corresponds to the zero eigenvalue.

As we will see in Chapters 11 and 12, Corollary 10.4.1 plays a fundamen-
tal role in the analysis of variance of random functions. We conclude this
section with a theorem which leads to such results. Suppose $\Psi \in \mathcal{S}$ is sym-
metric and nonnegative definite. Suppose we want to find a vector $x \in \mathcal{H}$ with
$\| x \| = 1$ such that $\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle$ is maximum. Since $\Psi$ is nonnegative definite,
all eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ are nonnegative. We arrange them in nonincreasing order,
so that $\lambda _ { 1 } \geq \lambda _ { 2 } \geq \ldots .$ To ensure a unique solution to the problem, assume

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="226" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

that $\lambda _ { 1 } > \lambda _ { 2 } .$ Then, by Theorem 10.4.3, $v _ { 1 }$ is orthogonal to all remaining
eigenvectors. We must maximize

$$\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle = \langle \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle , x \rangle = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 }$$

subject to $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } = 1 .$ By our assumptions on the eigenvalues, the
maximum is attained if we take $x = v _ { 1 }$ (or $x = - v _ { 1 } .$ We have thus shown
that $\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle$ is maximum if $x = v _ { 1 } ,$ the eigenvector corresponding to the
largest eigenvalue, and the maximum is equal to $\lambda _ { 1 } ,$ the largest eigenvalue.

In FDA, it is useful to solve a more general problem which can be formu-
lated as follows: we want to find $x$ with unit norm which maximizes $\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle ,$
but which is orthogonal to $v _ { 1 } .$ We will see that $x = v _ { 2 } .$ Next we want to
maximize $\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle$ over a subspace orthogonal to $v _ { 1 }$ and $v _ { 2 } ,$ etc. To obtain a
unique solution for $p$ iterations, we assume that

$$\lambda _ { 1 } > \lambda _ { 2 } > \ldots \lambda _ { p } > \lambda _ { p + 1 } .$$
(10.11)

THEOREM 10.4.5 Suppose $\Psi$ is a symmetric, nonnegative $d$ definite Hilbert-
Schmidt operator with eigenfunctions $v _ { j }$ and eigenvalues $\lambda _ { j }$ satisfying (10.11).
Then, for $i \leq p$

$$\sup \left\{ \langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle : \| x \| = 1 , \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle = 0 , 1 \leq j \leq i - 1 \right\} = \lambda _ { i } ,$$

and the supremum is achieved if $x = v _ { i } .$ The maximizing function is unique
up to a sign.

PROOF: The theorem follows from the identities

$$\langle \Psi \left( x \right) , x \rangle = \sum _ { j = i } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } , \quad \sum _ { j = i } ^ { \infty } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } = 1 .$$

Note that the above implies that for a symmetric nonnegative definite linear
operator with eigenvalues ordered as $\lambda _ { 1 } \geq \lambda _ { 2 } \geq \ldots .$ we have the relations

$$\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { i } ^ { 2 } \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad \| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \lambda _ { 1 } .$$

This allows us to gain a bit more understanding as to the difference between
a bounded operator and one that is Hilbert-Schmidt; a bounded operator has
bounded eigenvalues, while a Hilbert-Schmidt operator has eigenvalues which
go to zero fast enough to sum.

,

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="227" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->


### 10.5 Tensors

Later, when examining covariance operators, it is convenient to work with
tensor products and spaces. In finite dimensional spaces, use of tensors can
be avoided by stacking matrices in the right way. Such an approach does not
work in infinite dimensional spaces and thus use of tensors cannot be avoided
if one wants to work with general Hilbert spaces. The methods presented here
work well for real separable Hilbert spaces. For more general Banach spaces or
vectors spaces, the definitions become more complex, and we avoid them. For
a more complete treatment we refer to one of the many texts now available
on tensor methods and multilinear algebra such as Hackbusch (2012).

While tensors are often thought of as generalizations of matrices, this does
not translate well to infinite dimensional spaces. Instead, it is easier to think of
tensors as multilinear mappings. We begin with an example in Euclidian space
to illustrate this point. First, consider a matrix $\mathrm { A } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { N \times M }$ . A matrix can be
viewed in at least three different ways: 1) as a traditional matrix, 2) as a linear
transformation from $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \rightarrow \mathbb{R} ^ { M }$ (or the reverse), or 3) a bilinear functional
$\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \times \mathbb{R} ^ { M } \rightarrow \mathbb{R} .$ The first is, of course, how we often think of matrices. The
second is obtained by multiplying a matrix from the left or right by a vector.
One learns in linear algebra that all linear transformations in Euclidean space
are of such a form. The last view is maybe less common, but arises often in
the study of quadratic forms. Consider two Euclidean spaces $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } = \mathbb{R} ^ { N }$ and
$\mathcal{H} _ { 2 } = \mathbb{R} ^ { M } .$ Forming the tensor product between to vectors, $x _ { 1 } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { N }$ and
$x _ { 2 } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { M } ,$ leads to a matrix:

$$x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } : = x _ { 1 } x _ { 2 } ^ { \top } .$$

The space $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \otimes \mathbb{R} ^ { M }$ is then defined as the space of all finite linear combinations
of the above elements. In other words, if $\mathrm { A } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { N } \otimes \mathbb{R} ^ { M } \rightarrow \mathbb{R} ,$ then there exists
$J$ elements $x _ { 1 j } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { N }$ and $x _ { 2 j } \in \mathbb{R} ^ { M }$ such that

$$\mathrm { A } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } x _ { 1 j } \otimes x _ { 2 j } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } x _ { 1 j } x _ { 2 j } ^ { \top } .$$

Since any $N \times M$ matrix can be expressed in such a way, we have that $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \otimes$
$\mathbb{R} ^ { M } = \mathbb{R} ^ { N \times M } .$ We will now specify an alternative view, that while equivalent,
generalizes more readily to other spaces. Any $N \times M$ matrix, $\mathrm { A } ,$ leads to a
bilinear mapping $\mathrm { A } : \mathbb{R} ^ { N } \times \mathbb{R} ^ { M } \rightarrow \mathbb{R}$ in form of

$$\mathrm { A } \left( y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \right) : = y _ { 1 } ^ { \top } \mathrm { A } y _ { 2 } .$$

Therefore the space $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \otimes \mathbb{R} ^ { M }$ can also be identified as the $s e t$ of all bilinear
maps from $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \times \mathbb{R} ^ { M } \rightarrow \mathbb{R} .$ The object $x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 }$ can also be identified as a
bilinear mapping

$$\left( x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } \right) \left( y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \right) = y _ { 1 } ^ { \top } x _ { 1 } x _ { 2 } ^ { \top } y _ { 2 } = \left( y _ { 1 } ^ { \top } x _ { 1 } \right) \left( y _ { 2 } ^ { \top } x _ { 2 } \right) .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="228" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

It is in this way we view tensors. Tensors are multilinear maps from the carte-
sian product of the spaces involved to a field of scalars. $A s$ we will mention
later on, if $\left\{ e _ { 1 j } \right\}$ and $\left\{ e _ { 2 k } \right\}$ are the standard bases for $\mathbb{R} ^ { N }$ and $\mathbb{R} ^ { M }$ respec-
tively, then $\left\{ e _ { 1 j } \otimes e _ { 2 k } \right\}$ is a basis of $\mathbb{R} ^ { N } \otimes \mathbb{R} ^ { M } .$ In this way, one uses this basis
to get back to traditional matrices:

$$\mathrm { A } \left( e _ { 1 j } ^ { \top } , e _ { 2 k } \right) = e _ { 1 j } ^ { \top } \mathrm { A } e _ { 2 k } = \mathrm { A } \left( j , k \right) .$$

We are now in a position to define the tensor product between elements of
Hilbert spaces.

DEFINITION 10.5.1 Let $x _ { 1 } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $x _ { 2 } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ be two elements of two real
Hilbert spaces. Then the tensor product, $x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } : \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } \rightarrow \mathbb{R} ,$ is a bilinear
map defined as

$$\left( x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } \right) \left( y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \right) = \langle x _ { 1 } , y _ { 1 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } } \langle x _ { 2 } , y _ { 2 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } ,$$

for any $\left( y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \right) \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } .$

The tensor space $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \otimes \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ can be viewed as the space of all tensors which is $\mathrm { a l s o }$
complete. To construct it, first define the set of all finite linear combinations
of tensors

$$\mathcal{A} = \left\{ \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } x _ { 1 j } \otimes x _ { 2 j } : x _ { 1 j } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 j } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } , 1 \leq J < \infty \right\} .$$

By definition, this is a vector space. One can define an inner product on $\mathcal{A}$ as

$$\langle \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } x _ { 1 j } \otimes x _ { 2 j } , \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } y _ { 1 k } \otimes y _ { 2 k } \rangle _ { \mathcal{A} } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { K } \langle x _ { 1 j } , y _ { 1 k } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } } \langle x _ { 2 j } , y _ { 2 k } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } .$$

This inner product leads to an inner product norm. We thus define $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \otimes \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$
as the completion of $\mathcal{A}$ with respect to the above inner product norm (note
that if the spaces are finite dimensional, then $\mathcal{A}$ is already complete). Since
$\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \otimes \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ is also a Hilbert space, we can now define the tensor product of any
order since our original definition only requires that $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $\mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ be Hilbert
spaces.

Below are several properties which follow from our definition.

THEOREM 10.5.1 Let $x _ { 1 } ,$ $y _ { 1 } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $x _ { 2 } ,$ $y _ { 2 } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ be elements of two Hilbert
spaces. Then one has the following

a) $\left( x _ { 1 } + y _ { 1 } \right) \otimes x _ { 2 } = x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } + y _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 }$

b) $x _ { 1 } \otimes \left( x _ { 2 } + y _ { 2 } \right) = x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } + x _ { 1 } \otimes y _ { 2 }$
☒

c) $c \left( x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } \right) = \left( c x _ { 1 } \right) \otimes x _ { 2 } = x _ { 1 } \otimes \left( c x _ { 2 } \right)$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="229" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

THEOREM 10.5.2 If $\left\{ e _ { 1 j } \right\}$ and $\left\{ e _ { 2 k } \right\}$ are orthonormal bases of $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $\mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$
respectively, then $\left\{ e _ { 1 j } \otimes e _ { 2 k } \right\}$ is a basis of $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \otimes \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } .$

EXAMPLE 10.5.1 $A$ very relevant example for our applications occurs when
$\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } = \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{T} \right) :$

$$\left( x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } \right) \left( y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \right) = \int \int x _ { 1 } \left( t \right) x _ { 2 } \left( s \right) y _ { 1 } \left( t \right) y _ { 2 } \left( s \right) d t d s .$$

This implies that $x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 }$ has a square integrable kernel $x _ { 1 } \left( t \right) x _ { 2 } \left( s \right) .$ We there-
fore have that $L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{T} \right) \otimes L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{T} \right)$ is equivalent to $L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{T} \times \mathcal{T} \right)$ (one may even write
an equality if the interpretation is clear). Tensors in this case are merely square
integrable bivariate functions. This will be convenient later on as covariance
functions denote within curve covariance at different time points.
☐

Our description of tensors is not the only one, and there are other equiv-
alent approaches. We mention one alternative which fits in naturally with
the covariance operators we will discuss in subsequent chapters. Once again,
consider two real Hilbert spaces $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ and $\mathcal{H} _ { 2 } .$ For each pair of elements of
$\left( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \right) \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \times \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } ,$ one can define an operator $L : \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } \rightarrow \mathcal{H} _ { 2 }$ in the following
way:

$$L _ { x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } } \left( y _ { 1 } \right) = \langle x _ { 1 } , y _ { 1 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } } x _ { 2 } ,$$

where $y _ { 1 } \in \mathcal{H} _ { 1 }$ . Notice that the above operator induces a bilinear form which
is equivalent to $x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } ,$

$$\langle L _ { x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } } \left( y _ { 1 } \right) , y _ { 2 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } = \langle x _ { 1 } , y _ { 1 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 1 } } \langle x _ { 2 } , y _ { 2 } \rangle _ { \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } } .$$

Thus the operator approach is sometimes interpreted as the definition of a
tensor. An advantage is that it fits in well with covariance operators which
are defined as $\mathrm { E } \left[ \langle X _ { 1 } , x \rangle X _ { 2 } \right]$ (assuming the random elements are centered). The
approaches are completely equivalent mathematically, thus $i t$ is not uncom-
mon to move between the two depending on which is more convenient. The
downside of this is that it can create quite $a$ bit of ambiguity. For example,
when $\mathcal{H} _ { 1 } = \mathcal{H} _ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } \left( \mathcal{T} \right)$ one may interpret $x \otimes y$ as a bilinear functional,
an operator, or a bivariate function. For this reason, we will often use the
following conventions:

· Operator: $\left( x _ { 1 } \otimes y _ { 1 } \right) \left( y _ { 2 } \right) = \langle y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \rangle x _ { 1 } . ,$

· Functional: $\langle x _ { 1 } \otimes y _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \otimes y _ { 2 } \rangle = \langle x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } \rangle \langle y _ { 1 } , y _ { 2 } \rangle . ,$

. General Form: $x _ { 1 } \otimes x _ { 2 } .$

Extending the above definitions to the tensor product of multiple spaces
is straightforward as one would start with the form

$$\left( x _ { 1 } \otimes \cdots \otimes x _ { p } \right) \left( y _ { 1 } , \ldots , y _ { p } \right) = \langle x _ { 1 } , y _ { 1 } \rangle \ldots \langle x _ { p } , y _ { p } \rangle .$$

Note that is allows us to take tensor products of tensor spaces as well. However,
the operator view of a tensor becomes a bit confusing as there are multiple
interpretations.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="230" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->


### 10.6 Chapter 10 problems


#### 10.1 Prove Proposition 10.1.1.

10.2 Show that in any inner product space, the function $y \mapsto \langle x , y \rangle$ is con-
tinuous. $\left( x \right.$ is an arbitrary vector.)

10.3 Verify that the space $\ell ^ { 2 }$ defined in Example 10.1.1 is a Hilbert space.

10.4 Suppose $\mathcal{G}$ is a subspace of Hilbert space $\mathcal{H} .$ Show that $\mathcal{G} ^ { \perp }$ is a closed
subspace of $\mathcal{H} .$

10.5 Show that the functions

$$1 , \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \pi k t \right) , \sqrt { 2 } \cos \left( \pi k t \right) , k = 1 , 2 , \ldots , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$$ ☒ ☒

do not form an orthonormal basis in real $L ^ { 2 } = L ^ { 2 } \left( \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right) .$

10.6 Suppose $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is a complete orthonormal system in a Hilbert
space. Show that if $\left\{ f _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is an orthonormal sequence satisfying

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \| e _ { j } - f _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } < 1 ,$$

then $\left\{ f _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is also complete.

10.7 Suppose $L : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ is a linear operator. Prove the equivalence of the
following conditions:

(i) $L$ is bounded;

(ii) $L$ is continuous;

(iii) $L$ is continuous at a point.

10.8 A vector space is said to be a normed space if to each vector $x$ there is
associated a nonnegative number $\| x \|$ such that the following conditions hold:

(i) $\| x + y \| \leq \| x \| + \| y \|$

(ii) $\| a x \| = | a | \| x \| \left( a \text { is a scalar } \right)$

(iii) $\| x \| = 0$ implies $x =$ 0.

A Banach space is a normed space which is complete in the distance defined
by the norm.

Denote by $\mathcal{L}$ the vector space of bounded operators on a Hilbert space $\mathcal{H} .$
Show that $\mathcal{L}$ is a Banach space.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="231" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Elements of Hilbert space theory" -->

10.9 Recall the definition of a Banach space stated in Problem 10.8. Let $l ^ { \infty }$
be the space of bounded real sequences $x = \left\{ x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } , \ldots \right\}$ such that for any
$x \in l ^ { \infty } ,$

$$\| x \| _ { \infty } : = \sup _ { k \in \mathbb{N} } | x _ { k } | < \infty .$$

Prove that $\| \cdot \| _ { \infty }$ is a norm and $l ^ { \infty }$ is a Banach space. (The vector space
operations are defined pointwise as in Example 10.1.1.)

10.10 Suppose $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ and $\left\{ f _ { i } , i \geq 1 \right\}$ are orthonormal bases in $\mathcal{H} .$ Show
that for any Hilbert-Schmidt operators Y,

$$\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle \Psi \left( f _ { i } \right) , \Phi \left( f _ { i } \right) \rangle = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle \Psi \left( e _ { j } \right) , \Phi \left( e _ { j } \right) \rangle .$$

10.11 Suppose $\left\{ e _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis in $\mathcal{H} .$ Show that the op-
erators $e _ { j } \otimes e _ { i }$ defined by $\left( e _ { j } \otimes e _ { i } \right) \left( x \right) = \langle e _ { i } , x \rangle e _ { j }$ form an orthonormal basis
in $\mathcal{S} .$

10.12 Show that if $L$ is bounded then $L ^ { * }$ is also bounded, and

$$\| L ^ { * } \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } , \quad \| L ^ { * } L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } = \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } ^ { 2 } .$$

10.13 Let $\left\{ \lambda _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ be the set of nonzero eigenvalues of a Hilbert-Schmidt
operator $\Psi .$ Show that $\| \Psi \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } ^ { 2 } .$

10.14 Prove Theorem 10.5.1.

10.15 Show that a Linear operator is bounded if it is bounded on an or-
thonormal basis $\left\{ e _ { j } \right\} .$

10.16 Show that if a sequence $x _ { n } \rightarrow x$ in $\mathcal{H} ,$ then $x _ { n } \otimes x _ { n } \rightarrow x \otimes x$ in $\mathcal{S} .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

## 11 Random functions

In this chapter, we continue the exposition of the mathematical foundations
of FDA we started in Chapter 10. We revisit many concepts introduced in
Chapter 3. We rigorously define and explore in some depth: random func-
tions in abstract metric space, expectation and covariance in a Hilbert space,
Gaussian random functions and principal component analysis. This chapter
provides background required to state certain mathematical results of previ-
ous chapters. It is also a concise introduction to the most important concepts
of theoretical FDA. Familiarity with its contents will enable the readers to
understand most research papers in the field of FDA. Results that have sim-
ple proofs are proven. By studying these proofs, the reader will be able to
understand all concepts much better than by just reading the definitions and
theorems.

This chapter requires some background in measure theory. However, no dif-
ficult results of measure theory are used. Basically, only familiarity with the
concepts of the o-algebra and a measurable map is required. Many textbooks
provide such background. For example, Kallenberg (1997) or Rudin (1987)
(the first few pages of Chapter 1 of both) provide more than enough back-
ground. These concepts are recalled in a very concise manner at the beginning
of Section 11.1.


### 11.1 Random elements in metric spaces

Recall that a set $\mathcal{S}$ is a metric space if there is a function $d$ defined on $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S}$ such
that $d \left( x , y \right) = d \left( y , x \right) > 0$ if $x \neq y ,$ $d \left( x , x \right) = 0$ and $d \left( x , y \right) \leq d \left( x , z \right) + d \left( z , y \right) .$
$A$ collection $\mathfrak{S}$ of subsets of $\mathcal{S}$ is called a o-algebra if $\emptyset \in \mathfrak{S} ,$ $A \in \mathfrak{S}$ implies
$A ^ { c } \in \mathfrak{S}$ and $A _ { j } \in \mathfrak{S} , j \geq 1 ,$ implies $\bigcup _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } A _ { j } \in \mathfrak{S} .$ The smallest o-algebra
containing open sets is called the Borel o-algebra, and its members the Borel
sets. In the following, we denote the Borel o-algebra by G. A function $\mu$
defined on $\mathfrak{S}$ is called a probability measure if it is nonnegative, $\mu \left( \bigcup _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } A _ { j } \right) =$
$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \mu \left( A _ { j } \right)$ if no two $A _ { j }$ have a nonempty intersection (the $A _ { j }$ are pairwise
disjoint), and $\mu \left( \mathcal{S} \right) = 1 .$ In the following, we assume that $\mathcal{S}$ is a separable
metric space, i.e. it contains a dense countable subset. The assumption of
topological separability in needed to prove the results we cite in this section,

<!-- PageNumber="233" -->
<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="234" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

see Chapter 1 of Billingsley (1968) for more details. All metric spaces we use
in this book are separable.

Suppose $\Omega$ is a probability space with a o-algebra $\mathfrak{O}$ and a probability
measure $P .$ Let $\mathcal{S}$ be a metric space. We say that a mapping $X : \Omega \rightarrow \mathcal{S}$ is
a random element in $\mathcal{S}$ if $X ^ { - 1 } \left( A \right) \in \mathfrak{O}$ for every Borel set $A .$ If $\mathcal{S}$ is the real
line, we call $X$ a random variable, if $\mathcal{S} = \mathbb{R} ^ { k } ,$ the k-dimensional Euclidean
space, $X$ is a random vector. Our interest will focus on settings in which $\mathcal{S}$
is a space of functions, in which case $X$ is called a random function. Many
convergence properties of random functions do not depend on the structure of
the function space, and can be stated in the general context of a metric space.
Every random element $X$ induces a probability measure on $\mathcal{S}$ defined by

$$\mu \left( A \right) = P \left( X ^ { - 1 } \left( A \right) \right) = P \left( \omega \in \Omega : X \left( \omega \right) \in A \right) = P \left( X \in A \right) .$$

The measure $\mu$ is called the distribution of $X .$

DEFINITION 11.1.1 [weak convergence] Suppose $\mu _ { n } ,$ $n \geq 1$ 1, and $\mu$ are proba-
bility measures on a metric space $\mathcal{S} .$ We say that the sequence $\left\{ \mu _ { n } \right\}$ converges
weakly to $\mu$ if

$$\int _ { \mathcal{S} } f d \mu _ { n } \rightarrow \int _ { \mathcal{S} } f d \mu$$

for every bounded continuous real function on $\mathcal{S} .$

The integrals in Definition 11.1.1 are abstract integrals defined e.g. in
Chapter 1 of Rudin (1987). Theorem 11.1.1 provides some intuition behind
the concept of weak convergence. It is proven in Billingsley (1968) and in
several other advanced textbooks. A set $A \in \mathfrak{S}$ is called a p-continuity set if
the measure $\mu$ of its topological boundary is zero.

THEOREM 11.1.1 Suppose $\mu _ { n } ,$ $n \geq 1$ 1, and $\mu$ are probability measures on a
metric space $\mathcal{S} .$ The following conditions are equivalent.

a) $\int _ { \mathcal{S} } f d \mu _ { n } \rightarrow \int _ { \mathcal{S} } f d \mu$ for every bounded continuous real function on $\mathcal{S} .$

b) $\mu _ { n } \left( A \right) \rightarrow \mu \left( A \right)$ for every u-continuity set $A .$

Recall that if $X _ { n }$ and $X$ are random variables, then the sequence $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$
converges in distribution to $X$ if $F _ { n } \left( t \right) \rightarrow F \left( t \right)$ at every continuity point $t$ of
$F ,$ where $F _ { n }$ and $F$ are cumulative distribution functions. Using the theory
of o-algebras, this definition can be shown to be equivalent to condition 2 in
Theorem 11.1.1, where $\mu _ { n }$ and $\mu$ are probability measures generated by $F _ { n }$
and $F ,$ respectively. This motivates the following definition.

DEFINITION 11.1.2 [convergence in distribution] Suppose $X _ { n } ,$ $n \geq 1$ 1, and $X$
are random elements in $\mathcal{S} .$ We say that the sequence $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ converges in distri-
bution to $X ,$ denoted $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X ,$ if the distributions $\mu _ { n }$ of $X _ { n }$ converge weakly
to the distribution $\mu$ of $X$ .

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="235" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->

By condition 2 of Theorem 11.1.1, convergence in distribution is equivalent
to $P \left( X _ { n } \in A \right) \rightarrow P \left( X \in A \right)$ for every $A \in \mathfrak{S}$ such that $P \left( X \in \partial A \right) = 0 ,$ where
$\partial A$ is the boundary of $A .$

Another concept that will be often used is the convergence in probability.
To define it, we must assume that all random variables are defined on the same
probability space to be able to consider functions like $d \left( X _ { n } , X \right)$ or $d \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right)$
which are defined on $\Omega$ via $\omega \mapsto d \left( X _ { n } \left( \omega \right) , X \left( \omega \right) \right)$ and $\omega \mapsto d \left( X _ { n } \left( \omega \right) , Y _ { n } \left( \omega \right) \right) .$
From now on we assume that all random variables are defined on the common
probability space $\left( \Omega , \mathfrak{O} , P \right) .$ This assumption is not restrictive in any practical
sense; probability spaces are abstract constructs that are not observed directly,
and if random variables are not defined on the same probability space, one
can construct a larger probability space on which all copies in distribution are
defined. We will not concern ourselves with these issues.

DEFINITION 11.1.3 [convergence in probability] Suppose $X _ { n } ,$ $n \geq 1$ 1, and $X$
are random elements in $\mathcal{S} .$ We say that the sequence $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ converges in prob-
ability to $X ,$ denoted $X _ { n } \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } X ,$ if for every $\epsilon > 0$ 0,

$$P \left( \omega \in \Omega : d \left( X _ { n } \left( \omega \right) , X \left( \omega \right) \right) > \epsilon \right) = P \left( d \left( X _ { n } , X \right) > \epsilon \right) \rightarrow 0 .$$

Analogously, for two sequences, $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ and $\left\{ Y _ { n } \right\} ,$ we say that $d \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow }$
0, if the random variable $d \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right)$ converges to zero, i.e. $P \left( d \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) > \epsilon \right) \rightarrow$
0.

We will now state several theorems which are extremely useful in FDA,
and, in fact, in most arguments of asymptotic statistics. These results are
proven in Billingsley (1968).

THEOREM 11.1.2 If $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X$ and $d \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } 0 ,$ then $Y _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X .$

Taking $X _ { n } = X ,$ we see that convergence in probability implies conver-
gence in distribution.

In many arguments, establishing the convergence in distribution of $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ to
$X$ may be $\mathrm { d i f f i c u l t } ,$ but there may be a truncation type parameter u such that
for every fixed $u X _ { n } \left( u \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X \left( u \right) ,$ and $X \left( u \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X ,$ as $u$ approaches some limit.
The question is then: when can we conclude that $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X$ The following
result is often used.

THEOREM 11.1.3 Suppose that for $e a c h$ u, $X _ { n } \left( u \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X \left( u \right)$ (n > 0), and
$X \left( u \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X \left( u \rightarrow \infty \right) .$ If

$$\lim _ { u \rightarrow \infty } \lim \sup _ { n \rightarrow \infty } P \left( d \left( X _ { n } \left( u \right) , X _ { n } \right) > \epsilon \right) = 0 ,$$

then $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="236" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

The next result is known as the continuous mapping theorem. Suppose
$h : \mathcal{S} \rightarrow \mathcal{S} ^ { \prime } ,$ where $\mathcal{S} ^ { \prime }$ is another separable metric space. Denote by $D _ { h }$ the set
of points in $\mathcal{S}$ at which $h$ is not continuous.

THEOREM 11.1.4 [continuous mapping theorem] Suppose $X _ { n } ,$ $n \geq 1 ,$ and $X$
are random elements in $\mathcal{S}$ and $P \left( X \in D _ { h } \right) = 0 .$ If $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X ,$ then $h \left( X _ { n } \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow }$
$h \left( X \right) .$

In many settings, we work with two sequences, $\left\{ X _ { n } \right\}$ and $\left\{ Y _ { n } \right\} ,$ and are in-
terested in the convergence in distribution of the sequence $\left\{ \left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) , n \geq 1 \right\} ,$
which is a random element in the Cartesian product $\mathcal{S} \times \mathcal{S} .$ Since the pro-
jections are continuous functions, it follows from Theorem 11.1.4 that the
convergence $\left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \left( X , Y \right)$ implies $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X$ and $Y _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } Y .$ The converse
is not true. The following result is used very often.

THEOREM 11.1.5 [Slutsky's theorem] Suppose $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X$ and $Y _ { n } \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } a ,$ where
a is a point in S. Then $\left( X _ { n } , Y _ { n } \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } \left( X , a \right) .$


### 11.2 Expectation and covariance in a Hilbert space

In order to extend the concepts of the expected value and variance, and es-
tablish extensions of results like the law of large numbers and the central limit
theorem, we must assume that the random elements take values in a vector
space. A number of profound results can be established in Banach spaces as-
suming they have some additional properties. This was a very active area of
research in the 1970's, an interested reader is referred to Linde (1986) and
Vakhaniia et al. (1987), a concise introduction is given in Chapter 7 of Laha
and Roghatgi (1979). Chapters 1 and 2 of Bosq (2000) also provide a relevant
summary. We introduce in this section the concepts and results used in the
remainder of the book in the setting of a separable Hilbert space. We assume
that all random functions are defined on a common probability space $\Omega$ and
take values in a separable Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}$ with the distance defined by the
norm.

DEFINITION 11.2.1 $A$ random function $X$ is said to be integrable if $E \| X \| <$
$\infty .$ It is said to be weakly integrable if, for any $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ $E | \langle X , y \rangle | < \infty$ and if
there is $e \in \mathcal{H}$ such that $E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle \right] = \langle e , y \rangle .$

Integrable functions are also called strongly integrable, and weakly inte-
grable vectors are called integrable. It is not difficult to show, Problem 11.6,
that integrability implies weak integrability. The function $e$ in Definition 11.2.1
is unique because if $\langle e _ { 1 } , y \rangle = \langle e _ { 2 } , y \rangle$ for any $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ then $e _ { 1 } = e _ { 2 } .$ We denote

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="237" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->

this unique function by $E X ,$ and call it the expected value of $X .$ By definition,
we thus have

$$E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle \right] = \langle E X , y \rangle , \quad \forall y \in \mathcal{H} .$$
(11.1)

EXAMPLE 11.2.1 Suppose $\mathcal{H} = L ^ { 2 }$ is the Hilbert space of square integrable
functions introduced in Definition 10.1.4. The random function $X$ is integrable
if

$$E \left\{ \int X ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } < \infty .$$

Suppose $X$ is integrable and denote by $e$ its expected value $E X ;$ $e$ is a function
in $L ^ { 2 } .$ We will verify that $e \left( t \right) = E \left[ X \left( t \right) \right]$ for almost all $t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$

By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality in $L ^ { 2 } ,$

$$E \int | X \left( t \right) y \left( t \right) | d t \leq \left\{ \int y ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } E \left\{ \int X ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 1 / 2 } < \infty .$$

The absolute integrability of $X \left( \omega , t \right) y \left( \omega , t \right)$ on $\Omega \times \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ allows us to change
the order of $E$ and $\int$ (Fubini's theorem). Therefore, for any $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$

$$E \int X \left( t \right) y \left( t \right) d t = \int E \left[ X \left( t \right) \right] y \left( t \right) d t = \int e \left( t \right) y \left( t \right) d t .$$

The functions $t \mapsto E \left[ X \left( t \right) \right]$ and $e \left( t \right)$ are thus equal as elements of $L ^ { 2 } ,$ and so
for almost all $t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$
☐

The following theorem provides two useful properties of the expected value.

THEOREM 11.2.1 Suppose $X$ is integrable.

a) $\| E X \| \leq E \| X \|$ (contractive property);

b) For any bounded operator $L ,$ $L \left( X \right)$ is integrable and $E \left[ L \left( X \right) \right] = L \left( E X \right) .$

PROOF: Set $e = E X .$ Then

|
$$| \langle e , y \rangle | = | E \langle X , y \rangle | \leq E | \langle X , y \rangle | \leq \| y \| E \| X \| .$$

Taking $y =$ e, we obtain $\| e \| ^ { 2 } \leq \| e \| E \| X \| ,$ so the first claim follows.

To prove the second claim, first note that $L \left( X \right)$ is integrable because

$$E \| L \left( X \right) \| \leq E \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \| X \| = \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } E \| X \| < \infty .$$

Next, denote $e ^ { \prime } = L \left( e \right) .$ The equality $e ^ { \prime } = L \left( e \right)$ will follow if we can show
that for every $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ $\langle e ^ { \prime } , y \rangle = \langle L \left( e \right) , y \rangle .$ Recall the definition of the adjoint
operator $L ^ { * } ,$ cf. Definition 10.3.1. Then

$$\langle L \left( e \right) , y \rangle = \langle e , L ^ { * } \left( y \right) \rangle = E \langle X , L ^ { * } \left( y \right) \rangle = E \langle L \left( X \right) , y \rangle = \langle e ^ { \prime } , y \rangle .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="238" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

The conclusions of Theorem 11.2.1 remain valid if $L$ maps $\mathcal{H}$ into another
separable Hilbert space $\mathcal{H} ^ { \prime }$ the proof requires more general definitions of the
operator norm and of the adjoint operator. In particular, if $\mathcal{H} ^ { \prime } = \mathbb{R} ,$ the Riesz
representation theorem, Theorem 10.2.3, shows that claim 2 reduces to (11.1).

The second order properties of Hilbert space valued random functions are
described by the covariance operator.

DEFINITION 11.2.2 A random function $X$ is said to be square integrable if
$E \| X \| ^ { 2 } < \infty .$ The covariance operator of a square integrable random function
is defined by

$$\mathcal{H} \ni y \mapsto C \left( y \right) = E \left[ \langle X - E X , y \rangle \left( X - E X \right) \right] \in \mathcal{H} .$$

In the remainder of this section, to lighten the formulas, we will of-
ten assume that $E X = 0$ so that $C \left( y \right) = E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle X \right] .$ If $\mathcal{H} = \mathbb{R} ,$ then
$C \left( y \right) = E \left[ X y X \right] = \mathrm { V a r } \left[ X \right] y ,$ so the action of $C$ reduces to the multiplica-
tion by $\mathrm { V a r } \left[ X \right] ,$ and so $C$ can be identified with the variance. If $\mathcal{H} = \mathbb{R} ^ { d } ,$
$C$ can be similarly identified with the covariance matrix, Problem 11.7. If
$\mathcal{H} = L ^ { 2 } ,$ as in Example 11.2.1, then $C \left( y \right)$ is a function in $L ^ { 2 }$ whose value at
$t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is

$$C \left( y \right) \left( t \right) = E \left[ \left( \int X \left( s \right) y \left( s \right) d s \right) X \left( t \right) \right] = \int c \left( t , s \right) y \left( s \right) d s ,$$
(11.2)

where

$$c \left( t , s \right) = E \left[ X \left( t \right) X \left( s \right) \right] , \quad s , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] .$$
(11.3)

We see that the covariance operator $C$ is an integral operator with kernel
(11.3), called the covariance function. Equation (11.2) shows that the covari-
ance operator can be identified with the corresponding covariance function
$\left( 1 1 . 3 \right) ,$ which is actually more often used in $\mathrm { F D A } .$ The covariance operator
leads to a more elegant description of the second order structure in general
Banach spaces. In theoretical arguments of FDA, it is more convenient to
treat the covariance operator, and its sample counterparts, as elements of the
Hilbert space $\mathcal{S}$ of Hilbert-Schmidt operators. Before we elaborate on this
point, we state a theorem which characterizes covariance operators. We refer
to Section 10.4 for the required definitions.

THEOREM 11.2.2 Suppose $C : \mathcal{H} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ is a linear operator. The operator $C$ is
the covariance operator $o f$ some square integrable random function $X$ if and
only if

a) $C$ is symmetric;

b) $C$ is nonnegative-definite;

c) the eigenvalues of $C$ satisfy $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } < \infty .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="239" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->

PROOF: We will prove only that every covariance operator has properties
1-3. The converse statement follows by showing that if conditions 1-3 hold,
then $\varphi \left( y \right) = \exp \left\{ - \langle C \left( y \right) , y \rangle / 2 \right\}$ is the characteristic functional of a square
integrable random function in $\mathcal{H}$ with covariance operator $C ,$ $\mathrm { c f } .$ Section 11.3.
This is established by using Bochner's theorem in a Hilbert space, the Minlos-
Sazonov theorem, e.g. Section 7.6.2 of Laha and Roghatgi (1979). The details
are too extensive to be presented here.

Verification of condition 1: By (11.1),

(11.4)
$$\langle C \left( y \right) , z \rangle = \langle E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle X \right] , z \rangle = E \langle \left[ \langle X , y \rangle X \right] , z \rangle = E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle \langle X , z \rangle \right] .$$

This shows that $\langle C \left( y \right) , z \rangle = \langle y , C \left( z \right) \rangle .$

Verification of condition 2: By (11.4), $\langle C \left( y \right) , y \rangle = E \left[ \langle X , y \rangle ^ { 2 } \right] \geq 0 .$

Verification of condition 3: Suppose $v _ { j } ,$ $j \geq 1 ,$ are the eigenfunctions of $C .$ By
(11.4),

$$E \left[ \langle X , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } \right] = \langle C \left( v _ { j } \right) , v _ { j } \rangle = \langle \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle = \lambda _ { j } .$$

By Parseval's identity (10.4),

$$\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } E \left[ \langle X , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } \right] = E \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle X , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } = E \| X \| ^ { 2 } < \infty .$$

Operators for which $\sum _ { j } | \lambda _ { j } | < \infty$ are called nuclear. Theorem 11.2.2 thus
states that an operator is a covariance operator if and only if it is symmet-
ric, nonnegative definite and nuclear. One can show that every covariance
operator is Hilbert-Schmidt.


### 11.3 Gaussian functions and limit theorems

In this section, we continue to assume that all random functions take values
in a separable Hilbert space. Just as in the scalar case, the expected value and
the covariance operator do not determine the distribution. The appropriate
extension of the characteristic function is the characteristic functional.

DEFINITION 11.3.1 The characteristic functional of a random function $X$ in
a separable Hilbert space is defined by

$$\mathcal{H} \ni y \mapsto \varphi _ { X } \left( y \right) = E \exp \left\{ i \langle y , X \rangle \right\} .$$

One can show that if $\varphi _ { X } = \varphi _ { Y } ,$ then $X$ and $Y$ have the same distribution,
and that if $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X ,$ then $\varphi _ { X _ { n } } \left( y \right) \rightarrow \varphi _ { X } \left( y \right)$ for every $y \in \mathcal{H} .$ In finite dimen-
sional spaces, the pointwise convergence of characteristic functions implies

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="240" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

convergence in distribution. In infinite dimensional spaces this is no longer
true, Problem 11.13, an additional assumption of tightness is needed. An in-
terested reader is referred to Billingsley (1968) or Chapter 7 of Laha and
Roghatgi (1979). Some other properties of the characteristic functional are
stated in Problem 11.12.

DEFINITION 11.3.2 A random function $X$ is said to be Gaussian if its char-
acteristic functional has the form

$$\varphi _ { X } \left( y \right) = \exp \left\{ i \langle \mu , y \rangle - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \langle C \left( y \right) , y \rangle \right\} ,$$

where $\mu \in \mathcal{H}$ and $C$ is a covariance operator.

It can be shown, e.g. Section 7.6 of Laha and Roghatgi (1979), that if $X$
is Gaussian, then $E \| X \| ^ { 2 } < \infty ,$ $E X = \mu$ and $C$ is the covariance operator
of $X .$ Definition 11.3.2 shows that for every $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ $\langle y , X \rangle$ is a normal ran-
dom variable with expected value $\langle y , \mu \rangle$ and variance $\langle C \left( y \right) , y \rangle .$ The converse
statement is true. The following theorem provides a way of verifying that a
random function in $\mathcal{H}$ is Gaussian.

THEOREM 11.3.1 A random function $X$ is Gaussian (with $E X =$ 0) if and
only if for any $y \in \mathcal{H} ,$ $\langle y , X \rangle$ is a normal random variable (with mean zero).

A proof is given in Chapter 7 of Laha and Roghatgi (1979), Proposition 7.6.5.

The central limit theorem in a Hilbert space is stated as Theorem 11.3.2.
It is proven e.g. in Bosq (2000), Theorem 2.7.

THEOREM 11.3.2 Suppose $X _ { i } ,$ $i = 1 , 2 , \ldots .$ are iid square integrable with ex-
pected value $\mu$ and covariance operator $C .$ Then

$$N ^ { - 1 / 2 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } \left( X _ { i } - \mu \right) \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } Z ,$$

where $Z$ is normal with $E X = 0$ and covariance operator $C .$

The law of large numbers also takes a familiar form.

THEOREM 11.3.3 Suppose $X _ { i } ,$ $i = 1 , 2 , \ldots .$ are id integrable with $e x p e c t e d$
value $\mu .$ Then

$$N ^ { - 1 } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i } \stackrel { a . s . } { \rightarrow } \mu .$$

A proof is given in Section 7.2 of Laha and Roghatgi (1979), Theorem 7.2.1.

The iid assumption in Theorems 11.3.2 and 11.3.3 can be relaxed in many
ways. In most applications, it is useful to retain the assumption of the identical
distribution, but allow some weak dependence between the $X _ { i } .$ This point will
be revisited in the following chapters.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="241" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->


### 11.4 Functional principal components

As we have seen in Chapter 1, a fundamental approach of FDA is to expand
observed functions $X _ { n } \left( t \right) , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] ,$ as $X _ { n } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { m = 1 } ^ { M } c _ { n m } B _ { m } \left( t \right) ,$ where the
$B _ { m }$ are some basis functions. The basis functions can be deterministic, e.g.
Fourier or spline functions, but one can ask if there are some basis functions
which are in some sense optimal for an observed data set $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$ We
will address this problem in Chapter 12. In this section, we study it in the
context of a random function $X$ taking values in $L ^ { 2 } .$ We thus define population
parameters which can be estimated from a sample $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } , \ldots , X _ { N } .$

Fix $p \geq 1$ and suppose $u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { p }$ is any orthonormal system in $L ^ { 2 } .$
The projection of a random function $X$ on the subspace spanned by it is
$\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle u _ { k } .$ The system $u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { p }$ is optimal in the sense of mini-
mizing the expected distance between $X$ and its span if it minimizes

$$S \left( u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { p } \right) = E \| X - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle u _ { k } \| ^ { 2 }$$
(11.5)

over all orthonormal systems consisting of $p$ functions. To find such a system
observe that, by the orthonormality of the $u _ { k } ,$

$$S \left( u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { p } \right) = E \langle X - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle u _ { k } , X - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle u _ { k } \rangle$$
$$= E \| X \| ^ { 2 } - 2 E \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } + E \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 }$$
$$= E \| X \| ^ { 2 } - \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } E \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } .$$

Since, by (11.1),

$$E \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } = E \langle \langle X , u _ { k } \rangle X , u _ { k } \rangle = \langle C \left( u _ { k } \right) , u _ { k } \rangle ,$$

minimizing $S \left( u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , \ldots , u _ { p } \right)$ is equivalent to maximizing $\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { p } \langle C \left( u _ { k } \right) , u _ { k } \rangle$ .
We now assume that condition (10.11) holds. By Theorem 10.4.5, the largest
$p \mathrm { o s s i b l e }$ value of $\langle C \left( u _ { 1 } \right) , u _ { 1 } \rangle$ is $\lambda _ { 1 }$ and it is attained if $u _ { 1 } = v _ { 1 }$ (up to a
sign). Then, since $u _ { 2 }$ is orthogonal to $u _ { 1 } = v _ { 1 } ,$ the largest possible value of
$\langle C \left( u _ { 2 } \right) , u _ { 2 } \rangle$ is $\lambda _ { 2 }$ and it is attained at $u _ { 2 } = v _ { 2 } .$ Continuing in this manner,
establishes the following result.

THEOREM 11.4.1 Suppose $X$ is square integrable and the eigenvalues of its
covariance operator $C$ satisfy (10.11). For any fixed $p \geq 1 ,$ the expected error
(11.5) is minimized if $u _ { j } = v _ { j } ,$ where the $v _ { j }$ are the eigenfunctions of $C$ with
unit norm.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="242" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

Setting $\mu = E X ,$ observe that

$$X - \mu = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { j } v _ { j } , \quad \xi _ { j } = \langle X - \mu , v _ { j } \rangle .$$
(11.6)

Decomposition (11.6) is called the Karhunen-Loéve expansion. The determin-
istic functions $v _ { j }$ are called the functional principal components. Throughout
the book, we will use the abbreviation FPC's. The FPC's are thus the eigen-
functions of the covariance operator normalized to have unit norm. They are
defined only up to a sign. The random variables $\xi _ { j }$ are called their scores.
Observe that

$$E \xi _ { j } = 0 , \quad E \xi _ { j } ^ { 2 } = \lambda _ { j } \quad E \left[ \xi _ { j } \xi _ { i } \right] = 0 , \mathrm { i f } i \neq j .$$
(11.7)

Therefore, by Parseval's identity,

$$E \| X - \mu \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } .$$
(11.8)

Identity (11.8) has the following interpretation: The variance of $X$ is equal
to the sum of the variances of the projections of $X$ onto the FPC's. This
decomposition, or analysis, of variance is used very often in FDA.

In the space $L ^ { 2 } ,$ decompositions (11.6) and (11.8) take the form

$$X \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \xi _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) , \quad E \int \left( X \left( t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) ^ { 2 } d t = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } .$$

We see that every square integrable random function is the sum of the mean
function, $\mu \left( \cdot \right) ,$ and a random error function whose expected value is zero. The
error function is the sum of the FPC's $v _ { j } \left( \cdot \right)$ with mean zero random weights $\xi _ { j } .$
The importance of each $\mathrm { F P C } v$ is quantified by the standard deviation of its
weight $\xi _ { j } ,$ which is equal to $\sqrt { \lambda _ { j } } .$ The expected integrated squared difference
☒
between a random function and its mean function is equal to the sum of the
eigenvalues of the covariance operator.

EXAMPLE 11.4.1 [Gaussian random function] If $X$ is a Gaussian function in
$L ^ { 2 } ,$ then by Theorem 11.3.1, $\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } a _ { j } \xi _ { j } = \langle X - \mu , \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { d } a _ { j } v _ { j } \rangle$ is a normal
random variable for any $d$ and any numbers $a _ { 1 } , a _ { 2 } , \ldots , a _ { d } .$ This means that
the $\xi _ { j }$ are jointly normal. By (11.7) they are uncorrelated, so they are, in fact,
independent with variances $\lambda _ { j } .$ We conclude that a Gaussian random function
in $L ^ { 2 }$ admits the decomposition

$$X \left( t \right) = \mu \left( t \right) + \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \sqrt { \lambda _ { j } } N _ { j } v _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="243" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->

where the $N _ { j }$ are independent standard normal and

$$\lambda _ { j } = E \left\{ \int \left( X \left( t \right) - \mu \left( t \right) \right) v _ { j } \left( t \right) d t \right\} ^ { 2 } .$$

☐

Example 11.4.1 shows that the distribution a Gaussian random function
on $\left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ is fully characterized by the eigenfunctions $v _ { j } \left( \cdot \right)$ and and the eigenval-
ues $\lambda _ { j } .$ The two Gaussian random functions occurring most often in various
settings are the Wiener process, also called the Brownian motion, and the
Brownian bridge. We will use them extensively, so we now define them.

DEFINITION 11.4.1 [Wiener process and Brownian bridge] A random func-
tion $\left\{ W \left( t \right) , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right\}$ is called the Wiener process if the following conditions
hold

a) $W \left( 0 \right) = 0 \mathrm { a . s . } .$

b) If $0 \leq s < t \leq 1 ,$ then $W \left( t \right) - \quad W \left( s \right)$ is normal with mean zero and variance
$t \quad - \quad s .$

c) For any $0 \leq t _ { 0 } < t _ { 1 } < \ldots < t _ { k } \leq 1 ,$ the random variables $W \left( t _ { j } \right) -$
$W \left( t _ { j - 1 } \right) , 1 \leq j \leq k ,$ are independent.

If $\left\{ W \left( t \right) , t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right\}$ is the Wiener process, then

$$B \left( t \right) = W \left( t \right) - t W \left( 1 \right) , \quad t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$$

is called the Brownian bridge.

Based on Definition 11.4.1, the usual way of approximating a Wiener pro-
cess is based on the observation that for $0 \leq k \leq K$

$$W \left( \frac { k } { K } \right) - W \left( \frac { k - 1 } { K } \right) \stackrel { d } { = } N \left( 0 , \frac { 1 } { K } \right) \stackrel { d } { = } \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { K } } N _ { k } ,$$

so, with independent standard normal $N _ { k } ,$

$$W \left( \frac { k } { K } \right) \stackrel { d } { = } \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { K } } \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { k } N _ { k } .$$

The Karhunen-Loéve expansion allows us to view the Wiener process and
the Brownian bridge from a different angle. We begin with Proposition 11.4.1
whose proof is assigned as Problem 11.16.

PROPOSITION 11.4.1 The covariance function of the Wiener process is
$c _ { W } \left( t , s \right) = \min \left( t , s \right) .$ The covariance function of the Brownian bridge is
$c _ { B } \left( t , s \right) = \min \left( t , s \right) - t s .$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="244" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

THEOREM 11.4.2 The functional principal components and the corresponding
eigenvalues of the Wiener process are

$$v _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi t \right) , \quad \lambda _ { j } = \frac { 1 } { \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) ^ { 2 } \pi ^ { 2 } } , \quad j = 1 , 2 , \ldots .$$

For the Brownian bridge, these are

$$v _ { j } \left( t \right) = \sqrt { 2 } \sin \left( \pi j t \right) , \quad \lambda _ { j } = \frac { 1 } { j ^ { 2 } \pi ^ { 2 } } , \quad j = 1 , 2 , \ldots . ,$$

PROOF: We will verify the claim for the Wiener process. The verification for
the Brownian bridge is similar.

By Proposition 11.4.1, we must solve the integral equation $\int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } \min \left( s , t \right) v \left( s \right) d s =$
$\lambda v \left( t \right) ,$ which can be be written as

$$\int _ { 0 } ^ { t } s v \left( s \right) d s + t \int _ { t } ^ { 1 } v \left( s \right) d s = \lambda v \left( t \right) .$$

Differentiating with respect to $t$ yields

$$\int _ { t } ^ { 1 } v \left( s \right) d s = \lambda v ^ { \prime } \left( t \right) .$$
(11.9)

Differentiating again, we obtain the differential equation

$$- v \left( t \right) = \lambda v ^ { \prime \prime } \left( t \right) .$$

A general solution to this equation has the form

$$v \left( t \right) = A \sin \left( \frac { t } { \sqrt { \lambda } } \right) + B \cos \left( \frac { t } { \sqrt { \lambda } } \right) .$$

The constants $A$ and $B$ are determined by boundary conditions. Since $\int _ { 0 } ^ { 0 } = 0 ,$
$v \left( 0 \right) = 0 ,$ so $B = 0 .$ Setting $t = 1$ in $\left( 1 1 . 9 \right) ,$ gives $v ^ { \prime } \left( 1 \right) = 0 ,$ which implies

$$\cos \left( \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { \lambda } } \right) = 0 .$$

This means that $1 / \sqrt { \lambda } = - \pi / 2 + j \pi , j \geq 1 ,$ so all possible eigenval-
ues are as specified in the claim, and the corresponding eigenfunctions are
$A \sin \left( \pi \left( j - 1 / 2 \right) t \right) .$ The condition $\int v _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( t \right) d t = 1 ,$ yields $A = \sqrt { 2 } .$

Theorem 11.4.2 combined with Example 11.4.1 lead to the following Corol-
lary.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="245" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Random functions" -->

COROLLARY 11.4.1 Let $N _ { j } ,$ $j \geq 1 ,$ be independent standard normal random
variables.

The Karhunen-Loéve expansion of the Wiener process is

$$W \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { \sqrt { 2 } } { \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi } N _ { j } \sin \left( \left( j - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \pi t \right) .$$

For the Brownian bridge, this expansion $i s$

$$B \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \frac { \sqrt { 2 } } { j \pi } N _ { j } \sin \left( j \pi t \right) .$$

We conclude this section by explaining how functional data with specified
FPC's can be simulated. Set

$$X _ { n } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { j } Z _ { j n } e _ { j } \left( t \right) ,$$
(11.10)

where $a _ { j }$ are real numbers, for every n, the $Z _ { j n }$ are iid mean zero random
variables with unit variance, and the $e _ { j }$ are orthogonal functions with unit
norm. Denote by $X$ a random function with the same distribution as each
$X _ { n } ,$ i.e. $X \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { j } Z _ { j } e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$ Then the covariance operator of $X$ acting on
☒
$x$ is equal to

$$C \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = E \left[ \left( \int X \left( s \right) x \left( s \right) d s \right) X \left( t \right) \right] = \int E \left[ X \left( t \right) X \left( s \right) \right] x \left( s \right) d s .$$

By the independence of the $Z _ { j } ,$ the covariance function is equal to

$$E \left[ X \left( t \right) X \left( s \right) \right] = E \left[ \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { j } Z _ { j } e _ { j } \left( t \right) \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { i } Z _ { i } e _ { i } \left( s \right) \right] = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { j } ^ { 2 } e _ { j } \left( t \right) e _ { j } \left( s \right) .$$

Therefore,

$$C \left( x \right) \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } a _ { j } ^ { 2 } \left( \int e _ { j } \left( s \right) x \left( s \right) d s \right) e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

It follows that the FPC's of the $X _ { n }$ are the $e _ { j } ,$ and the eigenvalues are $\lambda _ { j } = a _ { j } ^ { 2 } .$

a) If $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X$ and $Y _ { n } \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } a ,$ then $X _ { n } + Y _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X + a$ and $Y _ { n } X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } a X$

This means that for every $\epsilon > 0 ,$ there is $M > 0$ such that $P \left( | X _ { n } | > M \right) < \epsilon$
for sufficiently large $n$ (those greater than some $n _ { 0 } .$ Show that if $X _ { n } \stackrel { d } { \rightarrow } X ,$

in the Euclidean space $\mathbb{R} ^ { M } .$
