estimates (which are very common in FDA), Scheffe's method works well and
can be computed very quickly. For estimates that are, for whatever reason,
rougher, a bootstrap approach will result in a noticeable improvement.


### 12.7 Proof of Theorem 12.2.1

The proof of Theorem 12.2.1 is based on Lemmas 12.7.1 and 12.7.2. These
lemmas have wider applicability, and will be used in subsequent chapters. They
state that if the operators are close, then their eigenvalues and eigenfunctions
(adjusted for the sign) are also close.

We begin with a very useful Lemma 12.7.1, which can be stated for op-
erators in a general class of compact operators, which are also called com-
$p l e t e l y$ continuous operators. There are several equivalent definition of these
operators and an interested reader is referred to Chapter 4 of Debnath and
Mikusinski (2005). The definition that emphasizes the complete continuity is
as follows: $T$ is completely continuous if $\langle x _ { n } , y \rangle \rightarrow \langle x , y \rangle$ for every $y \in \mathcal{H}$ im-
plies $\| T \left( x _ { n } \right) - T \left( x \right) \| \rightarrow 0 .$ Every compact operator is bounded. Every Hilbert-
Schmidt operator is a compact operator. In fact, in Theorem 10.4.4 and Corol-
lary 10.4.1, the assumption that $\Psi \in \mathcal{S}$ can be replaced by the assumption
that $\Psi$ is compact. If the assumption of symmetry is dropped, the represen-
tation in Corollary 10.4.1 must be replaced by a more general representa-
tion $\Psi \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle u _ { j } ,$ called the singular value decomposition, where
$\left\{ u _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\}$ is an orthonormal basis which may be different from the basis
$\left\{ v _ { j } , j \geq 1 \right\} .$ The eigenvalues of a compact operator satisfy $\lim _ { j \rightarrow \infty } \lambda _ { j } = 0 ,$
they do not have to be nonnegative.

Consider thus two compact operators, $C$ and $K ,$ with singular value de-

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="275" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Inference from a random sample" -->

compositions

$$C \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \lambda _ { j } \langle x , v _ { j } \rangle f _ { j } , \quad K \left( x \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \infty } \gamma _ { j } \langle x , u _ { j } \rangle g _ { j } .$$
(12.10)

The following Lemma is proven in Section VI.1 of Gohberg et al. (1990), see
their Corollary 1.6 on p. 99.

LEMMA 12.7.1 Suppose $C ,$ $K \in \mathcal{L}$ are two compact operators with singular
value decompositions (12.10). Then, for each $j \geq 1 ,$ $| \gamma _ { j } - \lambda _ { j } | \leq \| K - C \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$

If we replace $K ,$ by the zero operator, Lemma 12.7.1 formally reduces to
$| \lambda _ { j } | \leq \| C \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$ This special case is very easy to verify, see Problem 12.2. In
order to prove Theorem 12.2.1, we will apply Lemma 12.7.1 with $C$ being the
covariance operator and $K$ the sample covariance operator $\widehat { C } .$ We therefore
now tighten the conditions on the operator $C$ by assuming that it is symmetric
and $C \left( v _ { j } \right) = \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } ,$ i.e. $f _ { j } = v _ { j }$ in (12.10); any covariance operator $C$ satisfies
these conditions. To lighten the notation, we also define

$$v _ { j } ^ { \prime } = c _ { j } v _ { j } , \quad c _ { j } = \mathrm { s i g n } \left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle \right) .$$

LEMMA 12.7.2 Suppose $C ,$ $K \in \mathcal{L}$ are two symmetric compact operators with
singular value decompositions (12.10) $\left( f _ { j } = v _ { j } \right.$ and $g _ { j } = u _ { j } .$ If C's eigenval-
ues satisfy (10.11), then

$$\| u _ { j } - v _ { j } ^ { \prime } \| \leq \frac { 2 \sqrt { 2 } } { \alpha _ { j } } \| K - C \| _ { \mathcal{L} } , \quad 1 \leq j \leq p ,$$

where $\alpha _ { 1 } = \lambda _ { 1 } - \lambda _ { 2 }$ and $\alpha _ { j } = \min \left( \lambda _ { j - 1 } - \lambda _ { j } , \lambda _ { j } - \lambda _ { j + 1 } \right) ,$ $2 \leq j \leq p .$

PROOF: For a fixed $1 \leq j \leq p ,$ introduce the following quantities

$$D _ { j } = \| C \left( u _ { j } \right) - \lambda _ { j } u _ { j } \| , \quad S _ { j } = \sum _ { k \neq j } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } .$$

The claim will follow, once we have established that

$$\| u _ { j } - v _ { j } ^ { \prime } \| ^ { 2 } \leq 2 S _ { j } ,$$
$$\alpha _ { j } ^ { 2 } S _ { j } \leq D _ { j } ^ { 2 } ,$$
(12.11)
(12.12)

and

$$D _ { j } \leq 2 \| K - C \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$$
(12.13)

Verification of (12.11): By Parseval's identity

$$\| u _ { j } - v _ { j } ^ { \prime } \| ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle - c _ { j } \langle v _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle \right) ^ { 2 } = \left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle - c _ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } + S _ { j } .$$

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="276" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

If $c _ { j } = 0 ,$ then (12.11) clearly holds, since in this case $\| u _ { j } - v _ { j } ^ { \prime } \| = \| u _ { j } \| = 1$
and $S _ { j } = \| u _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } - \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle = 1 .$

If $| c _ { j } | = 1 ,$ then $\left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle - c _ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } = \left( 1 - | \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | \right) ^ { 2 } ,$ and using the identity
$\sum _ { k } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } = 1 ,$ we obtain

$$\left( 1 - | \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | \right) ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } - 2 | \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | + \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } .$$

Thus, if $| c _ { j } | = 1 ,$

$$\left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle - c _ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } = S _ { j } + 2 \left( \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle ^ { 2 } - | \langle u _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | \right) \leq S _ { j } .$$

Verification of (12.12): By Parseval's identity

$$D _ { j } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \langle C \left( u _ { j } \right) , v _ { k } \rangle - \lambda _ { j } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

Since $C$ is symmetric and $C \left( v _ { j } \right) = \lambda _ { j } v _ { j } ,$ $\langle C \left( u _ { j } \right) , v _ { k } \rangle = \lambda _ { k } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle$ . Therefore

$$D _ { j } ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { k \neq j } \left( \lambda _ { k } - \lambda _ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } \langle u _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle ^ { 2 } \geq S _ { j } \min _ { k \neq j } \left( \lambda _ { k } - \lambda _ { j } \right) ^ { 2 } .$$

Verification of (12.13): Observe that since $K$ is symmetric

$$C \left( u _ { i } \right) - \lambda _ { j } u _ { j } = \left( C - K \right) \left( u _ { j } \right) + \left( \gamma _ { j } - \lambda _ { j } \right) u _ { j } .$$

Therefore, by Lemma 12.7.1,

$$D _ { j } \leq \| C - K \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \| u _ { j } \| + | \gamma _ { j } - \lambda _ { j } | \| u _ { j } \| \leq 2 \| K - C \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$$
(12.14)

PROOF OF THEOREM 12.2.1: By Theorem 10.3.1, $\| Z _ { N } \| _ { \mathcal{L} } \leq \| Z _ { N } \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ,$ where
$Z _ { N }$ is the operator with the kernel

$$z _ { N } \left( t , s \right) = N ^ { 1 / 2 } \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { N } \left[ \widehat { c } \left( t , s \right) - c \left( t , s \right) \right] .$$

By Theorem 12.1.4, $E \| Z _ { N } \| _ { \mathcal{S} } ^ { 2 } = O \left( N \right) ,$ so the result follows from Lemmas
12.7.1 and 12.7.2.


### 12.8 Chapter 12 problems

12.1 Prove Lemma 12.1.1.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="277" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Inference from a random sample" -->

12.2 Show that for every eigenvalue $\lambda$ of a bounded operator $L ,$ we have
$| \lambda | \leq \| L \| _ { \mathcal{L} } .$

12.3 Recall the simulation scheme given in $P r o b l e m$ 1.5. For the three $\nu$
scenarios, increase $N$ to 1000.

(a) Plot the estimates for the first 10 eigenvalues and include confidence
intervals. Include everything in one plot if you can (but use no more than
three plots).

(b) How many of the CI's did not include 0? Suppose you used that to
determine the number of FPC's; how does that compare to the explained
variance approach?

(c) Using the formula for the CI's and your estimated eigenvalues, approx-
imately how many observations would $\mathrm { y o u }$ need to conclude that the 10th
eigenvalue is not zero?

12.4 Suppose that $X _ { 1 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$ are iid and normally distributed.

(a) What is the largest that $\| \widehat { v } _ { i } - v _ { i } \|$ could possibly be?

(b) Find an estimate for $E \| \widehat { v } _ { i } - v _ { i } \| ^ { 2 }$ in terms of the estimated eigenvalues.

(c) Part (b) gives you a way of quantifying the variability of a an eigen-
function estimate. Construct a plot of these estimates for the first 10 FPC's
for the simulation scheme given in Problem 12.3. What can you conclude?

12.5 Assume that $X _ { 1 } , \ldots , X _ { N }$ are id elements of $L ^ { 2 } \left[ 0 , 1 \right]$ with $E \| X _ { n } \| ^ { 4 } < \infty$
and whose first $p$ eigenvalues are distinct. Prove that

$$| N \langle \widehat { v } _ { j } - v _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | = O _ { P } \left( 1 \right) , \quad \mathrm { f o r } j = 1 , \ldots p .$$

Why is this a seemingly unusual convergence rate? (Hint: start by showing
that $\left. | \langle \widehat { v } _ { j } - v _ { j } , v _ { j } \rangle | = \frac { 1 } { 2 } \| \widehat { v } _ { j } - v _ { j } \| ^ { 2 } \right)$


### 12.6 Prove Theorem 12.1.3.

12.7 Suppose that the data $\left\{ X _ { n } \left( t \right) : t \in \left[ 0 , 1 \right] \right. ,$ $\left. 1 \leq n \leq N \right\}$ are expressed
using an orthonormal basis $e _ { 1 } , \ldots , e _ { J } :$

$$X _ { n } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } x _ { n j } e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

In this case, the EFPC's, $\widehat { \upsilon } _ { i } \left( t \right) ,$ can also be expressed as

$$\widehat { v } _ { i } \left( t \right) = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { J } \widehat { v } _ { i j } e _ { j } \left( t \right) .$$

Explain how to obtain the coefficients $\widehat { v } _ { i j }$ from the $x _ { n j } .$ Justify your answer.

12.8 Prove Corollaries 12.3.1 and 12.3.2.

<!-- PageBreak -->

<!-- PageNumber="278" -->
<!-- PageHeader="Introduction to Functional Data Analysis" -->

12.9 Using the expansion in Example 11.4.1 and (11.7), verify identity (12.5).

12.10 Suppose Assumption 12.0.1 holds. Show that the covariance operator
of the sample mean function $\widehat { \mu } = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { N } X _ { i }$ is $N ^ { - 1 } C$ and then establish (12.8).

12.11 Referring to the proof of Theorem 12.4.2, verify in detail that

$$\frac { N \left[ \langle \widehat { \mu } - \mu ^ { \star } , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle ^ { 2 } + 2 \langle \widehat { \mu } - \mu ^ { \star } , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle \langle \Delta , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle + \langle \Delta , \widehat { v } _ { i } \rangle ^ { 2 } \right] } { \widehat { \lambda } _ { i } } = \frac { N \langle \Delta , v _ { i } \rangle ^ { 2 } } { \lambda _ { i } } + O _ { P } \left( N ^ { 1 / 2 } \right) .$$

12.12 Under the same assumptions as in Problem 12.5 show that, for $j \neq k ,$
and $1 \leq j \leq p ,$

$$\langle \widehat { v } _ { j } - v _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle = \frac { \langle \widehat { C } - C , \widehat { v } _ { j } \otimes v _ { k } \rangle } { \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } - \lambda _ { k } } .$$

What can you conclude about the asymptotic distribution of $N ^ { 1 / 2 } \langle \widehat { v } _ { j } - v _ { j } , v _ { k } \rangle ^ { . }$
(justify your answer).

12.13 Under the same assumptions as in Problem 12.5 consider, for $j \leq p ,$
the mappings $G _ { j N } : \mathcal{S} \rightarrow \mathcal{H}$ defined by

$$G _ { j N } \left( \cdot \right) = \sum _ { k \neq j } \frac { \langle \cdot , \widehat { v } _ { j } \otimes v _ { k } \rangle } { \widehat { \lambda } _ { j } - \lambda _ { k } } v _ { k } .$$

(a) Show that $G _ { j N }$ is linear and continuous.

(b) Find a nontrivial Hilbert space which contains $G _ { j N } .$ What is the inner
product?

(c) Show that there exists mappings $G _ { j }$ such that $G _ { j N } \stackrel { P } { \rightarrow } G _ { j }$ with respect to
the operator norm.

12.14 Consider the confidence band in (12.9). Finding the $r _ { j }$ which lead to
the narrowest band is, in general, a very difficult task. However, we can readily
solve a slight variant of it. Namely, we can find the $r _ { j }$ which minimize

$$\int \sum r _ { j } ^ { 2 } v _ { j } \left( t \right) ^ { 2 } d t$$

subject to the constraint that

$$\sum \frac { \lambda _ { j } } { r _ { j } ^ { 2 } } = 1 .$$

Intuitively, this can be thought of replacing the quantile $c _ { N } \left( \alpha \right)$ with its mean.
Find the $r _ { j } .$
